  • Iatrogenic: Central line placement (subclavian>IJ), thoracentesis, transbronchial biopsy, mechanical ventilation (barotrauma), CPR
  • Traumatic: Blunt/penetrating chest trauma, rib fractures

Presentation: Acute-onset pleuritic chest pain, dyspnea. Exam: Decreased breath sounds, hyperresonance to percussion, decreased tactile fremitus on affected side. Tracheal deviation toward affected side (simple PTX) or AWAY (tension PTX)

TENSION PNEUMOTHORAX - EMERGENCY:

  • Pathophys: One-way valve ‚Üí air enters pleural space but can't escape ‚Üí ‚Üëpressure collapses lung, shifts mediastinum, compresses contralateral lung + great vessels ‚Üí ‚Üìvenous return ‚Üí shock
  • Presentation: Severe respiratory distress, hypotension, tachycardia, JVD, tracheal deviation AWAY from affected side, absent breath sounds, hyperresonance, cyanosis
  • CLINICAL DIAGNOSIS - DON'T WAIT FOR CXR!
  • Treatment: Immediate needle decompression: 14-16 gauge angiocath, 2nd intercostal space, midclavicular line (or 4th-5th ICS anterior axillary line). Insert needle over rib (avoid neurovascular bundle under rib). Rush of air = confirms diagnosis. THEN get CXR and place chest tube

CXR Findings: Visceral pleural line (edge of collapsed lung), absent lung markings peripherally. Upright film more sensitive. Deep sulcus sign on supine (air collects anteriorly). Quantify size: Measure distance from chest wall to lung edge at hilum level. Small <2cm, Large >2cm

Treatment Algorithm (Non-Tension):

  • Small (<2cm or <20%), stable, minimal symptoms:
    • Observation + supplemental O2 (hastens resorption - 4√ó faster with O2)
    • Repeat CXR in 6hr. If stable/improving, discharge with outpatient follow-up CXR 1-2 weeks
    • PSP: Resorbs ~1-2% per day (10-14 days). SSP: Higher risk progression, lower threshold to place tube
    • Instruct: Return if increased dyspnea, chest pain. No flying until resolved (pressure changes expand PTX)
  • Large (>2cm or >20%) OR symptomatic:
    • Aspiration (PSP, first episode, age <50, stable): 16-18 gauge IV catheter, 2nd ICS midclavicular line or 4th-5th ICS midaxillary line. Aspirate with 60 mL syringe + 3-way stopcock. Max 2.5L (if not successful, place chest tube). If successful (PTX <2cm, symptoms improved), observe 4hr, repeat CXR. Discharge if stable. Success ~60-80% PSP
    • Chest tube (SSP, large PSP failing aspiration, recurrent, bilateral, hemodynamic instability): 16-28Fr pigtail (small) or 28-32Fr tube (large). 4th-5th ICS, anterior axillary line, directed posteroapically. Connect to water seal +/- suction (-20 cm H2O). Keep tube until no air leak √ó 24hr + lung re-expanded on CXR
  • Recurrent PTX (20-50% recur within 2 years): Video-assisted thoracoscopic surgery (VATS) with bleb resection + pleurodesis. Reduces recurrence to <5%. Consider after 1st SSP or 2nd PSP

Special Situations:

  • Mechanical ventilation: If PTX develops, place chest tube immediately (positive pressure worsens). Don't wait/observe
  • Bilateral PTX: Higher risk respiratory compromise. Usually place bilateral tubes
  • Persistent air leak (tube in, still bubbling >7 days): Bronchopleural fistula. Consult thoracic surgery
üíé TEACHING PEARLS
  • Tension PTX is clinical diagnosis: Don't wait for CXR! Respiratory distress + hypotension + absent breath sounds + hyperresonance = needle decompression NOW
  • Tracheal deviation: Simple PTX ‚Üí toward affected side (volume loss). Tension PTX ‚Üí away from affected side (mass effect pushing mediastinum)
  • PSP = tall thin young men: "Walking around with time bombs." Apical blebs. Smoking ‚Üërisk 20√ó. Often rupture at rest. Recurrence 20-50%
  • SSP more dangerous than PSP: Underlying lung disease ‚Üí less reserve. Lower threshold to place chest tube. Higher morbidity/mortality
  • O2 speeds resolution 4√ó: Creates nitrogen gradient ‚Üí resorption. Give O2 even if saturating well
  • No flying until resolved: Pressure changes at altitude expand PTX. Wait 1-2 weeks post-resolution before flying
  • Aspiration vs tube: PSP, young, stable ‚Üí try aspiration first (60-80% success, less invasive). SSP ‚Üí chest tube (higher failure rate, don't waste time)
  • Recurrent PTX needs surgery: VATS pleurodesis after 1st SSP or 2nd PSP. Reduces recurrence <5%
‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

40. SYNCOPE

Vasovagal ‚Ä¢ Cardiac ‚Ä¢ Orthostatic ‚Ä¢ Neurologic
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Definition: Transient loss of consciousness (LOC) with loss of postural tone, followed by spontaneous complete recovery. Caused by global cerebral hypoperfusion. Duration typically <20 seconds

Classic: Sudden LOC, fall, rapid spontaneous recovery without intervention. Patient recalls events before and after (vs seizure with postictal confusion)

Key Historical Questions:

  • Prodrome: Nausea, diaphoresis, tunnel vision, lightheadedness = vasovagal. No warning = cardiac
  • Position: Standing ‚Üí orthostatic or vasovagal. Supine ‚Üí cardiac (very concerning!)
  • Activity: Exertional syncope = cardiac until proven otherwise (HOCM, AS, arrhythmia, PE)
  • Triggers: Pain, emotion, standing, prolonged standing, hot environment = vasovagal. Post-micturition/defecation = situational
  • Recovery: Immediate = syncope. Confusion/lethargy = seizure or stroke
  • Witnesses: Tonic-clonic movements? (can occur in syncope but brief <15sec, vs prolonged in seizure). Tongue biting/incontinence more typical of seizure

Alternate Presentations by Etiology:

  • Vasovagal (Neurocardiogenic): Most common (40-50%). Prodrome of nausea, diaphoresis, visual changes. Triggered by emotional stress, pain, prolonged standing, hot environment. Young patients. Benign
  • Orthostatic Hypotension: Syncope upon standing from sitting/supine. Elderly, autonomic dysfunction, medications (Œ±-blockers, diuretics, vasodilators), volume depletion. BP drop ‚â•20/10 mmHg within 3 minutes of standing
  • Cardiac Arrhythmia: No warning, sudden collapse. Palpitations before/after. High-risk: age >60, exertional, supine, no prodrome, heart disease. Bradyarrhythmias (sick sinus, AV block) or tachyarrhythmias (VT, SVT)
  • Structural Cardiac: Aortic stenosis (exertional syncope, systolic murmur), HOCM (young athletes, exertional, family history sudden death), cardiac tamponade, myxoma, PE (dyspnea, pleuritic chest pain)
  • Neurologic (RARE cause): Vertebrobasilar TIA (diplopia, ataxia, vertigo), subclavian steal, seizure (postictal confusion differentiates from syncope)
  • Situational: Specific triggers - micturition (post-void), defecation, cough, swallow. Vasovagal-mediated
  • Carotid Sinus Hypersensitivity: Elderly men, triggered by tight collar, head turning, shaving. Bradycardia/hypotension with carotid massage
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

ALL Patients Must Have:

  • Detailed History: Events before/during/after, position, activity, prodrome, recovery time, witnesses, prior episodes, medications, cardiac history
  • Physical Exam: Complete cardiac exam (murmurs, JVD, edema), neurologic exam (focal deficits?), orthostatic vitals (supine ‚Üí standing at 1 and 3 min)
  • ECG (MANDATORY for ALL): Look for: prolonged QT (>480ms = high risk for Torsades), Brugada pattern (ST elevation V1-V3), pre-excitation (WPW = short PR, delta wave), epsilon wave (ARVC), pathologic Q waves (old MI), heart block, SVT/VT

Risk Stratification - San Francisco Syncope Rule (HIGH RISK if ANY):

  • History of CHF
  • ECG abnormal (any non-sinus rhythm or new changes)
  • Anemia (Hct <30%)
  • Respiratory - Shortness of breath
  • Triage systolic BP <90 mmHg
  • If ANY present ‚Üí 7-10% risk of serious outcome at 30 days ‚Üí ADMIT

Labs (Based on Clinical Suspicion):

  • Basic: CBC (anemia?), BMP (electrolytes - hypokalemia can cause QT prolongation), glucose (hypoglycemia?)
  • If cardiac suspected: Troponin (ACS?), BNP (heart failure?), D-dimer if PE suspected
  • If volume depletion suspected: BUN/Cr ratio, orthostatic vitals
  • Pregnancy test: All women of childbearing age (ectopic pregnancy can present as syncope)

Advanced Testing (If Indicated):

  • Echocardiography: If structural heart disease suspected (murmur, abnormal ECG, exertional syncope, known heart disease). Assess for AS, HOCM, RV strain (PE), tamponade, LV function
  • Telemetry/Holter Monitor: If arrhythmia suspected. Admit for telemetry if high-risk features. Outpatient Holter (24-48hr) or event monitor (30 days) for recurrent syncope
  • Exercise Stress Test: If exertional syncope (evaluate for ischemia, arrhythmias). Contraindicated if severe AS suspected
  • Tilt Table Test: If recurrent syncope, no cardiac cause found. Reproduces vasovagal response. Positive = BP drop >25mmHg or HR <60 with symptoms
  • Electrophysiology Study (EP Study): For unexplained syncope with structural heart disease or high suspicion for arrhythmia. Can induce/document VT, assess sinus/AV node function
  • CT Head: Only if focal neurologic deficits, head trauma, persistent altered mental status. Syncope alone does NOT require head CT
  • Carotid Ultrasound: Generally low yield. Consider if carotid bruit or vertebrobasilar symptoms
  • CT PE Protocol: If dyspnea, pleuritic chest pain, hypoxia, tachycardia, RV strain on ECG

Orthostatic Vitals Technique:

  • Supine √ó 5 minutes ‚Üí measure BP/HR
  • Stand immediately ‚Üí measure at 1 minute and 3 minutes
  • Positive: SBP drop ‚â•20 mmHg OR DBP drop ‚â•10 mmHg OR HR increase ‚â•30 bpm (or HR >120)
  • Symptoms (lightheadedness, presyncope) with standing = clinical orthostasis even if BP criteria not met
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

General Principles:

  • Treatment depends on underlying cause
  • Admit if high-risk features (see San Francisco Syncope Rule, cardiac cause suspected)
  • Discharge if low-risk and clear benign etiology (young, vasovagal, normal ECG, normal exam)

Vasovagal Syncope (Reflex/Neurocardiogenic):

  • Reassurance: Benign condition, excellent prognosis. Explain pathophysiology
  • Trigger Avoidance: Avoid prolonged standing, hot environments, dehydration, emotional stress. Sit/lie down at first prodromal symptom
  • Hydration: Increase fluid intake to 2-3 L/day. Liberal salt intake (6-10g/day) if no contraindications
  • Physical Counterpressure Maneuvers: At first sign of prodrome: leg crossing + tensing, squatting, arm tensing. Can abort episode
  • Tilt Training: Gradual exposure to prolonged standing (start 5 min, increase to 30+ min)
  • Pharmacologic (Refractory cases only):
    • Midodrine 5-10mg TID (Œ±-agonist, increases BP) - first-line medication
    • Fludrocortisone 0.1-0.2mg daily (volume expansion)
    • Œ≤-blockers (metoprolol) - controversial, limited evidence
  • Pacemaker: Very rarely indicated. Only if severe cardioinhibitory response (asystole >3 sec) on tilt table and failed medical therapy

Orthostatic Hypotension:

  • Identify and Address Causes:
    • Volume depletion ‚Üí IV fluids, oral hydration
    • Medications ‚Üí stop/reduce Œ±-blockers, diuretics, vasodilators, antihypertensives, antidepressants
    • Autonomic dysfunction ‚Üí treat underlying (diabetes, Parkinson's, amyloidosis)
  • Non-Pharmacologic:
    • Rise slowly from supine/sitting (sit at edge of bed √ó 1 min before standing)
    • Increase fluid intake (2-3 L/day) and salt (6-10g/day if no CHF)
    • Compression stockings (waist-high, 30-40 mmHg) - wear before rising
    • Elevate head of bed 10-20 degrees at night (reduces nocturnal diuresis)
    • Avoid large meals (can cause postprandial hypotension)
    • Physical counterpressure maneuvers
  • Pharmacologic:
    • Midodrine 2.5-10mg TID (last dose before 6 PM to avoid supine hypertension)
    • Fludrocortisone 0.1-0.3mg daily (watch for hypokalemia, edema, supine hypertension)
    • Droxidopa 100-600mg TID (norepinephrine precursor, for neurogenic OH)
    • Pyridostigmine 60mg TID (if autonomic failure)

Cardiac Syncope - ADMIT for Telemetry:

  • Arrhythmia Management:
    • Bradycardia (sick sinus, AV block) ‚Üí Cardiology consult, consider pacemaker
    • Tachyarrhythmia (VT, SVT) ‚Üí Antiarrhythmics, ablation, ICD evaluation
    • Long QT syndrome ‚Üí Remove offending drugs, electrolyte repletion (Mg, K), Œ≤-blockers, ICD if high risk
    • Brugada, ARVC ‚Üí EP study, ICD placement
  • Structural Disease:
    • Aortic stenosis ‚Üí Cardiology/CT surgery referral for valve replacement if severe + symptomatic
    • HOCM ‚Üí Œ≤-blockers, avoid volume depletion/vasodilators. ICD if high risk. Septal reduction if refractory
    • Pulmonary embolism ‚Üí Anticoagulation, thrombolytics if massive PE
    • Cardiac tamponade ‚Üí Pericardiocentesis
  • Ischemia: If syncope due to ACS ‚Üí treat per ACS protocol (aspirin, heparin, cath lab)

Situational Syncope:

  • Micturition syncope ‚Üí Sit to void, avoid straining, stay hydrated
  • Defecation syncope ‚Üí Avoid straining (stool softeners), adequate hydration
  • Cough/swallow syncope ‚Üí Treat underlying cause (GERD, cough), maneuvers to reduce triggering

Neurologic Syncope (Rare):

  • Vertebrobasilar TIA ‚Üí Neurology consult, antiplatelet therapy, imaging (MRA/CTA neck)
  • Subclavian steal ‚Üí Vascular surgery referral
  • Seizure (if postictal confusion suggests) ‚Üí Neurology consult, antiepileptics, EEG
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • ECG is MANDATORY for ALL syncope patients: Single most important test. Can diagnose Brugada, long QT, WPW, ARVC, heart block, old MI. Never discharge syncope patient without ECG
  • Syncope while supine = cardiac until proven otherwise: Vasovagal almost never occurs supine. If patient syncopizes lying down, think arrhythmia and admit for telemetry
  • Exertional syncope = high-risk: Think HOCM (young athletes), AS (elderly), VT, coronary ischemia. These patients need echo, stress test, and cardiology evaluation
  • Red flags for cardiac syncope: Age >60, exertional, supine position, no prodrome, known heart disease, abnormal ECG, family history sudden cardiac death. Admit these patients
  • Vasovagal has a prodrome, cardiac doesn't: If patient describes nausea, diaphoresis, tunnel vision before passing out = vasovagal (low risk). No warning, sudden collapse = cardiac (high risk)
  • Don't confuse syncope with seizure: Syncope = brief LOC (<20 sec), rapid complete recovery, may have brief myoclonic jerks (<15 sec). Seizure = prolonged LOC, tonic-clonic movements, tongue biting, incontinence, postictal confusion/lethargy. But can be tough to distinguish!
  • Head CT rarely useful: Only if focal neurologic findings, head trauma, or persistent altered mental status. Syncope is due to global hypoperfusion, not focal brain lesion. Don't reflexively order head CT
  • San Francisco Syncope Rule is your friend: If none of the 5 criteria (CHF, abnormal ECG, Hct <30%, SOB, SBP <90), patient is low-risk (<1% serious outcome) and can likely be discharged with outpatient follow-up if vasovagal etiology clear
  • Orthostatic vitals technique matters: Must be supine √ó 5 min first, then check at 1 and 3 minutes standing. Immediate standing without adequate supine time can give false positives
  • Young healthy patient with classic vasovagal story needs minimal workup: Prodrome, triggered by standing/heat/emotion, quick recovery, normal ECG = can discharge with reassurance and anticipatory guidance. Don't over-test these patients
  • Medications are a common culprit: Œ±-blockers (BPH meds), diuretics, antihypertensives, TCAs, antipsychotics, PDE5 inhibitors. Always review medication list carefully
  • Carotid massage to diagnose carotid sinus hypersensitivity: Only perform if safe (no carotid bruits, no recent stroke/TIA). Massage one side √ó 5 seconds. Positive = >3 sec asystole or >50 mmHg BP drop. Never massage both sides simultaneously!
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Syncope with ongoing chest pain suggesting acute MI
  • Hemodynamically unstable (persistent hypotension, shock)
  • Malignant arrhythmia (sustained VT/VF requiring cardioversion)
  • High-grade AV block (Mobitz II, complete heart block) requiring pacing
  • Massive pulmonary embolism with hypotension
  • Cardiac tamponade
  • Aortic dissection
  • Ruptured ectopic pregnancy with hemorrhagic shock
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Clear benign etiology identified (vasovagal, orthostatic)
  • NO high-risk features (San Francisco Syncope Rule negative)
  • Normal or unchanged ECG
  • Normal vital signs and orthostatics
  • No concerning cardiac exam findings
  • Labs normal (if obtained)
  • No recurrent episodes during observation
  • Able to ambulate safely without recurrence
  • Patient counseled on trigger avoidance (if vasovagal)
  • Close follow-up arranged (PCP within 1 week, Cardiology if needed)
  • Clear return precautions: recurrent syncope, chest pain, palpitations, dyspnea
  • Driving restrictions discussed (varies by state, typically 3-6 months abstinence after cardiac syncope)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

From Syncope Episode: Trauma from fall (head injury, fractures, lacerations), motor vehicle accidents (if driving), sudden cardiac death (if malignant arrhythmia), prolonged cerebral hypoperfusion causing ischemia (rare)

From Underlying Cardiac Cause: Sudden cardiac death (VT/VF, complete heart block), acute MI/ACS, cardiac arrest, stroke from arrhythmia with thrombus formation, progression of structural heart disease (AS, HOCM)

From Recurrent Episodes: Injury from repeated falls, impaired quality of life, anxiety/depression, loss of driving privileges, loss of employment (especially if operate machinery), social isolation

From Treatment: Supine hypertension (midodrine, fludrocortisone - can cause nocturnal HTN), hypokalemia (fludrocortisone), edema (fludrocortisone), bradycardia (Œ≤-blockers), pacemaker complications (infection, lead malfunction), ICD shocks (appropriate or inappropriate)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

41. PERICARDITIS/PERICARDIAL EFFUSION/CARDIAC TAMPONADE

Inflammatory ‚Ä¢ Effusion ‚Ä¢ Tamponade Physiology
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic Pericarditis: Sharp, pleuritic chest pain worse with inspiration and lying flat, relieved by sitting forward. Friction rub (scratchy, triphasic sound)

Pericardial Effusion: Often asymptomatic if slow-accumulating. Dyspnea, muffled heart sounds, enlarged cardiac silhouette on CXR

Cardiac Tamponade: Beck's triad (hypotension, JVD, muffled heart sounds), pulsus paradoxus >10mmHg, tachycardia, dyspnea, shock

Alternate Presentations:

  • Uremic pericarditis: ESRD patients, often painless. May indicate need for urgent dialysis
  • Post-MI (Dressler syndrome): 1-3 weeks post-MI. Fever, pleurisy, pericarditis
  • Malignant effusion: Gradual dyspnea, weight loss. Lung/breast cancer, lymphoma most common
  • TB pericarditis: Endemic areas, HIV patients. Chronic symptoms, large effusions
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Labs: CBC, CRP/ESR (elevated), troponin (mildly elevated if myopericarditis), BUN/Cr, TSH, ANA if autoimmune suspected

ECG: Diffuse ST elevation with PR depression (90%), upright T waves. Stage 1‚Üí4: ST elevation ‚Üí normalization ‚Üí T inversion ‚Üí resolution

Imaging:

  • CXR: Enlarged cardiac silhouette if >250mL effusion ("water bottle heart")
  • Echocardiogram: GOLD STANDARD. Quantify effusion, assess for tamponade physiology (RA/RV collapse, respiratory variation, IVC plethora)
  • CT chest: If imaging for etiology (malignancy, TB, trauma)

Pericardiocentesis: If tamponade, purulent/TB suspected, or large effusion not responding to NSAIDs. Send fluid for cell count, cultures, cytology, adenosine deaminase (TB)

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Acute Pericarditis (no effusion/tamponade):

  • First-line: NSAIDs + Colchicine. Ibuprofen 600-800mg TID √ó 1-2 weeks, taper over 1-2 weeks. Colchicine 0.6mg BID √ó 3 months (reduces recurrence 50%)
  • Alternative: Aspirin 750-1000mg TID (if post-MI or ASA allergy)
  • Steroids: AVOID unless refractory or contraindication to NSAIDs (increases recurrence risk). Prednisone 0.25-0.5mg/kg/day
  • Activity restriction: No strenuous exercise until symptoms resolve and CRP normalizes (risk of recurrence/tamponade)

Pericardial Effusion (no tamponade):

  • Small/moderate: Treat underlying cause, anti-inflammatory therapy, serial echos q48-72hr
  • Large (>20mm): Admit for monitoring, daily echos, consider pericardiocentesis if symptomatic or not responding

Cardiac Tamponade (EMERGENCY):

  • Immediate: Aggressive IV fluids (increase preload), avoid diuretics/vasodilators
  • Definitive: Urgent pericardiocentesis (echo or fluoro-guided). Drain 50mL often enough for immediate improvement
  • Approach: Subxiphoid (most common). Leave pigtail catheter if large volume or suspected recurrence
  • Refractory: Surgical drainage (pericardial window) if loculated, recurrent, or traumatic
  • Post-procedure: Monitor for re-accumulation. Echo in 24-48hr

Specific Etiologies:

  • Uremic: Urgent dialysis. Most resolve after adequate dialysis
  • Malignant: Drainage for symptoms, often recurs. Consider pericardial window or sclerosis
  • Purulent: Pericardiocentesis + broad-spectrum antibiotics (vanco + ceftriaxone). Often needs surgical drainage
  • TB: Rifampin, isoniazid, pyrazinamide, ethambutol (RIPE) √ó 2 months, then RIF/INH √ó 4 months. Consider steroids (reduce constriction)
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Pericarditis ECG vs STEMI: Pericarditis has diffuse ST elevation (multiple leads) with PR depression, STEMI has localized ST elevation with reciprocal changes. Pericarditis ST is concave ("smiling"), STEMI is convex ("frowning")
  • Pulsus paradoxus: >10mmHg drop in SBP with inspiration. Normal <10mmHg. Also seen in COPD, PE, severe asthma
  • Colchicine is key: Reduces recurrence from 30-50% to 10-15%. Use in all cases unless contraindicated
  • Low-pressure tamponade: Can occur with hypovolemia or RV dysfunction. Tamponade physiology without elevated JVP
  • Electrical alternans: Beat-to-beat variation in QRS amplitude. Classic for large effusion/tamponade but uncommon (10-20%)
  • Avoid steroids if possible: 2-4√ó increased risk of recurrence. Reserve for refractory cases or contraindication to NSAIDs
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Cardiac tamponade (SBP <90, pulsus paradoxus >20mmHg)
  • Hemodynamic instability
  • Large/rapidly accumulating effusion
  • Need for urgent pericardiocentesis
  • Myopericarditis with arrhythmias or reduced EF
  • Purulent pericarditis
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Pain controlled on oral NSAIDs
  • Hemodynamically stable
  • No evidence of tamponade on echo
  • CRP trending down
  • If effusion: Small/stable on repeat echo
  • Follow-up arranged (repeat echo in 1 week if effusion present)
  • Activity restrictions counseled
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Cardiac tamponade (most feared), myopericarditis (troponin ‚Üë, EF ‚Üì), arrhythmias (rare)

Chronic: Recurrent pericarditis (15-30%, higher without colchicine), constrictive pericarditis (chronic inflammation ‚Üí calcification ‚Üí restricted filling, often post-TB/radiation/cardiac surgery)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

42. INFECTIVE ENDOCARDITIS

Native Valve ‚Ä¢ Prosthetic Valve ‚Ä¢ Duke Criteria
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic: Fever (90%), new or changing murmur (85%), embolic phenomena, positive blood cultures. Subacute: weeks of constitutional symptoms

Acute Presentation: Rapid valve destruction, heart failure, septic shock. More common with S. aureus

Peripheral Stigmata (classic but rare <10%):

  • Osler nodes: Painful, raised, red nodules on fingers/toes (immunologic)
  • Janeway lesions: Painless, red macules on palms/soles (septic emboli)
  • Splinter hemorrhages: Linear red-brown streaks under nails
  • Roth spots: Retinal hemorrhages with pale centers (rare)

Complications at Presentation:

  • Heart failure: Acute regurgitation from valve destruction (most common cause of death)
  • Embolic stroke: 15-30%, highest risk with mitral valve and S. aureus
  • Mycotic aneurysm: Brain, splenic, coronary arteries
  • Perivalvular abscess: New heart block (AV node near aortic valve)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP (Duke Criteria)

Blood Cultures: 3 sets from separate sites BEFORE antibiotics. Time spacing less critical than previously thought (can obtain all within 1-2hr if clinically unstable)

Imaging:

  • TTE: First-line. Sensitivity 60-70% for vegetations. Excellent for assessment of valve function and complications
  • TEE: Gold standard. Sensitivity 90-95%. REQUIRED if: prosthetic valve, negative TTE with high suspicion, suspected abscess/perforation, S. aureus bacteremia
  • Cardiac CT/MRI: If TEE contraindicated or to evaluate perivalvular extension
  • Brain MRI: If neurologic symptoms (ischemic stroke, hemorrhage, abscess, mycotic aneurysm)

Labs: CBC (anemia of chronic disease), ESR/CRP (elevated), UA (hematuria/proteinuria), RF (positive in subacute), creatinine, LFTs

Duke Criteria (Definite IE = 2 major OR 1 major + 3 minor OR 5 minor):

  • Major: (1) Positive blood cultures with typical organisms in ‚â•2 separate cultures, OR persistently positive cultures; (2) Echocardiographic evidence (vegetation, abscess, new dehiscence of prosthetic valve, new valvular regurgitation)
  • Minor: Predisposing condition, fever >38¬∞C, vascular phenomena (emboli, mycotic aneurysm), immunologic phenomena (Osler nodes, Roth spots, RF), microbiologic evidence not meeting major criteria
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Empiric Antibiotics (start after blood cultures √ó 3):

  • Native valve: Vancomycin 15mg/kg IV q12h + ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h
  • Prosthetic valve: Vancomycin + ceftriaxone + gentamicin 1mg/kg IV q8h (or cefepime instead of ceftriaxone)
  • IVDU: Same as native valve (covers S. aureus, Streptococcus, gram-negatives)

Organism-Specific Therapy:

  • Viridans Streptococcus (most common, ~30%): Penicillin G 4 million units IV q4h OR ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h √ó 4 weeks. Can add gentamicin √ó 2 weeks for synergy
  • S. aureus (MSSA): Nafcillin/oxacillin 2g IV q4h √ó 4-6 weeks. Consider adding gentamicin √ó 3-5 days for synergy
  • S. aureus (MRSA): Vancomycin 15mg/kg IV q12h √ó 6 weeks. Consider daptomycin 8-10mg/kg if persistent bacteremia
  • Enterococcus: Ampicillin 2g IV q4h + gentamicin 1mg/kg q8h (or ceftriaxone 2g q12h instead of gent) √ó 4-6 weeks
  • HACEK (Haemophilus, Aggregatibacter, Cardiobacterium, Eikenella, Kingella): Ceftriaxone 2g IV q24h √ó 4 weeks
  • Culture-negative (10-15%): Continue empiric therapy. Consider Bartonella, Coxiella, Brucella, fungi in specific epidemiology

Duration: Native valve 4-6 weeks (S. aureus 6 weeks), prosthetic valve 6 weeks minimum. Time from FIRST negative blood culture

Surgical Indications (Cardiothoracic Surgery Consult):

  • Heart failure: From severe acute regurgitation or valve obstruction (Class I indication, emergent)
  • Uncontrolled infection: Perivalvular abscess, persistent bacteremia >5-7 days on appropriate antibiotics, fungal endocarditis
  • Prevention of embolism: Large mobile vegetation (>10mm) with embolic event, especially anterior mitral valve
  • Prosthetic valve: Early prosthetic valve endocarditis (<1 year), heart failure, dehiscence, persistent bacteremia
  • Timing: Emergent (heart failure/cardiogenic shock), urgent (uncontrolled infection, abscess), elective (large vegetation, embolic prevention)

Post-Stroke Timing for Surgery: If intracranial hemorrhage, delay surgery if possible. If ischemic stroke without hemorrhage, surgery can proceed but discuss risk/benefit

üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • S. aureus bacteremia = TEE: 25% have IE even without murmur. Always get TEE for S. aureus bacteremia unless clear alternative source + no cardiac risk factors
  • Culture first, antibiotics second: Don't delay cultures waiting for fever spike. Get 3 sets ASAP then start empiric therapy (unless stable subacute presentation)
  • Right-sided endocarditis: IVDU, indwelling catheters. Tricuspid valve most common. Septic pulmonary emboli (not stroke). Better prognosis than left-sided
  • New heart block = perivalvular abscess: AV node near aortic valve. High-grade block indicates abscess until proven otherwise. Surgical emergency
  • Anticoagulation controversy: If on warfarin for other indication (prosthetic valve, AFib), continue but avoid starting new anticoagulation (doesn't prevent emboli, may increase hemorrhagic stroke)
  • Mycotic aneurysm: Not fungal! Bacterial infection of arterial wall. Can rupture. Brain MRA if headache or neurologic symptoms
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Hemodynamic instability/cardiogenic shock
  • Acute heart failure (pulmonary edema)
  • High-grade heart block (perivalvular abscess)
  • Embolic stroke with decreased level of consciousness
  • Septic shock despite antibiotics
  • Perioperative (cardiac surgery for IE)
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Appropriate IV antibiotics for ‚â•7-14 days (bloodstream clearance documented)
  • Hemodynamically stable
  • Blood cultures negative √ó 2-3 sets
  • No indication for urgent surgery
  • Plan for completion of IV antibiotics (home IV, long-term facility, or outpatient infusion)
  • Follow-up with Infectious Disease and Cardiology arranged
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Cardiac: Heart failure (most common cause of death, 50-60%), perivalvular abscess (10-40% prosthetic, 10-15% native), valve perforation, fistula formation

Neurologic: Ischemic stroke (15-30%), intracerebral hemorrhage (5%), mycotic aneurysm rupture (rare but devastating), brain abscess

Embolic: Spleen (abdominal pain), kidneys (hematuria, infarction), bones (osteomyelitis, vertebral), coronary arteries (MI)

Immunologic: Glomerulonephritis (immune complex deposition), arthritis, splinter hemorrhages

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

43. MYOCARDITIS

Viral ‚Ä¢ Immune-mediated ‚Ä¢ Fulminant
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic: Chest pain (mimics MI), dyspnea, fatigue, palpitations. Often recent viral URI or gastroenteritis (1-3 weeks prior)

Severity Spectrum:

  • Mild: Chest pain, elevated troponin, preserved EF. May be asymptomatic
  • Moderate: Heart failure symptoms, reduced EF, arrhythmias
  • Fulminant: Cardiogenic shock, severe LV dysfunction, arrhythmias. Higher mortality but survivors often recover completely

Alternate Presentations:

  • Sudden cardiac death: Ventricular arrhythmias in young, previously healthy patients
  • Dilated cardiomyopathy: Chronic presentation after subclinical acute phase
  • Pericardial involvement: Myopericarditis (chest pain, friction rub, ST elevation + troponin elevation)
  • Post-COVID: Multisystem inflammatory syndrome (MIS-C in children, MIS-A in adults)
  • Vaccine-related: mRNA COVID vaccines (rare, ~12 cases/million, mostly young males, mild)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Labs: Troponin (elevated, often markedly), BNP, CBC, CRP/ESR, BMP, LFTs. Viral PCR if clinically indicated

ECG: ST elevation (diffuse, not localized), T wave changes, conduction abnormalities. May mimic STEMI or pericarditis

Imaging:

  • Echocardiogram: Assess EF, regional wall motion abnormalities, pericardial effusion, LV thrombus
  • Cardiac MRI: GOLD STANDARD for diagnosis (when stable). Late gadolinium enhancement in mid-wall/epicardial distribution (vs subendocardial in MI). T2-weighted imaging shows edema
  • Coronary angiography: If concern for ACS (especially if STEMI-like presentation, risk factors). Normal coronaries support myocarditis

Endomyocardial Biopsy: Gold standard but rarely done. Consider if: fulminant myocarditis, giant cell myocarditis suspected, not improving with supportive care, concern for infiltrative disease

Etiologies:

  • Viral (most common): Coxsackie B, adenovirus, parvovirus B19, HHV-6, COVID-19, influenza
  • Autoimmune: Giant cell myocarditis, cardiac sarcoidosis, lupus, eosinophilic myocarditis
  • Toxic: Cocaine, alcohol, chemotherapy (anthracyclines), clozapine
  • Immune checkpoint inhibitors: Pembrolizumab, nivolumab. High mortality if not recognized early
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Supportive Care (Mainstay):

  • Activity restriction: CRITICAL. Bed rest, no exercise for ‚â•3-6 months (even if feeling better). Exercise during acute phase increases mortality
  • Heart failure management: ACE-I/ARB, Œ≤-blockers (start low, go slow when stable), diuretics, aldosterone antagonist
  • Arrhythmia monitoring: Telemetry. Treat ventricular arrhythmias per ACLS. Consider temporary pacing if high-grade block
  • Anticoagulation: If EF <35%, consider prophylaxis for LV thrombus prevention

Immunosuppression (CONTROVERSIAL):

  • Generally NOT recommended for viral myocarditis (may worsen outcomes)
  • Consider for: Giant cell myocarditis, cardiac sarcoidosis, eosinophilic myocarditis, immune checkpoint inhibitor myocarditis
  • Regimen (if indicated): High-dose steroids ¬± other immunosuppression (azathioprine, cyclosporine). Consult cardiology/rheumatology

Fulminant Myocarditis (ICU Management):

  • Inotropes: Dobutamine or milrinone if low cardiac output
  • Mechanical support: IABP, Impella, VA-ECMO if cardiogenic shock. Bridge to recovery (most improve) or transplant
  • Temporary pacing: If complete heart block
  • AVOID: NSAIDs (may worsen myocardial inflammation and increase mortality). Use acetaminophen for pain

Specific Etiologies:

  • Giant cell: High-dose steroids + immunosuppression. Transplant evaluation
  • Immune checkpoint inhibitor: STOP immunotherapy immediately. High-dose methylprednisolone 1g/day √ó 3 days. Consider other immunosuppression if refractory
  • Eosinophilic: Steroids. Check for hypereosinophilic syndrome
  • Chagas: Benznidazole or nifurtimox (if acute). Treat heart failure in chronic phase
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Myocarditis vs MI: Younger age, no CAD risk factors, normal coronaries on angiography, diffuse ST elevation, troponin often disproportionately high for degree of ECG changes
  • Cardiac MRI is diagnostic: Mid-wall/epicardial late gadolinium enhancement (vs subendocardial in MI). T2 signal for edema. Sensitivity/specificity >85%
  • Fulminant paradox: Worse acute presentation but BETTER long-term outcomes (if survive). More likely to recover completely. Chronic myocarditis has worse prognosis
  • Activity restriction saves lives: Athletes who return to sports too early have increased risk of sudden death. Minimum 3-6 months rest, longer if EF reduced
  • NSAIDs are contraindicated: Animal studies show increased mortality. Use acetaminophen instead
  • Troponin levels don't predict outcomes: Very high troponin can be seen in mild cases. EF and hemodynamics are better prognostic markers
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Fulminant myocarditis (cardiogenic shock, EF <30%)
  • Hemodynamic instability
  • Sustained ventricular arrhythmias
  • High-grade AV block
  • Need for inotropes or mechanical support
  • Respiratory failure
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Hemodynamically stable off inotropes
  • No sustained arrhythmias ‚â•48hr
  • On appropriate heart failure meds
  • Activity restrictions counseled (critical!)
  • Follow-up arranged (cardiology in 1-2 weeks, repeat echo in 1-3 months)
  • If EF reduced: Wearable defibrillator (LifeVest) until reassessment
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Cardiogenic shock (10-15% fulminant cases), sustained VT/VF (sudden death risk), complete heart block, acute heart failure

Chronic: Dilated cardiomyopathy (10-30% of acute cases progress), chronic heart failure, need for transplant, recurrent myocarditis (rare), ventricular aneurysm

Recovery: Most patients with preserved EF recover completely. Reduced EF may improve over 6-12 months with aggressive medical therapy. Repeat imaging essential

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

44. CARDIOGENIC SHOCK

Pump Failure ‚Ä¢ Mechanical Complications ‚Ä¢ MCS
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Definition: Sustained hypotension (SBP <90mmHg) + end-organ hypoperfusion + cardiac dysfunction. Cardiac index <2.2 L/min/m¬≤, PCWP >15mmHg

Classic: Hypotension, cool extremities, altered mental status, oliguria, elevated lactate. Following acute MI, myocarditis, or acute decompensated heart failure

Clinical Signs:

  • Hypoperfusion: Cold, clammy skin, delayed capillary refill (>3 sec), weak pulses, confusion, oliguria (<0.5mL/kg/hr)
  • Congestion: Elevated JVP, pulmonary edema, hepatomegaly, peripheral edema (often "cold and wet")
  • Compensatory: Tachycardia (attempt to maintain cardiac output), tachypnea

SCAI Shock Stages:

  • Stage A (At risk): No hypotension but risk factors present
  • Stage B (Beginning): Relative hypotension, tachycardia, without hypoperfusion
  • Stage C (Classic): Hypotension requiring intervention, tissue hypoperfusion
  • Stage D (Deteriorating): Not responding to initial interventions, worsening hypoperfusion
  • Stage E (Extremis): Circulatory collapse, cardiac arrest, requiring CPR or multiple interventions
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Labs: Lactate (>2 suggests shock), BMP (creatinine rising), LFTs (hepatic hypoperfusion, transaminitis), CBC, troponin, BNP, blood gas (metabolic acidosis)

Imaging:

  • ECG: Assess for acute MI (most common cause), arrhythmia
  • Echocardiogram: URGENT. Assess EF, wall motion, valvular pathology, mechanical complications (VSD, acute MR, free wall rupture), pericardial effusion/tamponade, RV function
  • CXR: Pulmonary edema, cardiomegaly
  • Coronary angiography: If STEMI/NSTEMI. Revascularization is cornerstone of treatment for MI-related shock

Hemodynamic Monitoring (consider PA catheter or advanced monitoring):

  • Cardiogenic shock: CI <2.2, PCWP >15, SVR elevated
  • Helps differentiate: From distributive shock (high CI, low SVR), hypovolemic shock (low PCWP)

Etiologies:

  • Acute MI (80%): Large anterior MI, mechanical complications (VSR, acute MR, free wall rupture)
  • Acute decompensated HF: On chronic cardiomyopathy
  • Acute valvular: Acute severe MR (papillary muscle rupture, endocarditis), acute severe AR (endocarditis, dissection)
  • Myocarditis: Fulminant, usually viral or autoimmune
  • RV failure: Massive PE, RV infarction
  • Arrhythmia: Sustained VT, bradycardia, uncontrolled AFib with RVR
  • Tako-tsubo (stress cardiomyopathy): Apical ballooning, mimics MI
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

General Principles:

  • Treat underlying cause: Emergent revascularization (PCI/CABG) for MI, surgery for mechanical complications, cardioversion for arrhythmia
  • Optimize preload: Careful fluid management (avoid overload if already congested, give fluids if underfilled - esp RV MI)
  • Improve contractility: Inotropes
  • Reduce afterload: Vasodilators (if adequate BP)
  • Mechanical circulatory support: If refractory to medical therapy

Inotropes (ICU - arterial line required):

  • Dobutamine: 2.5-20mcg/kg/min. First-line. Œ≤‚ÇÅ agonist, increases contractility and reduces afterload. Risk: tachycardia, arrhythmias
  • Milrinone: Load 50mcg/kg over 10min, then 0.375-0.75mcg/kg/min. PDE-3 inhibitor, inotrope + vasodilator. Avoid if hypotensive. Risk: hypotension, arrhythmias
  • Dopamine: 5-20mcg/kg/min. Œ≤ and Œ± effects at higher doses (vasoconstriction). More arrhythmogenic than dobutamine
  • Epinephrine: 0.05-0.5mcg/kg/min. For refractory shock. Potent inotrope + vasoconstrictor. Risk: tachycardia, ischemia, lactic acidosis
  • Norepinephrine: Add if persistent hypotension despite inotropes (provides vasoconstriction)

Vasodilators (only if adequate BP, SBP >90):

  • Nitroprusside: 0.25-10mcg/kg/min. Reduces afterload, improves forward flow. Watch for cyanide toxicity (>48hr high doses)
  • Nitroglycerin: Reduces preload (less afterload reduction than nitroprusside)

Mechanical Circulatory Support (MCS) - Cardiology/CT Surgery Consult:

  • Intra-aortic balloon pump (IABP): Inflates in diastole (improves coronary perfusion), deflates in systole (reduces afterload). Modest improvement in hemodynamics. No mortality benefit per IABP-SHOCK II trial, but still used as bridge
  • Impella: Percutaneous ventricular assist device. Greater hemodynamic support than IABP. Can be placed at catheterization
  • VA-ECMO: Veno-arterial ECMO. Complete cardiopulmonary support. For refractory shock. Bridge to recovery, bridge to decision, or bridge to durable VAD/transplant
  • Surgical VAD: For bridge to transplant or destination therapy in refractory chronic cardiogenic shock

Specific Scenarios:

  • STEMI with shock: Emergent PCI (or CABG if anatomy requires). Revascularization reduces mortality 50%. Consider MCS if refractory
  • Mechanical complications post-MI: Surgery (VSD repair, MV repair/replacement, free wall rupture repair). Temporize with IABP/Impella until surgery
  • RV MI/shock: AVOID diuretics/nitrates (need preload). Give IV fluids aggressively, maintain preload. Revascularization of RCA. May need inotropes
  • Fulminant myocarditis: Supportive, mechanical support (ECMO). Most recover with bridge support

Avoid in Cardiogenic Shock:

  • Œ≤-blockers: Worsen contractility and shock (restart when stabilized)
  • ACE-I/ARB: Worsen hypotension acutely (restart when stabilized)
  • Excessive fluids: If already volume overloaded (worsens pulmonary edema). Tailor to individual (RV MI needs fluids!)
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • "Time is muscle": In MI-related cardiogenic shock, door-to-balloon time <90min reduces mortality. Revascularization is MOST important intervention
  • Warm vs Cold, Dry vs Wet: Cold = hypoperfused (needs inotropes ¬± MCS), Warm = adequate perfusion. Wet = congested (needs diuresis), Dry = needs volume. Most CS is "cold and wet"
  • Lactate trends matter: Elevated and rising lactate = worsening shock, need for escalation. Clearing lactate = improving
  • RV MI is different: Hypotension + clear lungs + JVD. Give fluids, NOT diuretics. Revascularize RCA
  • Mechanical complications timing: VSR (1-5 days post-MI), free wall rupture (3-5 days), papillary muscle rupture (2-7 days). New murmur + shock = echo STAT!
  • Mortality remains high: Despite advances, CS mortality 40-50%. Early recognition and aggressive treatment (revascularization, MCS) improve outcomes
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • ALL cardiogenic shock patients require ICU
  • Need for inotropes/pressors
  • Hypotension (SBP <90mmHg)
  • End-organ hypoperfusion (oliguria, AMS, lactate >2)
  • Mechanical circulatory support
  • Post-cardiac catheterization/surgery
‚úì ICU ‚Üí FLOOR CRITERIA
  • Hemodynamically stable off inotropes ‚â•24hr
  • SBP >90-100mmHg consistently
  • Adequate end-organ perfusion (normal lactate, UOP, mentation)
  • No mechanical support
  • Underlying cause treated (revascularized, etc)
  • Transitioned to oral heart failure medications
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Death (40-50% mortality), multi-organ failure (renal, hepatic, intestinal ischemia), arrhythmias, cardiac arrest, limb ischemia (from MCS), stroke

MCS-related: Bleeding, vascular injury, infection, hemolysis (ECMO), limb ischemia (IABP, Impella), LV distension/thrombus (VA-ECMO)

Chronic (survivors): Chronic heart failure, need for transplant, durable VAD, recurrent ischemic events, chronic kidney disease

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

45. SYMPTOMATIC BRADYCARDIA/HEART BLOCK (PACING)

Sinus Bradycardia ‚Ä¢ AV Block ‚Ä¢ Pacing Indications
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic: Lightheadedness, presyncope, syncope, dyspnea, chest pain, fatigue with bradycardia (HR <50-60). May be asymptomatic if chronic and compensated

Emergency Presentations:

  • Syncope: Sudden loss of consciousness from inadequate cerebral perfusion
  • Shock/hypotension: Bradycardia-induced low cardiac output
  • Heart failure: Pulmonary edema from inadequate heart rate compensation
  • Ventricular escape rhythms: Wide-complex slow rhythms (unreliable, unstable)

Types of Bradycardia:

  • Sinus bradycardia: Normal P waves, normal PR, slow rate. Can be physiologic (athletes) or pathologic
  • 1¬∞ AV block: PR >200ms. Every P conducted. Usually benign, no treatment needed
  • 2¬∞ AV block Mobitz I (Wenckebach): Progressive PR prolongation until dropped QRS. Usually benign unless symptomatic
  • 2¬∞ AV block Mobitz II: Fixed PR with intermittent dropped QRS. High risk of complete heart block. Often needs pacing
  • 3¬∞ AV block (complete): No AV conduction. Atria and ventricles beat independently. Always pathologic
  • High-grade AV block: ‚â•2 consecutive P waves not conducted. Unstable, high-risk
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

ECG: Characterize rhythm, measure intervals, identify level of block (AV node vs infranodal)

  • Narrow QRS escape: Block at AV node (more benign, responds to atropine)
  • Wide QRS escape: Infranodal block (His-Purkinje). More unstable, less responsive to atropine, needs pacing

Labs: Electrolytes (K, Mg, Ca), digoxin level if on digoxin, TSH, troponin (if concern for MI)

Imaging: Echocardiogram (assess for structural disease, pericardial effusion/tamponade if suspected)

Identify Reversible Causes:

  • Medications: Œ≤-blockers, CCBs (diltiazem/verapamil), digoxin, antiarrhythmics (amiodarone, sotalol)
  • Ischemia: Inferior MI (RCA ‚Üí AV node), anterior MI with BBB/complete heart block (LAD ‚Üí His-Purkinje)
  • Infiltrative: Sarcoid, amyloid, hemochromatosis
  • Inflammatory: Lyme disease (endemic areas), endocarditis (perivalvular abscess)
  • Metabolic: Hypothyroidism, hyperkalemia, hypoxia
  • Increased vagal tone: Athletes, sleep, vasovagal
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

ACLS for Unstable Bradycardia:

  • Atropine: 1mg IV (can repeat q3-5min, max 3mg). First-line for symptomatic bradycardia. Blocks vagal tone. Works for AV nodal block, not infranodal
  • Transcutaneous pacing (TCP): If atropine ineffective or severe symptoms. Apply pads, set rate 60-80, increase output until capture (usually 50-100mA). Sedate if conscious!
  • Dopamine: 5-20mcg/kg/min. If atropine ineffective and TCP not available/not working
  • Epinephrine: 2-10mcg/min infusion. Alternative to dopamine

Transvenous Pacing (Temporary):

  • Indications: Symptomatic bradycardia refractory to medical therapy, unstable 2¬∞ Mobitz II or 3¬∞ AVB, post-MI with high-grade block
  • Approach: Central venous access (IJ or subclavian), fluoroscopy-guided wire into RV apex. Set rate 60-80, output 2√ó threshold
  • Temporary bridge: To permanent pacemaker or until reversible cause resolves

Permanent Pacemaker Indications (Cardiology Consult):

  • Class I (Definite):
    • 3¬∞ AVB or advanced 2¬∞ AVB (any level) with symptomatic bradycardia, pauses >3sec while awake, or escape rate <40
    • 3¬∞ AVB or advanced 2¬∞ AVB at any anatomic level associated with arrhythmias or medical conditions requiring drugs that result in symptomatic bradycardia
    • Persistent 2¬∞ Mobitz II AVB or 3¬∞ AVB after MI (even if asymptomatic)
    • Sinus node dysfunction with symptomatic bradycardia or pauses
    • Symptomatic chronotropic incompetence
    • Alternating BBB
  • Post-MI: Persistent 2¬∞ Mobitz II, 3¬∞ AVB, alternating BBB (even if asymptomatic) - permanent pacemaker indicated. Transient block with inferior MI usually resolves
  • Type of pacemaker: Dual-chamber (DDD) vs single-chamber (VVI) depending on underlying rhythm and conduction

Treating Reversible Causes:

  • Medications: Hold or reduce offending agents (Œ≤-blockers, CCBs, digoxin). Consider digibind for digoxin toxicity
  • Ischemia: Revascularization. Inferior MI with AV block often temporary (RCA supplies AV node). Anterior MI with high-grade block is more permanent (LAD supplies His bundle)
  • Hypothyroidism: Levothyroxine replacement (bradycardia improves over weeks)
  • Lyme carditis: Ceftriaxone 2g IV daily √ó 14-21 days. Often resolves, may need temporary pacing
  • Hyperkalemia: Treat hyperkalemia (calcium, insulin/D50, kayexelate, dialysis)
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Mobitz I vs Mobitz II: Mobitz I (Wenckebach) = progressive PR prolongation, block at AV node, usually benign. Mobitz II = sudden dropped beat without PR change, infranodal block, high risk of complete heart block, often needs pacing
  • Atropine can worsen infranodal block: By increasing sinus rate, more beats reach diseased His-Purkinje ‚Üí paradoxical worsening. Use cautiously if wide-complex escape or known infranodal disease
  • Inferior MI vs Anterior MI: Inferior (RCA occlusion) ‚Üí AV nodal ischemia ‚Üí transient block, usually resolves. Anterior (LAD) ‚Üí septal infarct, His-Purkinje damage ‚Üí often permanent, needs PPM
  • High-grade AV block ‚â† 3¬∞ AVB: High-grade = ‚â•2 consecutive non-conducted P waves but occasional conduction still present. Still dangerous, often progresses to complete
  • PPM after MI timing: If persistent block (not resolved by day 5-7), permanent pacemaker indicated. Transient block with inferior MI often resolves
  • Lyme carditis can cause ANY degree of AV block: Think Lyme in endemic areas (Northeast US) with new AV block, especially if young, no CAD. Usually reversible with antibiotics
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Unstable vital signs (hypotension, shock)
  • Syncope/presyncope
  • 3¬∞ AV block or high-grade 2¬∞ AV block
  • Mobitz II 2¬∞ AV block
  • Need for temporary pacing (TCP or transvenous)
  • Bradycardia + acute MI
  • Ventricular escape rhythm
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Asymptomatic on telemetry monitoring
  • Reversible cause identified and treated
  • If permanent pacemaker placed: functioning appropriately, no complications
  • Stable heart rate and rhythm ‚â•24-48hr
  • No syncope/presyncope episodes
  • Device check arranged (if PPM placed)
  • Cardiology follow-up arranged
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Syncope/falls, progression to complete heart block, asystole, ventricular escape rhythms (unstable), cardiogenic shock

Post-PPM: Pneumothorax (CXR post-placement), lead dislodgment, infection (pocket or endocarditis), hematoma, venous thrombosis, pacemaker syndrome (AV dyssynchrony in VVI pacing)

Chronic: Pacemaker-dependent (if complete heart block), need for generator replacement (battery life 5-15 years), lead complications, heart failure (if excessive RV pacing)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

46. VENTRICULAR TACHYCARDIA/VENTRICULAR FIBRILLATION

Wide Complex Tachycardia ‚Ä¢ Sudden Cardiac Arrest ‚Ä¢ ICD
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

VT (Ventricular Tachycardia): Wide-complex (QRS >120ms) tachycardia at rate >100bpm. Can be stable or unstable

  • Stable (Monomorphic VT): Palpitations, dyspnea, chest pain, presyncope. Hemodynamically stable, conscious
  • Unstable: Syncope, hypotension (SBP <90), altered mental status, pulmonary edema, shock
  • Pulseless VT: Cardiac arrest. No pulse, unconscious. Treat as VF

VF (Ventricular Fibrillation): Chaotic ventricular activity, no organized QRS complexes. Cardiac arrest. Pulseless, unconscious. Rapidly fatal without defibrillation

Special Types:

  • Polymorphic VT: Varying QRS morphology. Often degenerates to VF. If QT prolonged = Torsades de Pointes
  • Torsades de Pointes: Polymorphic VT with prolonged QT (>500ms). "Twisting of the points". Caused by QT-prolonging drugs, electrolyte abnormalities
  • Ischemic VT: Post-MI scar-related reentry. Most common in adults
  • Non-ischemic VT: Dilated cardiomyopathy, sarcoid, ARVD, genetic (Brugada, Long QT)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Immediate (During Event):

  • ECG/Telemetry: Document rhythm. Assess QRS width, morphology, rate, AV dissociation
  • Differentiate VT from SVT with aberrancy: Brugada criteria, AV dissociation (diagnostic of VT), capture/fusion beats, very wide QRS (>140ms), extreme axis deviation
  • Pearl: If wide-complex tachycardia and unsure, assume VT (safer). "When in doubt, it's VT"

Post-Event Workup:

  • Labs: Troponin (MI trigger?), BMP (K, Mg - correct abnormalities), CBC, digoxin level if on digoxin
  • ECG: Baseline rhythm, assess for MI, QT interval, epsilon wave (ARVD), Brugada pattern, pre-excitation
  • Echo: Assess EF, wall motion (scar), structural disease
  • Coronary angiography: If concern for ischemia/MI as trigger
  • Cardiac MRI: Assess for scar, infiltrative disease (sarcoid, ARVD), myocarditis
  • EP study: If recurrent VT, to map and potentially ablate VT circuit

Identify Triggers/Causes:

  • Ischemia/MI: Most common. VT/VF in first 48hr of MI is electrical instability. VT >48hr post-MI is scar-related
  • Cardiomyopathy: Low EF (<35%) is strongest predictor of sudden death
  • Electrolytes: Hypokalemia, hypomagnesemia, hypocalcemia (prolong QT ‚Üí Torsades)
  • Medications: QT-prolonging drugs (antiarrhythmics, antipsychotics, fluoroquinolones, macrolides)
  • Genetic: Long QT syndrome, Brugada, ARVD, catecholaminergic polymorphic VT (CPVT), hypertrophic cardiomyopathy
  • Toxic: Cocaine, methamphetamine, digoxin toxicity
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Pulseless VT/VF (Cardiac Arrest - ACLS):

  • Immediate defibrillation: 200J biphasic (or 360J monophasic). Resume CPR immediately after shock
  • CPR: High-quality compressions (‚â•100/min, depth ‚â•2 inches), minimize interruptions
  • Epinephrine: 1mg IV/IO q3-5min
  • Amiodarone: 300mg IV/IO first dose, then 150mg if recurrent VF/VT
  • Treat reversible causes: H's and T's (Hypovolemia, Hypoxia, H‚Å∫ [acidosis], Hypo/Hyperkalemia, Hypothermia, Toxins, Tamponade, Tension pneumothorax, Thrombosis [coronary or pulmonary], Trauma)

Unstable VT (With Pulse, SBP <90, AMS, pulmonary edema):

  • Synchronized cardioversion: 100J, then 150J, 200J if unsuccessful. Sedate if conscious!
  • If polymorphic VT: Treat as VF (unsynchronized high-energy shock 200J)
  • Post-cardioversion: Amiodarone 150mg IV over 10min, then 1mg/min √ó 6hr, then 0.5mg/min

Stable Monomorphic VT (With Pulse, Hemodynamically Stable):

  • Amiodarone: 150mg IV over 10min (can repeat), then 1mg/min √ó 6hr, then 0.5mg/min √ó 18hr
  • Procainamide: Alternative if normal EF. 20-50mg/min IV (max 17mg/kg). Monitor QT and BP
  • Sotalol: Another alternative. 1.5mg/kg IV over 5min. Monitor QT
  • If refractory: Synchronized cardioversion (consider sedation)

Torsades de Pointes (Polymorphic VT with Long QT):

  • Magnesium sulfate: 2g IV over 5-20min (even if Mg normal). First-line. Stabilizes membrane
  • Correct electrolytes: K >4.5, Mg >2
  • Increase heart rate: Isoproterenol infusion (1-4mcg/min) or overdrive pacing (rate 100-120) to shorten QT
  • STOP QT-prolonging agents: Review medication list, discontinue offending drugs
  • If unstable: Unsynchronized defibrillation (as VF)

VT Storm (Recurrent VT, ‚â•3 episodes in 24hr):

  • Amiodarone infusion: Load 150mg, then continuous infusion
  • Œ≤-blockers: Metoprolol or esmolol (reduce sympathetic drive)
  • Sedation: Reduce sympathetic tone (propofol, benzodiazepines)
  • Correct triggers: Ischemia (revascularization), electrolytes, medications
  • Catheter ablation: Consider urgent EP study and ablation if refractory

Secondary Prevention (ICD Placement - Cardiology/EP Consult):

  • Class I Indications (Definite):
    • Cardiac arrest due to VF/VT not due to reversible cause
    • Spontaneous sustained VT with structural heart disease
    • Unexplained syncope with inducible VT/VF at EP study
  • Primary Prevention ICD: EF ‚â§35% despite optimal medical therapy ‚â•3 months (ischemic) or ‚â•3 months (non-ischemic), NYHA Class II-III
  • Wearable defibrillator (LifeVest): Bridge to ICD assessment if not ready for permanent device (e.g., waiting for EF reassessment, infection, etc)

Treating Underlying Causes:

  • Ischemia: Urgent PCI/CABG. VT/VF in acute MI is electrical, may not recur after revascularization
  • Electrolytes: K >4, Mg >2 (higher targets in setting of VT/VF)
  • Long QT syndrome: Œ≤-blockers (propranolol, nadolol). ICD if syncope/VT despite therapy
  • Brugada: ICD. No proven pharmacologic therapy. Avoid drugs that unmask (procainamide, flecainide)
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Wide complex tachycardia = VT until proven otherwise: Especially with structural heart disease. Treating VT as SVT can be fatal (e.g., adenosine in VT can cause VF). When in doubt, use amiodarone
  • AV dissociation proves VT: P waves and QRS complexes independent. Capture beats (narrow QRS when P captures ventricle) and fusion beats also diagnostic
  • Early post-MI VT/VF (first 48hr) doesn't need ICD: Electrical instability of ischemic myocardium. Treat with revascularization. VT >48hr post-MI is scar-related and needs ICD
  • Torsades = Magnesium first: Even if serum Mg normal. Increases heart rate to shorten QT
  • Never give adenosine for wide-complex tachycardia unless certain it's SVT: Can cause VF in VT, can precipitate AF with rapid conduction in WPW
  • ICD is NOT primary treatment: ICD prevents sudden death but doesn't treat VT. Still need optimal medical therapy (Œ≤-blockers, ACE-I, etc) and treatment of underlying cause
  • VT storm is an emergency: Recurrent shocks from ICD, severe sympathetic surge. Sedate, amiodarone, consider urgent ablation
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Pulseless VT/VF (post-ROSC)
  • Unstable VT (hypotension, AMS, pulmonary edema)
  • VT storm (recurrent episodes)
  • Post-cardioversion/defibrillation
  • Continuous antiarrhythmic infusions
  • Acute MI with ventricular arrhythmias
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Stable rhythm ‚â•24-48hr on telemetry
  • No recurrent VT/VF
  • Hemodynamically stable
  • Underlying cause identified and treated
  • If ICD candidate: Device placed or LifeVest prescribed
  • Optimal medical therapy initiated (Œ≤-blockers, etc)
  • Cardiology/EP follow-up arranged
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Sudden cardiac death (if not defibrillated), cardiogenic shock, hypoxic brain injury (post-arrest), recurrent VT/VF (VT storm), ICD shocks (painful, psychological impact)

Post-ICD: Inappropriate shocks (lead malfunction, SVT misdetected), infection (pocket or lead endocarditis), lead dislodgment, pneumothorax, psychological impact of living with ICD

Chronic: Recurrent VT (may need ablation), progression of heart failure, need for transplant, complications of underlying disease (CAD, cardiomyopathy)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

47. ACUTE LIMB ISCHEMIA

Vascular Emergency ‚Ä¢ 6 P's ‚Ä¢ Revascularization
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic 6 P's:

  • Pain: Severe, sudden onset. Often at rest
  • Pallor: White, mottled appearance
  • Pulselessness: Absent distal pulses (femoral, popliteal, DP, PT)
  • Paresthesias: Numbness, tingling. Early sign of nerve ischemia
  • Paralysis: Weakness, inability to move limb. LATE finding, indicates severe ischemia
  • Poikilothermia: Cool to touch compared to contralateral limb

Severity Classification (Rutherford):

  • Class I (Viable): No immediate threat. No sensory/motor deficit. Audible Doppler signals
  • Class IIA (Marginally threatened): Salvageable if promptly treated. Minimal sensory loss, no motor deficit. Inaudible Doppler
  • Class IIB (Immediately threatened): Salvageable with immediate revascularization. Sensory loss beyond toes, mild-moderate motor deficit. Inaudible Doppler
  • Class III (Irreversible): Major tissue loss inevitable. Profound sensory/motor deficit, rigor. Skin marbling. Amputation likely

Etiologies:

  • Embolism (40%): Sudden onset, no prior claudication. AF (cardiac source), MI (LV thrombus), endocarditis, paradoxical embolus. Typically lodges at arterial bifurcations
  • Thrombosis (40%): Progressive onset, history of claudication/PAD. Atherosclerotic disease with superimposed thrombosis
  • Trauma: Fracture, dislocation, penetrating injury, iatrogenic (catheterization)
  • Other: Aortic dissection, compartment syndrome, popliteal aneurysm thrombosis, hypercoagulable state
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Clinical Exam: Vascular exam (pulses, capillary refill, temperature, color), neurologic exam (sensation, motor function), compare to contralateral limb

Doppler Ultrasound: Bedside assessment of flow. Audible signals suggest viability. Inaudible signals in threatened/irreversible ischemia

Labs: CBC, BMP (creatinine - may worsen with revascularization/rhabdo), CK (rhabdomyolysis if elevated), lactate, coagulation studies, type & screen

ECG: Assess for AF (cardioembolic source), recent MI

Imaging (don't delay revascularization if Class IIB/III):

  • CT Angiography (CTA): Gold standard for anatomy. Identifies level of occlusion, collaterals, assess for aneurysm/dissection. Fast
  • Conventional angiography: Diagnostic + therapeutic (catheter-directed thrombolysis). Done in angio suite
  • Duplex ultrasound: If concern for DVT, popliteal aneurysm

Echocardiogram: If embolic source suspected (AF, LV thrombus, endocarditis, PFO)

Embolism vs Thrombosis Clues:

  • Embolism: Sudden onset, no prior symptoms, AF, normal contralateral pulses, emboli elsewhere
  • Thrombosis: Progressive symptoms, history of claudication, abnormal contralateral pulses, chronic PAD
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Immediate Management (All Patients):

  • URGENT Vascular Surgery Consult: Time = limb! Revascularization within 6 hours optimal
  • IV Heparin: Bolus 80 units/kg, then 18 units/kg/hr. Prevents propagation of thrombus/embolus. DO NOT delay for imaging
  • Analgesia: IV opioids for severe pain
  • IV fluids: Aggressive hydration (prevent contrast-induced nephropathy, prepare for reperfusion injury)
  • Keep limb in dependent position: Improves blood flow. Do NOT elevate
  • Keep warm: Room warm, but avoid direct heat to limb (can increase metabolic demand)
  • Avoid flexing knee: Can worsen ischemia by compressing popliteal artery

Revascularization Strategies (Vascular Surgery):

  • Catheter-directed thrombolysis: For Class I/IIA. Infuse tPA directly into thrombus. Takes 12-24hr. For less severe ischemia, or if patient not surgical candidate
  • Surgical thromboembolectomy: For Class IIB/III (immediate threat). Fogarty catheter to remove clot. Faster than thrombolysis
  • Bypass surgery: If severe underlying PAD, long-segment occlusion, or failed endovascular approach
  • Hybrid approach: Combination of surgical and endovascular techniques

Reperfusion Injury Prevention:

  • IV fluids: Aggressive hydration to maintain UOP >1mL/kg/hr (prevent myoglobin-induced AKI)
  • Bicarbonate: Consider alkalinizing urine if rhabdomyolysis (keeps myoglobin soluble)
  • Monitor electrolytes: Watch for hyperkalemia (reperfusion releases K from ischemic muscle)
  • Fasciotomy: If compartment syndrome develops (swelling, pain, palpable tension). Prophylactic if prolonged ischemia (>6hr)

Post-Revascularization Care:

  • Monitor for compartment syndrome: Severe pain (out of proportion), pain with passive stretch, tense compartments, paresthesias. SURGICAL EMERGENCY - fasciotomy
  • Continue anticoagulation: Heparin bridge to warfarin or DOAC (if embolic source). Lifelong if AF, LV thrombus
  • Antiplatelet: ASA + P2Y12 inhibitor (clopidogrel) if thrombotic/PAD
  • Statin: High-intensity (PAD management)
  • Monitor CK, potassium, creatinine: For rhabdomyolysis and AKI

If Irreversible (Class III):

  • Amputation: To prevent systemic complications (rhabdomyolysis, sepsis from necrotic tissue)
  • Supportive care: Aggressive hydration, treat hyperkalemia, monitor for systemic toxicity
  • Do NOT revascularize: Reperfusion of necrotic tissue releases toxins (K, myoglobin, lactate) ‚Üí systemic collapse
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • "Time is limb": Revascularization within 6 hours gives best outcomes. Muscle tolerates 4-6hr ischemia, nerves 2-4hr. Paralysis = LATE finding, needs emergent revascularization
  • Heparin first, imaging second: Start heparin immediately based on clinical diagnosis. Don't delay for imaging confirmation
  • Paralysis is bad: Motor deficit indicates advanced ischemia (nerve damage). Urgent revascularization needed. Sensation goes before motor
  • Compartment syndrome after revascularization: Reperfusion causes swelling. High index of suspicion. Pain out of proportion, pain with passive stretch. Don't wait for pulselessness or pressure measurement - clinical diagnosis!
  • Blue toe syndrome: Emboli to digital arteries from proximal atherosclerotic plaque. Still palpable pulses. Suggests proximal source (aorta, iliac, popliteal aneurysm)
  • Don't revascularize Class III: Reperfusion of necrotic tissue causes systemic toxicity (hyperkalemia, myoglobin ‚Üí cardiac arrest, AKI). Amputation is life-saving
  • Check for bilateral involvement: Emboli can lodge in multiple locations. Assess contralateral limb, visceral arteries
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Class IIB/III (threatened or irreversible ischemia)
  • Hemodynamic instability
  • Post-revascularization (monitor for reperfusion injury)
  • Rhabdomyolysis (CK >5000, rising creatinine)
  • Severe hyperkalemia
  • Compartment syndrome requiring fasciotomy
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Successful revascularization with palpable pulses
  • No signs of compartment syndrome
  • Stable renal function (no rhabdo-induced AKI)
  • Electrolytes normalized
  • On appropriate anticoagulation/antiplatelet
  • Vascular surgery follow-up arranged
  • If embolic: Cardiology follow-up, address source (AF, LV thrombus)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Limb loss (amputation 10-30% depending on severity), compartment syndrome (post-reperfusion swelling), rhabdomyolysis ‚Üí AKI, hyperkalemia ‚Üí cardiac arrest, sepsis from necrotic tissue, death (15-20% in-hospital mortality)

Post-reperfusion: Compartment syndrome, reperfusion injury (release of toxins ‚Üí systemic inflammation), AKI (myoglobin), cardiac arrhythmias (hyperkalemia), recurrent thrombosis/embolism

Chronic: Chronic limb ischemia (incomplete revascularization), nerve damage (foot drop from peroneal nerve), chronic pain, graft/stent thrombosis, recurrent events if embolic source not addressed

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

48. DEEP VEIN THROMBOSIS (DVT)

Lower Extremity ‚Ä¢ Wells Criteria ‚Ä¢ Anticoagulation
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic: Unilateral leg swelling, pain, warmth, erythema. Calf tenderness, palpable cord. Homan's sign (pain with dorsiflexion) unreliable

Location:

  • Proximal DVT: Popliteal, femoral, iliac veins. Higher risk for PE (50% without treatment)
  • Distal DVT: Calf veins (tibial, peroneal). Lower PE risk (~5%), but 20% propagate proximally
  • Upper extremity DVT: Subclavian, axillary veins. Often related to central line, PICC, pacemaker lead. Can still embolize to lungs

Special Presentations:

  • Phlegmasia cerulea dolens: Massive ileofemoral DVT ‚Üí entire leg swollen, cyanotic, painful. Venous gangrene risk. EMERGENCY
  • Bilateral leg swelling: Think IVC thrombosis, pelvic mass, or bilateral DVT (rare)
  • Asymptomatic: 50% of DVT discovered incidentally or when presents as PE

Risk Factors (Virchow's Triad - Stasis, Hypercoagulability, Endothelial Injury):

  • Surgery: Especially orthopedic (hip/knee replacement), pelvic, abdominal
  • Immobility: Prolonged bed rest, long flights (>6hr), paralysis, casting
  • Malignancy: Pancreas, lung, GI, ovarian. Hypercoagulable state (Trousseau syndrome)
  • Hypercoagulable states: Factor V Leiden, prothrombin G20210A, antiphospholipid syndrome, protein C/S deficiency
  • Hormonal: Pregnancy, OCP, HRT, postpartum (6 weeks)
  • Prior VTE: 30% recurrence rate off anticoagulation
  • Other: CHF, obesity, smoking, central venous catheter, IBD, nephrotic syndrome
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Clinical Prediction (Wells Criteria for DVT):

  • Active cancer (+1)
  • Paralysis, paresis, or recent plaster immobilization (+1)
  • Recently bedridden >3 days or major surgery within 12 weeks (+1)
  • Localized tenderness along deep venous system (+1)
  • Entire leg swollen (+1)
  • Calf swelling >3cm compared to other leg (+1)
  • Pitting edema in symptomatic leg (+1)
  • Collateral superficial veins (+1)
  • Previously documented DVT (+1)
  • Alternative diagnosis as likely or more likely than DVT (-2)
  • Interpretation: ‚â§0 = low prob (3% DVT), 1-2 = moderate (17%), ‚â•3 = high (75%)

D-dimer:

  • Use: Rule-out test if low/moderate probability. High sensitivity (>95%), low specificity
  • Negative D-dimer + low/moderate Wells: DVT excluded, no further testing needed
  • Elevated D-dimer OR high Wells: Proceed to imaging
  • False positives: Pregnancy, malignancy, surgery, infection, advanced age, hospitalization

Imaging:

  • Compression ultrasound with Doppler: GOLD STANDARD. Sensitivity/specificity >95% for proximal DVT. Non-compressible vein = thrombus. Less sensitive for calf veins (70%)
  • Repeat ultrasound: If negative but high suspicion, repeat in 1 week (may miss early thrombus or calf DVT that propagates)
  • CT/MR venography: If US non-diagnostic or concern for pelvic/IVC thrombus
  • Venography: Rarely needed. Gold standard but invasive

Additional Labs:

  • CBC, BMP, PT/PTT: Baseline before anticoagulation
  • Consider hypercoagulable workup: If unprovoked DVT, age <50, recurrent VTE, strong family history, unusual site (mesenteric, cerebral). Test AFTER acute event treated (factor levels affected by acute clot)
  • Malignancy screening: Age-appropriate cancer screening if unprovoked DVT (occult malignancy in 10%)
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Anticoagulation (Start ASAP if high clinical suspicion, don't wait for imaging):

  • First-line: DOACs (Direct Oral Anticoagulants)
    • Apixaban: 10mg BID √ó 7 days, then 5mg BID (no bridging needed)
    • Rivaroxaban: 15mg BID √ó 21 days, then 20mg daily (no bridging needed)
    • Dabigatran: Requires 5-day heparin/LMWH lead-in, then 150mg BID
    • Edoxaban: Requires 5-day heparin/LMWH lead-in, then 60mg daily
  • Advantages DOACs: No monitoring, fixed dosing, rapid onset, lower bleeding risk than warfarin
  • Avoid DOACs if: CrCl <30, mechanical valve, antiphospholipid syndrome (warfarin preferred), pregnancy (use LMWH)

Alternative Anticoagulation:

  • LMWH (Enoxaparin): 1mg/kg SQ BID or 1.5mg/kg daily. Bridge to warfarin or use as monotherapy (cancer-associated VTE). Safe in renal insufficiency (dose-adjust if CrCl <30)
  • Unfractionated heparin: IV bolus 80 units/kg, then 18 units/kg/hr. Use if CrCl <30, high bleeding risk, need for procedures (shorter half-life). Bridge to warfarin
  • Warfarin: Start with LMWH/heparin bridge. Goal INR 2-3. Requires frequent monitoring. Use if DOAC contraindicated or cost issue
  • Fondaparinux: Weight-based SQ dosing. Bridge to warfarin. Alternative if HIT

Duration of Anticoagulation:

  • Provoked DVT (surgery, trauma, temporary risk): 3 months minimum
  • Unprovoked DVT: Minimum 3 months, consider indefinite (weigh bleeding risk vs recurrence risk)
  • Cancer-associated DVT: Minimum 3-6 months, often indefinite while cancer active. LMWH preferred over warfarin/DOACs
  • Recurrent VTE: Indefinite anticoagulation
  • Thrombophilia (Factor V Leiden, etc): 3 months if heterozygous + provoked. Consider indefinite if homozygous or unprovoked

Isolated Distal (Calf) DVT:

  • Symptomatic: Anticoagulate √ó 3 months (same as proximal)
  • Asymptomatic: Options include anticoagulation OR serial imaging (repeat US in 1 week) to monitor for propagation

IVC Filter:

  • Indications (rare): Absolute contraindication to anticoagulation (active bleeding), recurrent PE despite therapeutic anticoagulation, extensive proximal DVT with limited cardiopulmonary reserve
  • Retrievable filters: Remove when safe to anticoagulate
  • Complications: Filter thrombosis, IVC thrombosis, migration, perforation

Catheter-Directed Thrombolysis:

  • Consider for: Phlegmasia cerulea dolens, extensive ileofemoral DVT in young patient with good functional status, limb-threatening ischemia
  • Goal: Prevent post-thrombotic syndrome, preserve valve function
  • NOT routine: Increased bleeding risk, no mortality benefit

Adjunctive Measures:

  • Compression stockings: Controversial. May reduce post-thrombotic syndrome. Knee-high 30-40mmHg for 2 years
  • Early mobilization: Safe and encouraged (does NOT increase PE risk). Bed rest is OUTDATED
  • Leg elevation: When resting, reduces swelling
  • Analgesia: NSAIDs or acetaminophen for pain
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Don't wait for imaging if high suspicion: Start anticoagulation empirically while arranging ultrasound. DVT can propagate/embolize quickly
  • D-dimer is a rule-out test: Negative D-dimer + low probability = DVT excluded. Don't order if high probability (always positive, not helpful). Specificity decreases with age, hospitalization
  • Homan's sign is useless: Sensitivity and specificity both ~50%. Don't rely on it
  • DOACs are first-line: Unless contraindication (severe renal insufficiency, mechanical valve, APS, pregnancy). No bridge needed with apixaban/rivaroxaban
  • Cancer + VTE = LMWH: LMWH (enoxaparin) superior to warfarin for cancer-associated thrombosis. Lower recurrence rate
  • Early mobilization is safe: Bed rest does NOT prevent PE and increases risk of complications (pneumonia, deconditioning). Walk!
  • Post-thrombotic syndrome: Chronic leg pain, swelling, skin changes (hyperpigmentation, ulceration) in 20-50% of DVT patients. Caused by venous valvular incompetence from clot damage
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Phlegmasia cerulea dolens (limb-threatening)
  • Concurrent PE with hemodynamic instability
  • Need for thrombolysis
  • Bleeding on anticoagulation
  • Bilateral extensive DVT
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Proximal DVT: Anticoagulation initiated, stable on oral regimen or LMWH bridge arranged
  • Distal DVT: Anticoagulation started OR serial imaging plan arranged
  • No evidence of PE (or stable PE)
  • Pain controlled
  • Able to ambulate
  • Patient education on anticoagulation, bleeding precautions
  • Follow-up arranged (anticoagulation clinic, hematology if unprovoked/recurrent)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Pulmonary embolism (most feared, 50% risk if untreated proximal DVT), phlegmasia cerulea dolens (venous gangrene), compartment syndrome (rare), propagation to IVC

Treatment-related: Bleeding (major bleeding 1-3%/year on anticoagulation), HIT (with heparin), recurrent VTE despite anticoagulation

Chronic: Post-thrombotic syndrome (20-50%, chronic leg pain/swelling/ulceration), recurrent DVT (30% over 10 years if unprovoked), chronic thromboembolic disease

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

49. PLEURAL EFFUSION

Transudate vs Exudate ‚Ä¢ Light's Criteria ‚Ä¢ Thoracentesis
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic: Dyspnea (most common), pleuritic chest pain, cough. May be asymptomatic if small

Physical Exam: Decreased breath sounds, dullness to percussion, decreased tactile fremitus. Tracheal deviation away from effusion if massive

Severity:

  • Small: <300mL. Often asymptomatic. Blunting of costophrenic angle on CXR
  • Moderate: 300-1000mL. Dyspnea on exertion. Visible on CXR (meniscus sign)
  • Large: >1000mL. Dyspnea at rest. Occupies significant hemithorax
  • Massive: Complete white-out of hemithorax. Severe respiratory distress

Common Etiologies:

  • Transudates (‚Üìoncotic pressure or ‚Üëhydrostatic pressure): CHF (most common overall), cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome, PE
  • Exudates (inflammation/infection): Pneumonia (parapneumonic), malignancy, TB, PE, pancreatitis, esophageal rupture, autoimmune (lupus, RA)
  • Hemorrhagic: Trauma, malignancy, PE, post-procedure
  • Chylous: Lymphatic disruption (thoracic duct injury, malignancy, trauma)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Imaging:

  • CXR: Blunting of costophrenic angle (>200mL), meniscus sign. Lateral decubitus view shows layering of free-flowing effusion
  • Ultrasound: More sensitive than CXR. Detects small effusions (50mL). Guides thoracentesis (reduces pneumothorax risk)
  • CT chest: Distinguishes pleural fluid from consolidation, identifies loculations, evaluates for underlying lung pathology (malignancy, pneumonia, PE)

Thoracentesis Indications:

  • Diagnostic: New effusion of unknown etiology, >10mm on lateral decubitus CXR or US
  • Therapeutic: Symptomatic dyspnea from large effusion
  • Exceptions (don't tap): Clear CHF exacerbation with symmetric bilateral effusions responding to diuresis, small effusions <10mm

Pleural Fluid Analysis:

  • Appearance: Straw-colored (transudate/exudate), bloody (malignancy, PE, trauma), turbid/purulent (empyema), milky (chylothorax)
  • Cell count with differential: WBC >10,000 suggests infection. Neutrophils (bacterial), lymphocytes (TB, malignancy)
  • Chemistry: Protein, LDH, glucose, pH (send in heparinized syringe on ice)
  • Microbiology: Gram stain, culture (aerobic, anaerobic), AFB smear and culture if TB suspected
  • Cytology: If malignancy suspected. Sensitivity 60-70%, higher with repeated taps
  • Additional tests (if indicated): Triglycerides (>110mg/dL = chylothorax), amylase (pancreatitis, esophageal rupture), ADA (>40 U/L suggests TB), pH (empyema <7.2)

Light's Criteria (Transudate vs Exudate):

  • Exudate if ANY of following:
    • Pleural fluid protein / Serum protein >0.5
    • Pleural fluid LDH / Serum LDH >0.6
    • Pleural fluid LDH > 2/3 upper limit of normal serum LDH
  • Transudate: None of above criteria met
  • Sensitivity/Specificity: 98% for identifying exudates. Occasionally misclassifies diuretic-treated CHF as exudate
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Treat Underlying Cause:

  • CHF: Diuretics, afterload reduction. Effusions typically resolve with treatment
  • Pneumonia (parapneumonic effusion): Antibiotics. If uncomplicated (pH >7.2, glucose >60, LDH <1000, no bacteria), monitor. If complicated, needs drainage
  • Empyema: Pus in pleural space. Chest tube drainage + antibiotics. Criteria: gross pus, positive gram stain/culture, pH <7.2, glucose <60, LDH >1000
  • Malignant effusion: Recurrent, often large. Therapeutic thoracentesis for symptoms. Consider pleurodesis or indwelling pleural catheter for recurrent effusions
  • TB effusion: Lymphocyte-predominant, ADA >40. Treat TB with RIPE regimen. May need steroid taper to prevent pleural thickening

Therapeutic Thoracentesis:

  • Volume: Remove up to 1-1.5L in one sitting. Stop if chest pain, cough (re-expansion pulmonary edema), or if chest pain develops
  • Ultrasound-guided: Preferred (reduces pneumothorax risk by 50%)
  • Contraindications: Coagulopathy (relative, correct if possible), mechanical ventilation (higher risk), small effusion (<10mm)
  • Post-procedure CXR: Only if symptomatic (chest pain, dyspnea, cough). Routine CXR not needed if US-guided and uncomplicated

Chest Tube Drainage:

  • Indications: Empyema, complicated parapneumonic effusion, hemothorax, recurrent symptomatic malignant effusion not amenable to pleurodesis
  • Size: Large-bore (28-32Fr) for empyema/hemothorax. Small-bore (10-14Fr) adequate for most other effusions
  • Fibrinolytics: Consider for loculated empyema/complicated parapneumonic (tPA + DNase intrapleural). Improves drainage, reduces need for surgery

Pleurodesis (For Recurrent Malignant Effusions):

  • Chemical: Talc (most effective, 90% success), doxycycline, bleomycin. Instilled via chest tube or at thoracoscopy
  • Criteria: Lung fully expanded (trapped lung won't pleurodese), life expectancy >3 months
  • Indwelling pleural catheter (PleurX): Alternative to pleurodesis. Allows outpatient drainage. Better for trapped lung

Specific Etiologies:

  • Chylothorax: Milky appearance, triglycerides >110. Conservative management (low-fat diet, MCT oil) or thoracic duct ligation/embolization if high output
  • Hemothorax: Trauma, malignancy, PE. Chest tube drainage. Surgery if >1500mL immediate or >200mL/hr continued bleeding
  • Post-CABG effusion: Common (30-60%). Usually small, resolves spontaneously. Tap if large/symptomatic
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Light's Criteria is 98% sensitive: If exudate criteria met, it's almost certainly an exudate. If transudate, look for CHF/cirrhosis/nephrotic syndrome
  • Bilateral effusions = CHF until proven otherwise: If symmetric and patient has CHF, can treat without tapping. If asymmetric or not responding to diuresis, tap
  • Yellow nail syndrome: Lymphedema + yellow nails + pleural effusion. Rare cause of exudative effusion
  • Empyema needs drainage: Antibiotics alone are insufficient. Chest tube + antibiotics. If loculated, consider fibrinolytics or VATS
  • Malignant effusion = poor prognosis: Usually incurable, median survival 4-6 months. Focus on palliation (pleurodesis or indwelling catheter)
