  • Re-expansion pulmonary edema: Rare complication of large-volume thoracentesis (>1.5L). Presents with cough, chest tightness, hypoxia. Usually self-limited
  • Post-thoracentesis CXR only if symptomatic: Routine CXR no longer recommended if uncomplicated US-guided procedure. Check only if chest pain, dyspnea, or cough
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Empyema with sepsis/septic shock
  • Massive effusion with respiratory failure
  • Hemothorax (ongoing bleeding, shock)
  • Post-thoracentesis pneumothorax requiring chest tube
  • Re-expansion pulmonary edema
  • Chylothorax with high output (>1L/day)
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Effusion drained or small/stable
  • No respiratory distress
  • Underlying cause identified and treated (or plan in place)
  • If empyema: Chest tube removed, afebrile, stable WBC
  • If malignant: Pleurodesis done or PleurX catheter placed with patient/family education
  • Follow-up arranged (repeat imaging in 4-6 weeks if needed)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Thoracentesis complications: Pneumothorax (3-15%, lower with US guidance), bleeding (rare, <1%), re-expansion pulmonary edema (if >1.5L removed), pain, infection (rare), splenic/liver laceration (rare)

Chest tube complications: Bleeding, infection (empyema), lung injury, malposition, persistent air leak, failure to drain (if loculated)

Chronic: Pleural thickening (TB, empyema), trapped lung (malignancy, chronic effusion), recurrent effusion (malignancy, CHF), chronic dyspnea

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

50. PNEUMOTHORAX/TENSION PNEUMOTHORAX

Primary ‚Ä¢ Secondary ‚Ä¢ Tension Physiology
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Simple Pneumothorax: Sudden-onset pleuritic chest pain, dyspnea. May be asymptomatic if small

Physical Exam: Decreased breath sounds, hyperresonance to percussion, decreased tactile fremitus on affected side

Tension Pneumothorax (EMERGENCY): Severe respiratory distress, hypotension, shock, tracheal deviation away from affected side, JVD, hypoxia, tachycardia. CLINICAL DIAGNOSIS - don't wait for imaging!

Types:

  • Primary spontaneous: Tall, thin, young males (20s-30s). Smoking. Rupture of apical blebs. No underlying lung disease
  • Secondary spontaneous: Underlying lung disease (COPD, asthma, cystic fibrosis, PCP pneumonia, TB, lung cancer). Higher risk of complications
  • Traumatic: Blunt or penetrating chest trauma, rib fracture with lung puncture
  • Iatrogenic: Central line placement (subclavian most common), thoracentesis, lung biopsy, mechanical ventilation (barotrauma), CPR
  • Catamenial: Occurs with menstruation. Associated with endometriosis (thoracic endometrial implants). Recurrent, right-sided, within 72hr of menses

Size Classification:

  • Small: <2cm rim of air between lung and chest wall at hilum level
  • Large: ‚â•2cm rim of air. Or >50% hemithorax volume
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Imaging:

  • CXR (upright PA): Visceral pleural line separated from chest wall. No lung markings beyond line. Best seen at apex
  • Expiratory CXR: May make small pneumothorax more visible (lung gets smaller, pneumothorax stays same). Not routinely needed
  • CT chest: Most sensitive. Identifies small pneumothorax missed on CXR, evaluates for underlying lung disease, detects blebs/bullae
  • Ultrasound: Absence of lung sliding, absence of B-lines, lung point sign (transition from normal to pneumothorax). Useful in trauma/ICU setting
  • DO NOT delay treatment for imaging if tension pneumothorax suspected!

ABG: If significant dyspnea or underlying lung disease. May show hypoxemia, respiratory alkalosis (hyperventilation)

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Tension Pneumothorax (EMERGENCY - Life-threatening):

  • Immediate needle decompression: BEFORE imaging! 14-16G angiocath in 2nd intercostal space, midclavicular line (or 4th-5th ICS, anterior axillary line). Remove needle, leave catheter. Rush of air confirms diagnosis
  • Follow with chest tube: Definitive management. Large-bore (28-32Fr)
  • DO NOT wait for CXR if tension pneumothorax suspected clinically!

Primary Spontaneous Pneumothorax (No underlying lung disease):

  • Small (<2cm) + minimal symptoms: Observation + supplemental O‚ÇÇ (increases reabsorption 4√ó). Repeat CXR in 6hr and 24hr. Discharge if stable and reliable
  • Large (‚â•2cm) OR symptomatic: Aspiration first attempt. If successful (lung expanded, <2cm residual), observe and repeat CXR. If fails or re-expands, chest tube
  • Aspiration technique: 16-18G catheter, 2nd ICS MCL or 4th-5th ICS AAL. Aspirate up to 2.5L. If lung expands and patient improved, can observe without tube

Secondary Spontaneous Pneumothorax (Underlying lung disease - COPD, asthma, etc):

  • Small (<1cm at hilum) + minimal symptoms: Observation + supplemental O‚ÇÇ. Admit for 24hr observation (higher risk of complications)
  • Small (1-2cm) OR any symptoms: Aspiration attempt. If successful, admit for observation
  • Large (‚â•2cm) OR failed aspiration: Chest tube. Lower threshold for tube placement than primary (worse lung reserve, higher complication rate)
  • Note: Secondary pneumothorax has higher morbidity/mortality. Lower threshold for intervention

Chest Tube Placement:

  • Size: Small-bore (12-14Fr) adequate for simple pneumothorax. Large-bore (28-32Fr) for hemothorax, tension PTX, or if on mechanical ventilation
  • Location: 4th-5th ICS, anterior to mid-axillary line ("safe triangle"). Avoid 2nd ICS due to vessels
  • Suction: -20cmH‚ÇÇO. Helps lung re-expand
  • Removal criteria: No air leak √ó 24hr, lung fully expanded on CXR, patient off suction (water seal trial)

Indications for Surgery (VATS/Thoracotomy):

  • Persistent air leak: >5-7 days despite chest tube
  • Recurrent ipsilateral: 2nd episode on same side (50% recurrence after 1st PTX, 80% after 2nd)
  • Bilateral: Simultaneous or history of contralateral PTX
  • First episode in high-risk occupation: Pilots, divers (cannot have recurrence risk)
  • Hemopneumothorax: Significant bleeding component
  • Procedure: Pleurodesis (mechanical or chemical) ¬± bleb resection

Special Scenarios:

  • Mechanical ventilation: Always place chest tube (high risk of tension). Increase PEEP cautiously
  • Iatrogenic (post-procedure): Small and asymptomatic often resolve spontaneously with observation. Larger or symptomatic need aspiration/tube
  • Air travel: Contraindicated until fully resolved. Pressure changes at altitude can expand pneumothorax
  • Catamenial: Hormonal suppression (OCPs, GnRH agonists). May need thoracoscopy to resect endometrial implants. Pleurodesis for recurrent

Discharge Planning:

  • Activity: No strenuous activity, no flying, no diving until cleared (minimum 2 weeks after full resolution)
  • Smoking cessation: Decreases recurrence risk 10-fold. CRITICAL
  • Return precautions: Worsening dyspnea, chest pain, signs of recurrence
  • Follow-up CXR: 2-4 weeks to confirm full resolution
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Tension pneumothorax is CLINICAL diagnosis: Respiratory distress + hypotension + unilateral absent breath sounds + tracheal deviation. Needle decompress immediately, don't wait for CXR!
  • Not all pneumothorax has tracheal deviation: Tracheal deviation is a late finding and may be absent even in tension PTX. Don't rely on it!
  • Supplemental O‚ÇÇ helps reabsorption: High-flow O‚ÇÇ increases nitrogen gradient, speeds reabsorption by 4√ó. Useful in observation strategy
  • Recurrence rates: 30% after 1st, 50% after 2nd primary spontaneous PTX. Consider pleurodesis after 2nd episode
  • Size matters less than symptoms: Symptomatic small pneumothorax needs intervention. Asymptomatic large pneumothorax in young healthy person might be observed
  • Secondary PTX is higher risk: Poor lung reserve (COPD, asthma). Lower threshold for chest tube. Higher morbidity
  • Heimlich valve: One-way flutter valve on chest tube allows outpatient management in stable patients. Allows air out but not in
  • Re-expansion pulmonary edema: Rare complication after rapid lung re-expansion (more common if pneumothorax >3 days or aggressive suction). Usually self-limited
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Tension pneumothorax (even after decompression - post-resuscitation care)
  • Respiratory failure requiring mechanical ventilation
  • Hemodynamic instability
  • Large pneumothorax with significant underlying lung disease (COPD, severe asthma)
  • Bilateral pneumothorax
  • Persistent air leak despite chest tube
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Pneumothorax resolved or stable/small (<1-2cm)
  • No respiratory distress, O‚ÇÇ sat >90% on room air
  • If chest tube: Removed after no air leak √ó 24hr and lung fully expanded
  • Reliable for follow-up and understands return precautions
  • Smoking cessation counseling provided (critical!)
  • Activity restrictions explained (no flying, no diving, no strenuous activity)
  • Follow-up CXR arranged in 2-4 weeks
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Tension pneumothorax (cardiovascular collapse if not decompressed), respiratory failure, hemopneumothorax (bleeding into pleural space), re-expansion pulmonary edema (post-drainage)

Treatment-related: Chest tube complications (bleeding, infection, malposition, persistent air leak, pain), failed re-expansion (trapped lung, persistent air leak needing VATS)

Chronic: Recurrent pneumothorax (30-50% risk, higher without pleurodesis), chronic air leak, pleural thickening, chronic dyspnea (if underlying lung disease worsened)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

51. TRANSIENT ISCHEMIC ATTACK (TIA)

Stroke Warning ‚Ä¢ ABCD¬≤ Score ‚Ä¢ Urgent Workup
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Definition: Transient episode of neurologic dysfunction from focal brain, spinal cord, or retinal ischemia WITHOUT acute infarction. Symptoms resolve within 24hr (most <1hr)

Classic Symptoms (sudden onset, focal):

  • Motor: Unilateral weakness (face, arm, leg). Arm drift, facial droop
  • Sensory: Unilateral numbness, paresthesias
  • Speech: Aphasia (expressive or receptive), dysarthria (slurred speech)
  • Visual: Monocular vision loss (amaurosis fugax - retinal TIA), hemianopia, diplopia
  • Ataxia: Unsteady gait, incoordination (posterior circulation)
  • Vertigo: With other posterior circulation symptoms (NOT isolated vertigo)

TIA Mimics (NOT TIA): Seizure with Todd's paralysis, migraine with aura, hypoglycemia, syncope, peripheral vertigo alone, isolated sensory symptoms without other deficits

High-Risk Features:

  • Multiple TIAs in short period (crescendo TIAs)
  • Symptoms >1 hour
  • Carotid stenosis >50%
  • AF or cardioembolic source
  • ABCD¬≤ score ‚â•4
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Urgent Imaging (within 24hr, ideally emergently):

  • Brain MRI with DWI: GOLD STANDARD. Detects acute infarction in 30-50% of "TIAs". DWI-positive = stroke, not TIA
  • CT head (if MRI unavailable): Rule out hemorrhage, large stroke. Less sensitive for acute ischemia
  • CTA or MRA head/neck: Assess for large vessel stenosis/occlusion (carotid, vertebral, intracranial). Identify surgical candidates

Vascular Studies:

  • Carotid Doppler ultrasound: Screen for carotid stenosis. If >50% stenosis, confirm with CTA/MRA
  • Consider CTA chest: If concern for aortic arch atheroma

Cardiac Workup:

  • ECG: Assess for AF (most important modifiable risk factor for cardioembolic stroke)
  • Telemetry: 24hr minimum. Consider prolonged monitoring (30-day event monitor) if no AF found and high suspicion
  • Transthoracic echo (TTE): Assess for cardioembolic source (LV thrombus, valvular disease, reduced EF)
  • TEE: If high suspicion for cardioembolic source and TTE negative. Better visualization of LA appendage (thrombus), PFO, aortic arch atheroma

Labs: CBC, BMP, lipid panel, HbA1c, PT/PTT (if anticoagulation planned), ESR (if vasculitis concern), hypercoagulable workup (if age <50, unprovoked, recurrent)

ABCD¬≤ Score (Stroke Risk Stratification):

  • Age ‚â•60: 1 point
  • BP ‚â•140/90: 1 point
  • Clinical features: Unilateral weakness (2 points), Speech impairment without weakness (1 point)
  • Duration: ‚â•60min (2 points), 10-59min (1 point)
  • Diabetes: 1 point
  • Score 0-3: Low risk (1% 2-day stroke risk). Outpatient workup possible if reliable
  • Score 4-5: Moderate risk (4% 2-day stroke risk). Admit for expedited workup
  • Score 6-7: High risk (8% 2-day stroke risk). Admit for urgent workup
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Immediate Management (Goal: Prevent stroke within 48-90 days):

  • Aspirin: 325mg loading dose immediately (if not already on antiplatelet). Reduces early stroke risk by 80%
  • Dual antiplatelet therapy (DAPT): Aspirin 81mg + clopidogrel 75mg daily √ó 21 days, then aspirin alone. Reduces stroke risk by 32% compared to aspirin alone (POINT/CHANCE trials). Start within 24hr of symptom onset
  • BP management: Goal <140/90 long-term. Avoid aggressive lowering in acute phase unless hypertensive emergency
  • Statin: High-intensity (atorvastatin 40-80mg or rosuvastatin 20-40mg). Start immediately regardless of LDL. Reduces recurrent stroke

Etiology-Specific Treatment:

  • Atrial fibrillation: Anticoagulation (DOAC preferred: apixaban, rivaroxaban, edoxaban, dabigatran). Start within 4-14 days depending on stroke risk. CHA‚ÇÇDS‚ÇÇ-VASc score guides decision
  • Carotid stenosis 70-99% (symptomatic): Urgent CEA (carotid endarterectomy) within 2 weeks. NNT=6 to prevent stroke. Consider CAS (carotid artery stenting) if high surgical risk
  • Carotid stenosis 50-69%: CEA beneficial (NNT=15). Consider if good surgical candidate
  • Carotid stenosis <50%: Medical management. No benefit from CEA
  • Intracranial stenosis: Aggressive medical management (dual antiplatelet √ó 90 days, statin, BP control). Stenting NOT superior to medical therapy
  • Cardioembolic (not AF): Antiplatelet vs anticoagulation depending on source. TEE to identify source
  • Cryptogenic (no identified cause): Aspirin. Consider extended cardiac monitoring for paroxysmal AF. PFO closure if large PFO + no other cause (controversial, age <60)

Risk Factor Modification (CRITICAL):

  • BP control: Goal <130/80 (ACC/AHA) or <140/90 (ESH/ESC). ACE-I or ARB preferred
  • Diabetes control: HbA1c <7%
  • Lipids: LDL <70mg/dL (consider <55 if very high risk)
  • Smoking cessation: Doubles stroke risk. Counsel + pharmacotherapy (varenicline, bupropion, NRT)
  • Exercise: 30min moderate activity 5√ó week
  • Diet: Mediterranean diet, low sodium
  • Weight loss: If overweight/obese

Admission vs Outpatient:

  • Admit: ABCD¬≤ ‚â•4, crescendo TIAs, within 48hr of symptom onset, unreliable for follow-up, unable to complete workup as outpatient
  • Outpatient: ABCD¬≤ <3, reliable patient, can complete MRI/vascular imaging/echo within 48hr, no high-risk features
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • TIA = stroke until proven otherwise: 30-50% of "TIAs" have infarction on DWI-MRI. If DWI-positive, it's a stroke, not TIA. Treat aggressively either way
  • Highest stroke risk is within 48hr: 5-10% have stroke within 90 days, half of those within 48hr. TIA is an EMERGENCY. Expedited workup prevents stroke
  • Dual antiplatelet (ASA + clopidogrel) for 21 days: POINT and CHANCE trials showed 32% relative risk reduction. Start within 24hr. Then continue aspirin alone
  • Amaurosis fugax = retinal TIA: Transient monocular blindness ("curtain coming down"). Ipsilateral carotid stenosis or emboli. Same stroke risk as hemispheric TIA
  • NOT all transient neuro symptoms are TIA: Isolated vertigo, isolated diplopia, isolated sensory symptoms = low likelihood. Need focal symptoms (motor/speech/visual field loss)
  • Carotid stenosis timing matters: Symptomatic stenosis 70-99% needs CEA within 2 weeks. Benefit diminishes after that. Asymptomatic stenosis has different indications
  • Extended cardiac monitoring increases AF detection: 30-day monitors detect AF in 10-20% of cryptogenic TIA/stroke. Consider if no cause found
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE/ADMIT IF:
  • ABCD¬≤ score ‚â•4 (moderate-high risk)
  • Crescendo TIAs (multiple in short period)
  • Symptoms occurred within 48hr
  • Carotid stenosis >50% (needs urgent evaluation for CEA)
  • Atrial fibrillation (needs anticoagulation)
  • Unable to complete urgent outpatient workup
  • Unreliable for follow-up
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Imaging complete (MRI/CTA or CT/carotid Doppler)
  • Cardiac evaluation done (ECG, telemetry ‚â•24hr, echo)
  • Started on appropriate therapy (antiplatelet or anticoagulation + statin)
  • If carotid stenosis >50%: Vascular surgery consult, CEA scheduled
  • Risk factors addressed (BP, lipids, diabetes)
  • Neurology follow-up arranged (within 1-2 weeks)
  • Patient educated on stroke warning signs
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Stroke (5-10% within 90 days, highest risk first 48hr), recurrent TIA (15-20% have another TIA), progression to stroke during evaluation

Procedural: CEA complications (stroke 2-3%, MI, cranial nerve injury, bleeding), CAS complications (stroke, dissection, access site complications)

Chronic: Recurrent stroke/TIA (if risk factors not controlled), bleeding on antiplatelets/anticoagulation, cognitive decline (vascular dementia), disability from eventual stroke if not treated aggressively

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

52. GUILLAIN-BARR√â SYNDROME (GBS)

Acute Inflammatory Demyelinating Polyneuropathy ‚Ä¢ Ascending Paralysis ‚Ä¢ IVIG/Plasmapheresis
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic Triad: (1) Progressive symmetric weakness, (2) Areflexia/hyporeflexia, (3) Symptoms progress over days-weeks (peak at 2-4 weeks)

Typical Presentation: Ascending symmetric weakness starting in legs ‚Üí arms ‚Üí bulbar ‚Üí respiratory muscles. Paresthesias in hands/feet (glove-stocking distribution). Preceding illness 1-3 weeks prior (URI, GI infection)

Motor Findings:

  • Weakness: Symmetric, ascending (legs before arms typically). Proximal and distal affected
  • Areflexia: Absent or markedly diminished deep tendon reflexes (hallmark finding)
  • Bulbar involvement: Facial weakness (bilateral), dysphagia, dysarthria (30-50%)
  • Respiratory failure: 20-30% require mechanical ventilation. Progressive weakness of diaphragm/intercostals

Sensory Findings: Paresthesias, numbness (glove-stocking). Pain (back, radicular) in 50%. Sensory exam often normal despite symptoms

Autonomic Dysfunction (60%): Tachycardia, bradycardia, arrhythmias, labile BP (hyper/hypotension), ileus, urinary retention, SIADH

Variants:

  • AIDP (Acute Inflammatory Demyelinating Polyneuropathy): Most common in US/Europe (90%). Classic ascending paralysis
  • AMAN/AMSAN (Axonal variants): More common in Asia. Axonal damage. Worse prognosis
  • Miller Fisher syndrome: Triad of ataxia, areflexia, ophthalmoplegia. Anti-GQ1b antibodies (90%)
  • Pharyngeal-cervical-brachial variant: Starts with bulbar/arm weakness

Triggers (preceding infection in 60-70%):

  • Campylobacter jejuni (30%): Most common. Diarrheal illness 1-3 weeks prior
  • Upper respiratory infections: CMV, EBV, Mycoplasma
  • Other: Zika virus, COVID-19, vaccines (rare, <1 per million), surgery, trauma
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Clinical Diagnosis (primarily): Brighton Criteria or Asbury & Cornblath Criteria. Diagnosis based on clinical features + CSF + EMG

Lumbar Puncture (CSF Analysis):

  • Albuminocytologic dissociation (CLASSIC): Elevated protein (>45mg/dL) with normal or mildly elevated WBC (<10 cells/¬µL)
  • Timing: Protein elevation may not appear until week 2. If early and normal, repeat LP in 1 week if high suspicion
  • WBC >50: Consider alternative diagnosis (infection, malignancy, sarcoid)

Electrodiagnostic Studies (EMG/NCS):

  • Findings: Prolonged distal latencies, slowed conduction velocities, conduction block, prolonged/absent F-waves (demyelination)
  • Timing: Abnormalities may not appear until 2 weeks. Early studies may be normal
  • Axonal variants (AMAN/AMSAN): Low-amplitude CMAPs with normal velocities

Bedside Respiratory Assessment (CRITICAL):

  • Vital capacity (VC): Measure q4-6hr if progressive. VC <20mL/kg or rapidly declining = high risk for intubation
  • Negative inspiratory force (NIF): <-30cmH‚ÇÇO = respiratory muscle weakness
  • Single-breath count: Unable to count to 20 in one breath = severe weakness
  • 20/30/40 rule: VC <20mL/kg, NIF >-30cmH‚ÇÇO, MIP <40cmH‚ÇÇO = likely need intubation

Labs: CBC, CMP, LFTs (baseline before IVIG), ABG (if respiratory concern), serology for infections (Campylobacter, CMV, EBV, Mycoplasma), anti-ganglioside antibodies (anti-GM1, anti-GQ1b - supportive but not required)

MRI spine: Consider to rule out mimics (cord compression, transverse myelitis). May show nerve root enhancement in GBS

Mimics to Rule Out: Spinal cord compression, transverse myelitis, myasthenia gravis, botulism, tick paralysis, acute intermittent porphyria, critical illness polyneuropathy, vasculitis, HIV, Lyme, West Nile virus

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Immunotherapy (Equally Effective - choose one):

  • IVIG (Intravenous Immunoglobulin): 0.4g/kg/day √ó 5 days (total 2g/kg). First-line in most centers. Easier to administer than plasmapheresis
  • Plasmapheresis (Plasma Exchange): 5 exchanges over 7-14 days (200-250mL/kg total). Equally effective as IVIG. Use if IVIG contraindicated (IgA deficiency) or failed
  • NO benefit to combination: IVIG + plasmapheresis NOT better than either alone
  • Timing: Start within 2 weeks of symptom onset for maximum benefit. Still beneficial up to 4 weeks
  • Steroids NOT beneficial: Corticosteroids do not work in GBS. Do NOT use

Respiratory Management (CRITICAL):

  • Monitor closely: Vital capacity, NIF q4-6hr if progressive weakness
  • Indications for intubation: VC <15-20mL/kg, rapidly declining VC, NIF >-30cmH‚ÇÇO, hypoxia, hypercapnia, inability to clear secretions, bulbar weakness with aspiration
  • Intubation considerations: Avoid succinylcholine (hyperkalemia risk in denervation). Use non-depolarizing paralytics
  • Ventilation strategy: Lung-protective ventilation. Avoid over-sedation (need to assess neuro status)
  • Tracheostomy: Consider if anticipated ventilation >2 weeks (many GBS patients)

Autonomic Management:

  • Cardiac monitoring: Telemetry for all patients. Risk of arrhythmias (brady, tachy, heart block)
  • BP lability: Avoid aggressive treatment of transient hyper/hypotension. Short-acting agents if needed. Fluid boluses for hypotension
  • Avoid: Œ≤-blockers (can worsen bradycardia), aggressive BP lowering (can precipitate hypotension)

Supportive Care:

  • DVT prophylaxis: SCDs + heparin/LMWH (high risk due to immobility)
  • GI: Proton pump inhibitor (stress ulcer prophylaxis), bowel regimen (ileus common)
  • Bladder: Foley catheter if urinary retention
  • Pain management: Gabapentin, pregabalin for neuropathic pain. Opioids if needed
  • Physical therapy: Early mobilization when stable. Prevent contractures
  • Nutrition: Consider NG/NJ tube if dysphagia. Assess swallow before PO
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Areflexia is the key finding: Absent reflexes + ascending weakness = GBS until proven otherwise. DTRs are lost early and universally
  • Respiratory failure is the biggest risk: 20-30% need intubation. Monitor VC closely. Don't wait for hypoxia/hypercapnia (late findings). Intubate early if declining
  • CSF protein may be normal early: Classic albuminocytologic dissociation may not appear until week 2. Repeat LP if high suspicion and initially normal
  • IVIG = Plasmapheresis in efficacy: Both equally effective. IVIG easier to give. Combination NOT better. Start within 2 weeks of symptom onset
  • Steroids don't work in GBS: Unlike most autoimmune conditions, corticosteroids are NOT beneficial and should NOT be used
  • Prognosis overall good but slow: 80-85% recover fully, but takes months-year. 5-10% permanent disability. 3-5% mortality (respiratory failure, autonomic dysfunction)
  • Campylobacter-associated GBS has worse prognosis: More likely to be axonal variant (AMAN). Slower recovery, more residual deficits
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • ALL GBS patients need ICU or stepdown with close monitoring
  • VC <20mL/kg or rapidly declining
  • NIF >-30cmH‚ÇÇO
  • Bulbar weakness (dysphagia, dysarthria)
  • Autonomic instability (arrhythmias, labile BP)
  • Rapid progression (can't walk ‚Üí respiratory failure in days)
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Respiratory status stable (if intubated, successfully extubated)
  • No autonomic instability √ó 72hr
  • Weakness plateaued or improving
  • Completed course of IVIG or plasmapheresis
  • Able to swallow safely (speech/swallow eval)
  • DVT prophylaxis in place
  • Inpatient rehab arranged (most need extensive PT/OT)
  • Neurology follow-up arranged
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Respiratory failure (20-30%, #1 cause of death), autonomic dysfunction (arrhythmias, labile BP, sudden death), aspiration pneumonia, DVT/PE (immobility), ileus, urinary retention, pressure ulcers

Treatment-related: IVIG (aseptic meningitis, hemolysis, thrombosis, renal failure), Plasmapheresis (hypotension, line infection, citrate toxicity, bleeding)

Chronic: Residual weakness (10-15% permanent disability), chronic neuropathic pain (30%), fatigue (persistent in 50%), relapse (3-5%, usually within 2 months - consider CIDP if relapses), CIDP (chronic inflammatory demyelinating polyneuropathy - GBS-like but chronic/relapsing)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

53. MYASTHENIA GRAVIS CRISIS

Neuromuscular Junction ‚Ä¢ Respiratory Failure ‚Ä¢ IVIG/PLEX
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Myasthenia Gravis (Baseline): Autoimmune disorder causing fluctuating muscle weakness from antibodies blocking acetylcholine receptors at neuromuscular junction. Weakness worsens with activity, improves with rest

Classic MG Symptoms:

  • Ocular (85% at presentation): Ptosis (drooping eyelids), diplopia (double vision). Often first symptoms
  • Bulbar (60%): Dysphagia, dysarthria, nasal speech, difficulty chewing
  • Limb weakness (50%): Proximal > distal. Arms > legs. Can't raise arms, difficulty climbing stairs
  • Neck weakness: Head drop, difficulty holding head up
  • Fatigable weakness: HALLMARK. Worse at end of day, after repeated activity. Improves with rest

Myasthenic Crisis (EMERGENCY): Severe worsening of MG leading to respiratory failure requiring intubation. 15-20% of MG patients experience crisis at least once

Crisis Presentation:

  • Respiratory failure: Dyspnea, tachypnea, inability to clear secretions, hypoxia, hypercapnia. Can progress rapidly
  • Bulbar crisis: Severe dysphagia, aspiration risk, inability to manage secretions
  • Generalized weakness: Unable to sit up, lift head, limb weakness

Crisis Triggers:

  • Infection (30-40%): Most common. Pneumonia, UTI, URI
  • Medications: Aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, macrolides, Œ≤-blockers, CCBs, statins, steroids (paradoxical worsening first 2 weeks), neuromuscular blockers
  • Surgery/anesthesia: Stress, medications
  • Thyroid dysfunction: Hyper or hypothyroidism
  • Pregnancy/postpartum: Hormonal changes
  • Medication non-compliance: Stopped pyridostigmine or immunosuppressants
  • Aspiration pneumonia: From bulbar weakness

Cholinergic Crisis (Rare): Overdose of acetylcholinesterase inhibitors (pyridostigmine). Weakness from EXCESS acetylcholine. Associated with SLUDGE symptoms (Salivation, Lacrimation, Urination, Defecation, GI upset, Emesis) + miosis, bronchorrhea, bradycardia

DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Bedside Respiratory Assessment (CRITICAL):

  • Vital capacity (VC): <15-20mL/kg = high risk for intubation. Monitor q2-4hr
  • Negative inspiratory force (NIF): >-30cmH‚ÇÇO = severe weakness
  • ABG: Hypercapnia (pCO‚ÇÇ >45) and hypoxia = impending respiratory failure. Don't wait for ABG to intubate
  • Single-breath count: Unable to count to 15-20 in one breath = severe weakness
  • Signs of impending failure: Tachypnea, accessory muscle use, paradoxical breathing, inability to cough or clear secretions

Myasthenic vs Cholinergic Crisis (Differentiate):

  • Clinical: Cholinergic has SLUDGE symptoms + fasciculations + miosis. Myasthenic does not
  • Edrophonium (Tensilon) test: Short-acting acetylcholinesterase inhibitor. Myasthenic crisis improves. Cholinergic crisis worsens. RARELY done (dangerous in crisis)
  • Practical approach: Hold pyridostigmine in crisis. Treat with IVIG/PLEX (works for both). Restart pyridostigmine when improving

Confirm MG Diagnosis (if not already diagnosed):

  • Antibodies: Anti-AChR (acetylcholine receptor) antibodies (80-85% sensitivity). Anti-MuSK antibodies (5-10% if AChR-negative). Anti-LRP4 (<5%)
  • Repetitive nerve stimulation (RNS): >10% decrement in CMAP amplitude with low-frequency (2-3Hz) stimulation. Sensitivity 50-75%
  • Single-fiber EMG: Most sensitive (95%). Shows increased jitter. Not widely available
  • Edrophonium test: Improves strength transiently (3-5min). Rarely used (can cause bradycardia, bronchospasm)

Labs: CBC (infection), CMP (electrolytes), ABG (respiratory status), CK (normal in MG, unlike muscular dystrophy), TSH (thyroid disease common in MG), troponin (stress from respiratory failure)

Imaging:

  • CT chest: Assess for thymoma (present in 10-15% of MG). Thymic hyperplasia in 60-70%
  • CXR: Rule out pneumonia (common trigger)

Search for Triggers: Blood cultures, UA/UCx, CXR, review medications, TSH

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Airway/Respiratory Management (Priority #1):

  • Intubation criteria: VC <15mL/kg, NIF >-30cmH‚ÇÇO, hypercapnia, inability to protect airway/clear secretions, impending respiratory failure
  • Intubation considerations: AVOID succinylcholine (unpredictable response). Use non-depolarizing agents at reduced dose (increased sensitivity). Rocuronium 0.3-0.6mg/kg (half normal dose)
  • Ventilation: Lung-protective strategy. Low tidal volume (6mL/kg IBW). Adequate PEEP
  • Extubation: When VC >15-20mL/kg, NIF <-30cmH‚ÇÇO, able to protect airway. Often takes 1-2 weeks after treatment

Immunotherapy for Crisis (Start Immediately):

  • IVIG (Intravenous Immunoglobulin): 0.4g/kg/day √ó 5 days OR 1g/kg/day √ó 2 days (total 2g/kg). First-line. Improvement in 3-7 days
  • Plasmapheresis (Plasma Exchange): 5 exchanges over 7-14 days. Equivalent efficacy to IVIG. Improvement in 2-4 days (faster than IVIG). Use if IVIG failed or contraindicated
  • Duration of benefit: Temporary (weeks-months). Need long-term immunosuppression

Acetylcholinesterase Inhibitor Management:

  • HOLD pyridostigmine during crisis: Respiratory failure impairs clearance of secretions (cholinergic side effect). Resume when extubated
  • When stable: Pyridostigmine 30-60mg PO q4-6hr (typical dose 60mg q4hr). Max 120mg/dose. Adjust based on response

Corticosteroids (Long-term, NOT in acute crisis):

  • NOT for acute crisis: Can cause paradoxical worsening in first 1-2 weeks
  • For chronic MG control: Prednisone 20-100mg daily (start low if outpatient to avoid worsening). Taper slowly after improvement
  • Steroid-sparing agents: Azathioprine, mycophenolate, cyclosporine, tacrolimus. Takes months to work

Treat Triggers:

  • Infection: Antibiotics. Avoid contraindicated agents (aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, macrolides)
  • Aspiration pneumonia: NPO until swallow safe. Aspiration precautions
  • Medication review: Stop all potentially exacerbating medications

Supportive Care:

  • DVT prophylaxis: SCDs + heparin/LMWH
  • GI: PPI for stress ulcer prophylaxis. NPO if intubated or severe bulbar weakness
  • Nutrition: NG tube for feeding if prolonged intubation or severe dysphagia
  • Physical therapy: Early mobilization when stable

Thymectomy: Improves long-term outcomes in generalized MG, especially if thymoma present. Usually done electively, not during crisis. Discuss with neurology/thoracic surgery

üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Fatigable weakness is pathognomonic: Worse with activity, improves with rest. Ptosis worsens when looking up √ó 1min. This distinguishes MG from other causes of weakness
  • Crisis can develop rapidly: Can go from stable to intubated within hours. Monitor VC closely. Don't wait for ABG abnormalities (late finding)
  • Ice pack test for ptosis: Apply ice to closed eyelid √ó 2min. Ptosis improves in MG (cold improves NMJ transmission). Simple bedside test
  • Avoid succinylcholine: Unpredictable response in MG. May have prolonged paralysis. Use non-depolarizing agents at REDUCED doses (increased sensitivity)
  • Hold pyridostigmine in crisis: Increases secretions which impairs ventilation. Resume when extubated and improving
  • IVIG and PLEX are temporizing: Provide weeks-months of benefit. Need long-term immunosuppression (prednisone, azathioprine, etc) for disease control
  • Seronegative MG exists (10-15%): Negative antibodies don't rule out MG. Use clinical features + EMG (RNS or single-fiber)
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • ALL myasthenic crisis patients need ICU
  • VC <20mL/kg or rapidly declining
  • NIF >-30cmH‚ÇÇO
  • Severe bulbar weakness (aspiration risk)
  • Inability to clear secretions
  • Respiratory distress (tachypnea, accessory muscle use)
  • Need for intubation
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Successfully extubated with stable respiratory status
  • VC >20mL/kg, NIF <-30cmH‚ÇÇO
  • Completed IVIG or plasmapheresis
  • Swallow safe (passed speech/swallow eval)
  • Pyridostigmine restarted at stable dose
  • Long-term immunosuppression plan in place
  • Trigger identified and treated (infection, etc)
  • Neurology follow-up arranged (close follow-up needed)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Respiratory failure (15-20% of MG patients have crisis), aspiration pneumonia (bulbar weakness), prolonged intubation, ventilator-associated pneumonia, DVT/PE, pressure ulcers

Treatment-related: IVIG complications (aseptic meningitis, hemolysis, thrombosis, renal failure), Plasmapheresis complications (hypotension, line infection, citrate toxicity), Steroid complications (hyperglycemia, infection, osteoporosis, psychosis)

Chronic: Recurrent crises (30% have >1 crisis), medication side effects (chronic steroids), thymoma (10-15%, can be malignant), need for chronic immunosuppression, disability from weakness

Mortality: 4-8% mortality from myasthenic crisis (improved from 40% in pre-ICU era)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

Acute Delirium (Non-Alcohol)

[Content to be added in Phase 3]

54. INCREASED INTRACRANIAL PRESSURE (ICP)

Cushing's Triad ‚Ä¢ Herniation Syndromes ‚Ä¢ Emergency Management
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Normal ICP: 5-15 mmHg. Increased ICP = sustained elevation >20 mmHg. Life-threatening when >30 mmHg or when herniation occurs

Cushing's Triad (LATE finding, indicates impending herniation):

  • Hypertension: Widened pulse pressure (elevated systolic, normal/low diastolic)
  • Bradycardia: Reflex response to hypertension
  • Irregular respirations: Cheyne-Stokes, apneustic, or ataxic breathing
  • Pearl: Cushing's triad is a pre-terminal sign. Don't wait for it - treat earlier symptoms!

Early/Progressive Symptoms:

  • Headache: Severe, worse in morning, worse with Valsalva (cough, sneeze, straining)
  • Nausea/vomiting: Projectile vomiting (without nausea sometimes)
  • Altered mental status: Confusion, lethargy, obtundation ‚Üí coma
  • Papilledema: Swollen optic discs on fundoscopy. Takes hours-days to develop
  • Vision changes: Blurry vision, diplopia, transient visual obscurations
  • Seizures: From mass effect or ischemia

Herniation Syndromes (EMERGENCY):

  • Uncal herniation (most common): Ipsilateral dilated pupil (CN III compression), contralateral hemiparesis (compression of cerebral peduncle), Cushing's triad, coma
  • Central herniation: Small reactive pupils ‚Üí bilateral dilated pupils, decorticate ‚Üí decerebrate posturing, coma
  • Tonsillar herniation: Cerebellar tonsils herniate through foramen magnum. Apnea, sudden death from medullary compression
  • Subfalcine herniation: Often asymptomatic until late. Can cause ACA stroke

Common Causes:

  • Traumatic brain injury (TBI): Epidural hematoma, subdural hematoma, cerebral contusion, diffuse axonal injury
  • Hemorrhagic stroke: Intracerebral hemorrhage (ICH), subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH) with hydrocephalus
  • Ischemic stroke: Large MCA territory infarct with edema (malignant MCA syndrome)
  • Tumor: Primary or metastatic brain tumors with mass effect
  • Infection: Brain abscess, meningitis, encephalitis
  • Hydrocephalus: Obstructive (tumor, blood) or communicating
  • Idiopathic intracranial hypertension (IIH): Pseudotumor cerebri. Young obese women. Chronic headache, papilledema, vision loss
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Immediate Imaging (Don't Delay):

  • CT head (non-contrast): FIRST-LINE. Fast, identifies hemorrhage, mass lesions, hydrocephalus, midline shift, herniation. Signs of increased ICP: effaced sulci, compressed ventricles, loss of gray-white differentiation, midline shift
  • MRI brain: Better detail but takes longer. Use if CT non-diagnostic or for specific concerns (abscess, posterior fossa, tumor characterization)

ICP Monitoring (Neurosurgery):

  • Indications: Severe TBI (GCS ‚â§8) with abnormal CT, intraparenchymal hemorrhage requiring surgery, SAH with poor grade, refractory elevated ICP
  • Types: External ventricular drain (EVD) - gold standard, allows CSF drainage + monitoring. Intraparenchymal monitor (fiberoptic) - monitoring only
  • Goal ICP: <20 mmHg. Treat if >22 sustained
  • Cerebral perfusion pressure (CPP) = MAP - ICP: Goal CPP 60-70 mmHg

Fundoscopy: Look for papilledema (blurred disc margins, disc elevation, venous engorgement). Absence doesn't rule out increased ICP (takes time to develop)

Labs: CBC, CMP, coags (before procedures), osmolality (if using hyperosmolar therapy), ABG (avoid hypercapnia - vasodilates cerebral vessels)

DO NOT perform LP if increased ICP! Risk of herniation. Only after imaging rules out mass lesion and no signs of herniation

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

General Principles:

  • Treat underlying cause: Evacuate hematoma, resect tumor, drain abscess, treat infection, drain CSF (EVD for hydrocephalus)
  • Reduce ICP: Medical management + surgical if indicated
  • Maintain cerebral perfusion: CPP = MAP - ICP. Goal CPP 60-70 mmHg

Immediate Medical Management (if signs of herniation or ICP >22):

  • Head of bed elevation: 30 degrees. Improves venous drainage. Keep head midline (no neck rotation - obstructs jugular veins)
  • Hyperosmolar therapy:
    • Mannitol: 0.25-1g/kg IV bolus (typical 100g = 500mL of 20% solution). Repeat q4-6hr PRN. Monitor serum osmolality (keep <320 mOsm/kg). Watch for hypovolemia, AKI
    • Hypertonic saline: 3% NaCl 250mL bolus over 10-20min. Or 23.4% NaCl 30mL bolus via central line. Repeat PRN. Monitor Na (keep <160 mEq/L). May be superior to mannitol
  • Hyperventilation (TEMPORIZING ONLY): Target pCO‚ÇÇ 30-35 mmHg. Causes cerebral vasoconstriction ‚Üí reduces cerebral blood volume ‚Üí lowers ICP. ONLY for emergent herniation (buys time for definitive treatment). Prolonged use causes ischemia
  • Sedation: Propofol or midazolam. Reduces cerebral metabolic demand. Intubate first if severe
  • Seizure prophylaxis: Levetiracetam 500-1000mg IV. Seizures increase ICP

Supportive ICU Care:

  • Intubation: If GCS ‚â§8, inability to protect airway, need for hyperventilation. Use RSI with lidocaine pretreatment (blunts ICP spike from intubation)
  • Ventilation: Target pCO‚ÇÇ 35-40 mmHg (normal). Avoid hypercapnia (vasodilates) and excessive hypocapnia (causes ischemia)
  • Oxygenation: Keep O‚ÇÇ sat >90%. Hypoxia worsens cerebral edema
  • Blood pressure: Maintain adequate MAP to achieve CPP 60-70 mmHg. May need vasopressors (norepinephrine)
  • Temperature: Avoid hyperthermia (increases metabolic demand). Goal normothermia. Consider therapeutic hypothermia (32-36¬∞C) if refractory (controversial)
  • Glucose: Avoid hyper and hypoglycemia. Goal 140-180 mg/dL
  • Avoid: Hypotonic fluids (worsens cerebral edema). Use isotonic (0.9% NaCl) or hypertonic

Surgical Management (Neurosurgery Consult):

  • Hematoma evacuation: Epidural, subdural, or intraparenchymal hematoma with mass effect
  • External ventricular drain (EVD): Drains CSF, reduces ICP. For hydrocephalus or as ICP monitor
  • Decompressive craniectomy: Remove part of skull to allow brain to swell outward. For malignant MCA syndrome, refractory TBI. Reduces mortality but increases severe disability
  • Tumor resection/abscess drainage: If mass lesion causing ICP

Refractory ICP (if above measures fail):

  • Barbiturate coma: Pentobarbital load 5-10mg/kg, then 1-4mg/kg/hr. Reduces cerebral metabolic rate. Requires continuous EEG monitoring. High complication rate (hypotension, infection)
  • Decompressive craniectomy: Last resort for refractory ICP
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Cushing's triad is a late/pre-terminal sign: Don't wait for it! Treat based on symptoms, imaging, and ICP monitoring. By the time Cushing's triad appears, herniation is imminent
  • Herniation kills via brainstem compression: Respiratory centers (apnea), cardiovascular centers (shock), reticular activating system (coma). Uncal herniation ‚Üí ipsilateral blown pupil is classic
  • Hyperventilation is temporizing only: Buys 15-30 minutes by vasoconstricting cerebral vessels. Prolonged use causes ischemia. Use only for emergent herniation while preparing for definitive treatment
  • Mannitol vs hypertonic saline: Both work. Hypertonic saline may be superior (no osmotic diuresis, longer duration). Hypertonic saline preferred in hypovolemic patients
  • Avoid LP if increased ICP: Removing CSF creates pressure gradient ‚Üí brainstem herniates through foramen magnum (tonsillar herniation) ‚Üí death. Always image first!
  • Head of bed 30¬∞, not flat: Improves venous drainage, reduces ICP. Keep head midline (turning neck obstructs jugular veins ‚Üí worsens ICP)
  • CPP matters more than ICP: Brain needs perfusion. CPP = MAP - ICP. Goal CPP 60-70 mmHg. May need to increase MAP with pressors even if ICP elevated
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • ALL increased ICP patients need ICU
  • GCS ‚â§8
  • Signs of herniation (blown pupil, posturing, Cushing's triad)
  • Need for ICP monitoring
  • Need for hyperosmolar therapy or hyperventilation
  • Neurosurgical emergency
  • Intubated for airway protection
‚úì ICU ‚Üí FLOOR CRITERIA
  • ICP controlled (<20 mmHg sustained)
  • No herniation risk
  • Underlying cause treated (hematoma evacuated, CSF draining, etc)
  • Off hyperosmolar therapy √ó 24hr
  • Extubated (if intubated)
  • Neurologically stable or improving
  • Can remove ICP monitor if placed
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Herniation (uncal, central, tonsillar) ‚Üí death or severe disability, ischemic stroke (from low CPP or herniation-related vascular compression), seizures, vision loss (optic nerve compression from papilledema), brainstem injury

Treatment-related: Hyperventilation ‚Üí cerebral ischemia, Mannitol ‚Üí hypovolemia, AKI, rebound cerebral edema, Hypertonic saline ‚Üí hypernatremia, central pontine myelinolysis (if Na corrected too fast), Decompressive craniectomy ‚Üí infection, seizures, need for cranioplasty

Chronic: Cognitive impairment, motor deficits, vision loss (from chronic papilledema), seizure disorder, chronic disability, death (mortality varies by etiology: TBI 20-50%, malignant stroke 80% without craniectomy)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

55. SPINAL CORD COMPRESSION

Oncologic Emergency ‚Ä¢ Cauda Equina ‚Ä¢ Urgent MRI/Decompression
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Classic Triad: (1) Back pain, (2) Motor weakness, (3) Sensory level. Autonomic dysfunction (bowel/bladder) is LATE finding

Back Pain (>90%, often first symptom):

  • Characteristics: Severe, progressive, localized to site of compression. Worse with recumbency, Valsalva (cough, sneeze)
  • Radicular pain: Shooting pain down extremity (dermatome distribution)
  • Percussion tenderness: Tenderness over spinous process at level of compression

Motor Findings:

  • Weakness: Progressive, bilateral (though may start asymmetric). Upper motor neuron pattern (hyperreflexia, spasticity, upgoing Babinski) if compression above conus. Lower motor neuron if conus/cauda equina
  • Gait disturbance: Difficulty walking, ataxia
  • Severity: Can progress from subtle weakness to paraplegia in hours-days

Sensory Findings:

  • Sensory level: Decreased sensation below dermatome corresponding to compression level. Test pinprick from feet upward
  • Paresthesias: Numbness, tingling in legs/arms
  • Band-like sensation: Tight band around chest/abdomen at level of compression

Autonomic Dysfunction (LATE, indicates severe compression):

  • Bladder dysfunction: Urinary retention (most common), overflow incontinence, loss of urge sensation
  • Bowel dysfunction: Constipation, fecal incontinence
  • Sexual dysfunction: Erectile dysfunction, loss of sensation
  • Loss of anal sphincter tone: Decreased rectal tone on exam (check rectal exam!)

Cauda Equina Syndrome (Compression Below L1-L2):

  • Classic: Severe back/leg pain, saddle anesthesia (perineum, buttocks, inner thighs), bilateral leg weakness (lower motor neuron - areflexia), bowel/bladder dysfunction, decreased anal tone
  • Pearl: Lower motor neuron findings (flaccid weakness, areflexia, muscle atrophy) vs cord compression (upper motor neuron)

Etiologies:

  • Metastatic cancer (50-70%): Most common cause. Breast, lung, prostate, renal, melanoma, myeloma. Vertebral body ‚Üí epidural extension
  • Epidural abscess (5-10%): Staph aureus most common. Preceding bacteremia, IVDU, spinal procedure, immunosuppression
  • Epidural hematoma: Trauma, anticoagulation, spinal procedure (epidural, LP)
  • Disc herniation: Large central or posterolateral disc. Acute (trauma) or chronic (degenerative)
  • Spinal stenosis: Chronic compression from degenerative changes. Progressive symptoms
  • Primary cord tumor: Astrocytoma, ependymoma (intramedullary)
  • Trauma: Vertebral fracture, subluxation
  • Other: Transverse myelitis, spinal AVM, spontaneous epidural hematoma
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

MRI Spine with Gadolinium (URGENT, <24hr):

  • GOLD STANDARD: Identifies level and cause of compression. Visualizes entire spine (metastases can be multiple levels)
  • Image entire spine: Cervical, thoracic, lumbar. 20-30% have lesions at multiple non-contiguous levels
  • Timing: STAT MRI if progressive motor weakness or bowel/bladder dysfunction (hours matter!). Within 24hr if concerning symptoms
  • Contraindications: Pacemaker, metallic implants (some), severe claustrophobia

CT Myelography (if MRI contraindicated or unavailable):

  • Requires LP: Inject contrast into subarachnoid space, then CT spine. Shows obstruction to flow
  • Risk: If complete block, LP can worsen compression. MRI preferred

Labs:

  • CBC: Leukocytosis (abscess), anemia (malignancy)
  • ESR/CRP: Elevated (abscess, malignancy)
  • Blood cultures: If concern for epidural abscess (fever, elevated WBC)
  • PSA: If prostate cancer suspected
  • Coags: If epidural hematoma suspected (anticoagulation, bleeding disorder)

Rectal Exam (MANDATORY): Assess anal tone, perianal sensation. Decreased tone = cauda equina or severe cord compression

Post-Void Residual (PVR): Bladder scan after patient voids. PVR >100-200mL = retention (indicates autonomic dysfunction)

Red Flags Requiring URGENT MRI:

  • New bowel/bladder dysfunction
  • Saddle anesthesia
  • Progressive motor weakness
  • Known cancer with new back pain or neurologic symptoms
  • Fever + back pain (epidural abscess)
  • Severe back pain after trauma
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Immediate Management (BEFORE definitive treatment):

  • Dexamethasone (if malignancy suspected): 10mg IV STAT, then 4mg IV q6hr. Reduces cord edema. Give IMMEDIATELY while arranging MRI. Do NOT delay for imaging
  • Hold steroids if abscess suspected: Can worsen infection. Give antibiotics first
  • Pain control: Opioids as needed. Pain can be severe
  • Bladder catheterization: Foley if urinary retention
  • Spinal precautions: If trauma, keep spine immobilized

Definitive Treatment by Etiology:

  • Metastatic Epidural Spinal Cord Compression (MESCC):
    • Surgery: Decompressive laminectomy + tumor resection. Indications: radiosensitive tumor, spinal instability, neurologic decline on radiation, unclear diagnosis needing biopsy. Better outcomes than radiation alone if ambulatory pre-op
    • Radiation therapy: For radiosensitive tumors (lymphoma, myeloma, small cell lung, prostate). 8-10 Gy √ó 1-2 fractions emergently. Can preserve ambulation
    • Combination: Surgery followed by radiation for local control
    • Dexamethasone: Continue √ó 3-7 days, then taper over 2-4 weeks
  • Epidural Abscess:
    • Antibiotics: Empiric vancomycin 15mg/kg IV q12hr + ceftriaxone 2g IV q24hr OR cefepime 2g IV q8hr. Adjust based on cultures
    • Surgery: Urgent laminectomy and drainage if neurologic deficits, spinal instability, or failed medical therapy. Source control critical
    • Medical management alone: Consider if no neuro deficits, small abscess, high surgical risk. Requires close monitoring
    • Duration: 4-8 weeks IV antibiotics (longer if osteomyelitis)
  • Epidural Hematoma:
    • Reverse anticoagulation: Vitamin K, FFP, PCC, or idarucizumab/andexanet depending on agent
    • Surgery: Urgent decompression if neurologic deficits
    • Conservative: Small hematoma without neuro deficits can be observed
  • Disc Herniation/Cauda Equina:
    • Surgery: EMERGENT discectomy (within 24-48hr). Outcomes worsen with delay. Cauda equina = surgical emergency
    • Medical management: Only if no red flags, no progressive deficits. NSAIDs, PT

Surgical Timing (Critical):

  • Cauda equina: Within 24-48hr. Every hour delay worsens outcomes (bowel/bladder recovery)
  • Progressive motor weakness: Emergent (hours). Pre-op ambulatory status predicts post-op ambulation
  • Paraplegia >48hr: Unlikely to recover ambulation even with surgery. Still decompress for pain, bowel/bladder

Supportive Care:

  • DVT prophylaxis: SCDs + heparin/LMWH (unless hematoma)
  • Bowel regimen: Prevent constipation (ileus common)
  • Bladder management: Foley if retention. Intermittent cath when stable
  • Skin care: Turn q2hr, pressure ulcer prevention
  • Physical therapy: Early mobilization when safe
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Back pain + cancer = MRI until proven otherwise: 95% of MESCC patients have back pain. Any cancer patient with new/worsening back pain needs urgent spine MRI
  • Autonomic dysfunction = LATE finding: Bowel/bladder involvement indicates severe compression. Don't wait for it - intervene earlier based on motor/sensory findings
  • Ambulatory status predicts outcome: If ambulatory before treatment, 90% remain ambulatory. If paraplegic, only 10-20% regain ambulation. TIMING MATTERS
  • Give steroids BEFORE MRI if malignancy suspected: Dexamethasone 10mg IV immediately. Reduces cord edema while waiting for imaging/surgery. Don't delay for diagnosis
  • Check rectal tone! Mandatory part of exam. Decreased tone = cauda equina or severe compression requiring emergent intervention
  • Cauda equina = surgical emergency: Decompress within 24-48hr. Permanent bowel/bladder/sexual dysfunction if delayed. Saddle anesthesia + bladder retention + bilateral leg weakness = OR now
  • Epidural abscess triad: Back pain + fever + neurologic deficits. But only 10-15% have all three. High index of suspicion in IVDU, recent spinal procedure, or bacteremia
‚ö†Ô∏è URGENT NEUROSURGERY CONSULT IF:
  • ANY cord compression on imaging with neurologic deficits
  • Cauda equina syndrome (surgical emergency)
  • Progressive motor weakness
  • Bowel/bladder dysfunction
  • Epidural abscess with neuro deficits
  • Epidural hematoma with neuro deficits
  • Large disc herniation with cauda equina
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Neurologically stable or improving
  • Post-surgical if operated (wound healing, no infection)
  • Pain controlled on oral regimen
  • Bladder function recovered OR able to self-cath
  • Bowel regimen established
  • If abscess: Appropriate antibiotics, plan for weeks-long course
  • Inpatient rehab arranged (many need extensive PT/OT)
  • Oncology follow-up (if malignancy)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Permanent paraplegia (if delayed treatment), respiratory failure (if high cervical lesion), neurogenic shock (spinal shock - hypotension, bradycardia from loss of sympathetic tone), DVT/PE (immobility), pressure ulcers, urinary retention ‚Üí hydronephrosis

Surgical: Wound infection, CSF leak, worsening neurologic deficits, spinal instability, epidural hematoma

Chronic: Permanent bowel/bladder dysfunction (30-70% if cauda equina), chronic pain, sexual dysfunction, spasticity, recurrent UTIs (neurogenic bladder), pressure ulcers, DVT, depression, need for lifelong assistance/wheelchair

Prognosis: Depends on etiology, severity at presentation, and time to treatment. Pre-op ambulatory status is most important predictor. Paraplegia >48hr rarely recovers

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

56. INFLUENZA/SEVERE VIRAL PNEUMONIA

Viral Respiratory Illness ‚Ä¢ Primary Viral Pneumonia ‚Ä¢ Oseltamivir/Supportive Care
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Influenza (Uncomplicated): Abrupt onset fever, myalgias, headache, malaise, dry cough, sore throat. Self-limited in 3-7 days in healthy individuals

Severe Influenza/Primary Viral Pneumonia: Progressive dyspnea, hypoxemia, bilateral infiltrates despite antiviral therapy. Can rapidly progress to ARDS. More common in high-risk patients

Classic Presentation:

  • Respiratory: Dyspnea, tachypnea, hypoxemia (O‚ÇÇ sat <90%), cough (initially dry, may become productive), pleuritic chest pain
  • Systemic: High fever (>38.5¬∞C), severe myalgias, fatigue, headache
  • Timeline: Initial flu symptoms ‚Üí worsening respiratory status over 2-5 days ‚Üí ARDS (severe cases)

High-Risk Groups (for Severe Disease):

  • Age: <5 years (especially <2 years), ‚â•65 years
  • Chronic conditions: Asthma, COPD, heart disease, diabetes, chronic kidney disease, liver disease, obesity (BMI ‚â•40)
  • Immunocompromised: HIV, malignancy, transplant, chronic steroids
  • Pregnancy/postpartum: Up to 2 weeks postpartum
  • Nursing home residents: Frail, multiple comorbidities

Complications:

  • Primary viral pneumonia: Diffuse bilateral infiltrates, hypoxemia, rapid progression to ARDS
  • Secondary bacterial pneumonia: S. aureus (including MRSA), S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae. Suspect if improvement followed by clinical worsening, new fever, purulent sputum
  • ARDS: Acute respiratory distress syndrome. P/F ratio <300. Bilateral infiltrates. High mortality
  • Myocarditis: Troponin elevation, arrhythmias, heart failure
  • Rhabdomyolysis: Severe myalgias, elevated CK, acute kidney injury
  • Encephalopathy/encephalitis: Altered mental status, seizures (more common in children)

Influenza Subtypes: Type A (H1N1, H3N2 - cause pandemics), Type B (seasonal, less severe). Type A more virulent. Novel strains (e.g., H1N1 pdm09, avian H5N1, H7N9) can cause severe disease in previously healthy adults

DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Rapid Influenza Testing:

  • Rapid antigen tests (RIDT): Results in 15-30min. Sensitivity 50-70% (low). Specificity >90%. Negative test does NOT rule out influenza
  • Molecular tests (PCR): GOLD STANDARD. High sensitivity (90-95%). Results in 1-8hr. Can differentiate influenza A vs B, subtypes. Preferred in hospitalized patients
  • Multiplex respiratory panel: Tests for influenza + RSV, adenovirus, parainfluenza, coronavirus, others. Useful for ruling in/out other pathogens. Helps determine need for isolation precautions
  • Timing: Test within first 4 days of illness (viral shedding highest). Can test up to 7 days if hospitalized or immunocompromised
  • Sample: Nasopharyngeal swab (preferred), nasal swab, throat swab. Lower respiratory samples (BAL, sputum) if intubated

Labs:

  • CBC: Leukopenia or normal WBC (lymphopenia common). Leukocytosis suggests bacterial superinfection
  • CMP: Assess renal function (before antivirals), electrolytes
  • Blood cultures: If bacterial superinfection suspected (fever after initial improvement, leukocytosis)
  • Sputum culture: If productive cough, suspicion for bacterial pneumonia
  • Procalcitonin: Low in viral infection (<0.25). Elevated in bacterial co-infection (>0.5)
  • CK: If severe myalgias (check for rhabdomyolysis)
  • Troponin: If chest pain, ECG changes (myocarditis/myopericarditis can occur)

Imaging:

  • CXR: May be normal early. Bilateral interstitial infiltrates (viral pneumonia). Lobar consolidation (bacterial superinfection). Diffuse bilateral infiltrates (ARDS)
  • CT chest: Ground-glass opacities (viral), tree-in-bud (bronchiolitis), consolidation. More sensitive than CXR but not routinely needed

ABG: If hypoxemia (O‚ÇÇ sat <90%). Assess P/F ratio (PaO‚ÇÇ/FiO‚ÇÇ). <300 = ARDS

Clinical Diagnosis: In outbreak setting with typical symptoms, clinical diagnosis is acceptable. Testing confirms diagnosis and guides treatment/isolation

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Antiviral Therapy (CRITICAL):

  • Oseltamivir (Tamiflu): 75mg PO BID √ó 5 days. FIRST-LINE. Neuraminidase inhibitor. Reduces duration of illness by 1-2 days. Reduces complications and mortality in hospitalized patients
  • Zanamivir (Relenza): 10mg inhaled BID √ó 5 days. Alternative if oseltamivir not available. Contraindicated in asthma/COPD (bronchospasm risk)
  • Peramivir (Rapivab): 600mg IV once daily √ó 5 days. For hospitalized patients unable to take PO/inhaled
  • Baloxavir (Xofluza): Single dose (40-80mg PO based on weight). Newer agent. Polymerase inhibitor. Not preferred in hospitalized patients (limited data)
  • Timing: START WITHIN 48 HOURS of symptom onset for maximum benefit. Still beneficial if started >48hr in hospitalized/severe cases. DO NOT delay treatment for test results in hospitalized patients
  • Who to treat: ALL hospitalized patients with confirmed or suspected influenza, regardless of illness duration. Outpatients with high-risk conditions. Severe illness (pneumonia, hypoxemia, respiratory failure)
  • Duration: Standard 5 days. Consider longer course (10 days) in immunocompromised, severe disease, or persistent viral shedding

Respiratory Support:

  • Supplemental oxygen: Target O‚ÇÇ sat ‚â•90-92%. Nasal cannula ‚Üí face mask ‚Üí high-flow nasal cannula ‚Üí NIPPV
  • High-flow nasal cannula (HFNC): 40-60L/min, FiO‚ÇÇ 0.4-1.0. May avoid intubation in hypoxemic respiratory failure
  • NIPPV (BiPAP/CPAP): Consider if HFNC insufficient. Caution: increased risk of aerosolization (use negative pressure room)
  • Mechanical ventilation: If refractory hypoxemia, work of breathing, altered mental status. Lung-protective ventilation (TV 6mL/kg IBW, plateau pressure <30cmH‚ÇÇO, PEEP titrated)
  • Prone positioning: Consider if P/F <150 (severe ARDS). Improves oxygenation and mortality
  • ECMO: Consider in refractory ARDS despite maximal support. Transfer to ECMO center if available

Antibiotics (for Bacterial Superinfection):

  • When to add: Lobar consolidation on imaging, purulent sputum, leukocytosis, clinical worsening after initial improvement, procalcitonin >0.5, positive bacterial cultures
  • Community-acquired pneumonia coverage: Ceftriaxone 1-2g IV daily + azithromycin 500mg IV daily OR levofloxacin 750mg IV daily
  • MRSA coverage (if suspected): ADD vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hr OR linezolid 600mg IV q12hr. Post-influenza MRSA pneumonia is severe (necrotizing, high mortality)
  • Duration: 5-7 days for CAP. Longer if slow response or complicated (empyema, abscess)

Supportive Care:

  • Hydration: IV fluids (NS or LR) for dehydration. Maintain euvolemia (avoid fluid overload in ARDS)
  • Antipyretics: Acetaminophen 650-1000mg q6hr PRN or ibuprofen 400-600mg q6hr PRN for fever/myalgias
  • Cough suppressants: Dextromethorphan or benzonatate PRN (use sparingly - cough clears secretions)
  • DVT prophylaxis: Heparin 5000 units SQ q8-12hr or LMWH (unless contraindicated)
  • Stress ulcer prophylaxis: PPI if mechanically ventilated or high bleeding risk

Corticosteroids (CONTROVERSIAL):

  • NOT routinely recommended: May prolong viral shedding, increase secondary infections
  • Consider in refractory shock: Hydrocortisone 50mg IV q6hr if vasopressor-dependent shock
  • Consider in ARDS: Low-dose methylprednisolone (1mg/kg/day) in severe ARDS (P/F <150) - controversial, mixed data

Infection Control:

  • Droplet precautions: Surgical mask for healthcare workers. Private room preferred. Respirators (N95) for aerosol-generating procedures
  • Duration of isolation: Until 24hr afebrile without antipyretics AND improvement in respiratory symptoms. Immunocompromised: may shed virus for weeks (consult ID)
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Don't delay antivirals: Start oseltamivir empirically in hospitalized patients with suspected influenza BEFORE test results. Greatest benefit if started within 48hr, but still beneficial in severe cases started later
  • Post-influenza S. aureus pneumonia is deadly: Classic scenario: Patient improves, then worsens 3-7 days later with new fever, leukocytosis, necrotizing pneumonia. Add vancomycin or linezolid immediately
  • Negative rapid flu test doesn't rule out influenza: RIDT sensitivity only 50-70%. If high clinical suspicion, send PCR and treat empirically
  • Primary viral pneumonia can cause ARDS rapidly: Young, healthy patients can deteriorate quickly (24-72hr). Early ICU transfer and aggressive respiratory support
  • Procalcitonin helps guide antibiotics: Low PCT (<0.25) = viral. High PCT (>0.5) = bacterial co-infection. Can help avoid unnecessary antibiotics
  • Influenza myocarditis is underrecognized: Check troponin in severe cases, especially with chest pain or arrhythmias. Can cause acute heart failure
  • Zanamivir is inhaled - avoid in lung disease: Can cause bronchospasm in asthma/COPD patients. Use oseltamivir instead
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Hypoxemic respiratory failure (O‚ÇÇ sat <90% despite supplemental O‚ÇÇ)
  • Need for HFNC, NIPPV, or mechanical ventilation
  • Septic shock (hypotension requiring vasopressors)
  • ARDS (P/F ratio <300)
  • Altered mental status, encephalopathy
  • Lactic acidosis (lactate >4mmol/L)
  • Rapid clinical deterioration despite therapy
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Afebrile ‚â•24hr without antipyretics
  • O‚ÇÇ sat ‚â•90% on room air (or back to baseline if chronic lung disease)
  • Respiratory rate <24/min at rest
  • Tolerating PO intake and medications
  • Completed 5-day course of oseltamivir (or at least 48hr and improving)
  • No evidence of bacterial superinfection (or completed appropriate antibiotic course)
  • Able to perform ADLs or return to baseline function
  • Follow-up arranged (PCP within 1 week)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Primary viral pneumonia ‚Üí ARDS (P/F <200, high mortality), secondary bacterial pneumonia (S. aureus, S. pneumoniae - most common), septic shock, myocarditis (troponin elevation, arrhythmias, cardiogenic shock), rhabdomyolysis (CK >10,000, AKI), encephalitis/encephalopathy (altered mental status, seizures, especially children), exacerbation of underlying conditions (asthma, COPD, heart failure)

Chronic: Post-viral chronic cough (weeks-months), persistent fatigue, prolonged recovery in elderly/frail patients, cardiovascular events (MI, stroke in first 2 weeks post-infection - 3-5x increased risk)

Prevention: Annual influenza vaccine (inactivated or live attenuated). Highest priority: healthcare workers, elderly, chronic disease, pregnant women, young children. Vaccine reduces risk of hospitalization by 40-60%. Post-exposure prophylaxis with oseltamivir 75mg daily √ó 10 days for high-risk unvaccinated contacts

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

57. COVID-19 SEVERE DISEASE

SARS-CoV-2 ‚Ä¢ Viral Pneumonia ‚Üí ARDS ‚Ä¢ Dexamethasone + Supportive Care
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Disease Severity Classification (NIH):

  • Mild: Any symptoms (fever, cough, fatigue) without dyspnea or abnormal imaging
  • Moderate: Evidence of lower respiratory disease (clinical or imaging) + O‚ÇÇ sat ‚â•94% on room air
  • SEVERE: O‚ÇÇ sat <94% on room air, P/F ratio <300, RR >30/min, or lung infiltrates >50%
  • CRITICAL: Respiratory failure (requiring mechanical ventilation, HFNC, NIPPV), septic shock, multiorgan dysfunction

Severe/Critical COVID-19 Presentation:

  • Respiratory: Progressive dyspnea (typically 5-10 days after symptom onset), tachypnea (RR >30/min), hypoxemia (O‚ÇÇ sat <94%), increased work of breathing, bilateral pneumonia
  • Systemic: Persistent fever, fatigue, myalgias, anorexia
  • GI: Diarrhea (20-30%), nausea, abdominal pain
  • Neurologic: Confusion, delirium, headache, anosmia/ageusia (loss of smell/taste)
  • Thromboembolic: DVT, PE, stroke, MI (hypercoagulable state)

High-Risk Groups (for Severe Disease):

  • Age: ‚â•65 years (risk increases exponentially with age)
  • Chronic conditions: Obesity (BMI ‚â•30), diabetes, hypertension, cardiovascular disease, chronic lung disease (COPD, asthma), chronic kidney disease, liver disease
  • Immunocompromised: Solid organ transplant, hematologic malignancy, chemotherapy, HIV (CD4 <200), chronic high-dose steroids (‚â•20mg prednisone daily), immunosuppressive medications
  • Pregnancy: Increased risk of severe disease, preterm birth, ICU admission
  • Other: Smoking, sickle cell disease, Down syndrome, neurologic conditions, tuberculosis

Typical Timeline: Symptom onset (day 0) ‚Üí fever/cough/fatigue (days 1-4) ‚Üí hypoxemia/dyspnea (days 5-8) ‚Üí ARDS/critical illness (days 8-12) ‚Üí recovery or multiorgan failure (weeks 2-4+)

Key Features Distinguishing COVID-19 ARDS: "Silent hypoxemia" (profound hypoxemia with minimal dyspnea), preserved lung compliance early, hypercoagulability (elevated D-dimer), inflammatory cytokine storm (elevated CRP, ferritin, IL-6)

DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

SARS-CoV-2 Testing:

  • Nucleic acid amplification (NAAT/PCR): GOLD STANDARD. Nasopharyngeal swab. Sensitivity 80-90%. Detects active infection
  • Rapid antigen test: Results in 15-30min. Sensitivity 60-80% (lower than PCR). Best within first 5-7 days of symptoms. False negatives common
  • Timing: Test all hospitalized patients with respiratory symptoms. PCR preferred. Antigen OK for screening, but confirm negatives with PCR if high suspicion
  • Repeat testing: If initial negative but high suspicion, repeat in 24-48hr or send lower respiratory sample (sputum, BAL)

Labs (for Severity Assessment & Prognostication):

  • CBC: Lymphopenia (common, correlates with severity), thrombocytopenia (severe disease)
  • CMP: BUN/Cr (AKI in 20-40% of hospitalized), transaminitis (ALT/AST elevation), hypoalbuminemia (poor prognosis)
  • Inflammatory markers: CRP, ferritin, LDH (elevated in severe disease, correlate with mortality), IL-6 (if available - very high in cytokine storm)
  • D-dimer: Elevated in >70% of severe cases. Markedly elevated (>5,000ng/mL) = poor prognosis, high VTE risk
  • Procalcitonin: Usually low-normal in viral infection. Elevation suggests bacterial superinfection
  • Troponin: Elevated in 20-30% of hospitalized patients (myocardial injury, myocarditis). Associated with increased mortality
  • BNP: If heart failure suspected (COVID-19 can precipitate HF)

Imaging:

  • CXR: Bilateral patchy infiltrates, consolidation, ground-glass opacities. Peripheral distribution. Normal CXR does NOT exclude COVID-19 pneumonia
  • CT chest: More sensitive. Bilateral ground-glass opacities (GGO) with peripheral distribution, "crazy-paving" pattern, consolidation. Useful if CXR nondiagnostic. CT not needed for diagnosis/management in typical cases

ABG: Assess P/F ratio if hypoxemic. <300 = ARDS. A-a gradient typically elevated

Other Testing (as indicated):

  • ECG: If chest pain, troponin elevation (check for STEMI, arrhythmias, myocarditis)
  • Blood cultures: If bacterial superinfection suspected
  • Respiratory viral panel: Rule out co-infections (influenza, RSV) if early in illness
  • VTE workup: CT-PE if high suspicion (tachycardia, hypoxemia out of proportion to lung findings, elevated D-dimer >5,000)

Poor Prognostic Indicators: Age >65, oxygen requirement, elevated inflammatory markers (CRP >100mg/L, D-dimer >5,000ng/mL, ferritin >1,000ng/mL, LDH >400U/L), lymphopenia (<800/¬µL), thrombocytopenia, AKI, troponin elevation, obesity (BMI ‚â•35)

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Corticosteroids (PROVEN MORTALITY BENEFIT - MAINSTAY):

  • Dexamethasone 6mg IV/PO daily √ó 10 days (or until discharge): RECOVERY trial showed 30% reduction in mortality at 28 days in patients requiring oxygen. Number needed to treat = 8 (for supplemental O‚ÇÇ), 4 (for mechanical ventilation)
  • Alternatives (if dexamethasone unavailable): Prednisone 40mg daily, methylprednisolone 32mg daily, hydrocortisone 160mg daily (divided doses)
  • Who to treat: ALL patients requiring supplemental oxygen or mechanical ventilation
  • Who NOT to treat: Mild COVID-19 not requiring oxygen (may cause harm)
  • Duration: 10 days or until discharge (whichever comes first). Can stop earlier if no longer requiring oxygen

Antiviral Therapy:

  • Remdesivir (Veklury): 200mg IV loading dose, then 100mg IV daily √ó 4 days (total 5 days). Modest benefit - reduces time to recovery by 5 days in hospitalized patients requiring oxygen (not ventilated). Consider in severe COVID-19 within 10 days of symptom onset. Less effective in mechanically ventilated patients. NOT a mortality benefit drug
  • Timing: Start within 10 days of symptom onset for maximum benefit
  • Contraindications: eGFR <30mL/min, ALT >10√ó ULN. Use cautiously if eGFR 30-60
  • Note: Other antivirals (nirmatrelvir-ritonavir/Paxlovid, molnupiravir) are for OUTPATIENTS only (within 5 days of symptoms). NOT for hospitalized patients with severe disease

IL-6 Receptor Antagonists (Anti-Cytokine Therapy):

  • Tocilizumab (Actemra) 8mg/kg IV once (max 800mg): Consider in patients with rapidly increasing oxygen requirements, CRP >75mg/L, within 24hr of ICU admission. Reduces mortality in severe/critical COVID-19 when combined with dexamethasone
  • Baricitinib (Olumiant) 4mg PO daily √ó 14 days: JAK inhibitor. Alternative to tocilizumab. Reduces mortality when combined with dexamethasone. Easier administration (oral). Contraindicated if ALC <200/¬µL, ANC <500/¬µL
  • Use ONE (not both): Tocilizumab OR baricitinib, in combination with dexamethasone
  • Screen for infections: Before use, rule out active bacterial/fungal infections

Respiratory Support:

  • Supplemental oxygen: Target O‚ÇÇ sat 92-96% (or 88-92% if COPD). Start with nasal cannula
  • High-flow nasal cannula (HFNC): 40-60L/min, FiO‚ÇÇ 0.4-1.0. Effective in preventing intubation in many patients. ROX index (SpO‚ÇÇ/FiO‚ÇÇ √ó RR) <3.85 after 12hr = high risk of HFNC failure
  • Prone positioning (AWAKE PRONING): Patient prone for 2-4hr several times daily while on HFNC. Improves oxygenation, may prevent intubation
  • NIPPV (BiPAP/CPAP): Use cautiously (risk of aerosolization, delayed intubation). Consider in select patients who decline intubation or as bridge
  • Mechanical ventilation: Intubate if worsening hypoxemia (P/F <150), work of breathing, or altered mental status. LUNG-PROTECTIVE VENTILATION: TV 6mL/kg IBW, plateau pressure <30cmH‚ÇÇO, PEEP 10-15cmH‚ÇÇO (higher PEEP in COVID-19), driving pressure <15cmH‚ÇÇO
  • Prone positioning (INTUBATED): For P/F <150 (moderate-severe ARDS). 16hr prone daily. Improves mortality
  • ECMO: Consider for refractory hypoxemia despite maximal support. Transfer to ECMO center

Anticoagulation (CRITICAL - Hypercoagulable State):

  • Prophylactic anticoagulation: ALL hospitalized patients unless contraindicated. Enoxaparin 40mg SQ daily (preferred) or heparin 5000 units SQ TID. Consider higher prophylactic dose (enoxaparin 0.5mg/kg BID) if BMI >40 or D-dimer >5,000
  • Therapeutic anticoagulation: If confirmed VTE (PE, DVT). NOT routinely for all severe COVID-19 patients (trials showed no benefit, increased bleeding in non-ICU patients)
  • Monitor D-dimer: Trending D-dimer useful. Persistently elevated or rising = higher VTE risk
  • Low threshold for CT-PE: If worsening hypoxemia, tachycardia, elevated D-dimer

Antibiotics (for Bacterial Superinfection ONLY):

  • When to add: Clinical worsening after 7-10 days, new fever, leukocytosis, procalcitonin >0.5, positive bacterial cultures, lobar consolidation (new or worsening)
  • Initial therapy: Ceftriaxone 1-2g IV daily + azithromycin 500mg IV daily (community-acquired pneumonia coverage). Consider vancomycin if MRSA risk (ICU, prior MRSA, necrotizing pneumonia)
  • De-escalate: Stop antibiotics if procalcitonin <0.25 and no bacterial source identified. COVID-19 itself does NOT require antibiotics
  • Bacterial co-infection uncommon: Only 8-15% of hospitalized COVID-19 patients have bacterial co-infection. Secondary bacterial pneumonia more common after 7-10 days (S. aureus, Pseudomonas, Enterobacterales)

Supportive Care:

  • Fluids: Conservative fluid strategy (avoid fluid overload - worsens ARDS). Target euvolemia. Use vasopressors early if shock
  • Vasopressors: Norepinephrine first-line for shock. Target MAP ‚â•65mmHg
  • Sedation/analgesia: Light sedation if mechanically ventilated (target RASS -1 to 0). Daily sedation interruption
  • Neuromuscular blockade: Consider in severe ARDS (P/F <150) with ventilator dyssynchrony. Time-limited (‚â§48hr). Increased risk of ICU weakness
  • Nutrition: Early enteral nutrition (within 48hr). NG/NJ tube if unable to tolerate PO
  • Stress ulcer prophylaxis: PPI for mechanically ventilated patients
  • Glycemic control: Target glucose 140-180mg/dL (avoid hypoglycemia). Hyperglycemia common with dexamethasone

Therapies NO LONGER RECOMMENDED (Ineffective or Harmful):

  • Hydroxychloroquine: NO benefit. DO NOT USE
  • Ivermectin: NO benefit. DO NOT USE
  • Convalescent plasma: NO mortality benefit. Not recommended
  • Vitamin C, vitamin D, zinc: No proven benefit in treatment (prevention data mixed)
  • Azithromycin alone: NO antiviral effect. Only use if bacterial superinfection

Infection Control:

  • Airborne + contact precautions: N95 respirator, gown, gloves, eye protection for all healthcare workers
  • Negative pressure room: Preferred for hospitalized patients, especially if aerosol-generating procedures
  • Duration of isolation: ‚â•10 days from symptom onset AND ‚â•24hr afebrile without antipyretics AND improvement in symptoms. Immunocompromised: ‚â•20 days (or negative PCR). Consult infection control
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Dexamethasone saves lives in severe COVID-19: 30% mortality reduction if requiring oxygen. Give to ALL patients on O‚ÇÇ. Don't give to mild cases not requiring O‚ÇÇ
  • "Silent hypoxemia" is real: Patients can have O‚ÇÇ sat 70-80% with minimal dyspnea. Check pulse ox on ALL suspected COVID-19 patients
  • Timing matters for progression: Most patients who worsen do so around days 7-10. Close monitoring during this window
  • COVID-19 is a hypercoagulable state: Elevated D-dimer in 70%. High VTE risk (10-30%). Low threshold for CT-PE. Prophylactic anticoagulation for all hospitalized patients
  • Bacterial co-infection is uncommon: Only 8-15% at admission. Don't reflexively start antibiotics. Check procalcitonin. Stop if <0.25 and no bacterial source
  • Awake proning works: Have patients on HFNC lie prone 2-4hr multiple times daily. Improves oxygenation and may prevent intubation
  • Most antivirals don't work in severe disease: Remdesivir has modest benefit. Paxlovid is for OUTPATIENTS only (within 5 days). Dexamethasone is the most important drug
  • Avoid fluid overload: COVID-19 ARDS - keep patients dry. Aggressive fluids worsen respiratory status. Use vasopressors early if needed
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • O‚ÇÇ sat <90% despite supplemental O‚ÇÇ >6L/min
  • Need for HFNC, NIPPV, or mechanical ventilation
  • Rapidly increasing oxygen requirements (>2L/hr escalation)
  • RR >30/min with accessory muscle use
  • Altered mental status, confusion, obtundation
  • Septic shock (hypotension requiring vasopressors)
  • P/F ratio <200
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • O‚ÇÇ sat ‚â•92% on room air at rest and with ambulation (or baseline if chronic lung disease)
  • Afebrile ‚â•24hr without antipyretics
  • Respiratory rate <24/min at rest
  • Clinical improvement (resolving symptoms)
  • Completed minimum 5 days of treatment (dexamethasone)
  • Able to perform ADLs independently
  • Adequate home support, especially if elderly/high-risk
  • Follow-up arranged (telehealth or in-person within 3-7 days)
  • Pulse oximeter for home monitoring if marginal O‚ÇÇ sats
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute (During Hospitalization): ARDS (20-40% of hospitalized patients, high mortality), secondary bacterial/fungal infections (aspergillosis, candidemia after prolonged steroids/ICU stay), thromboembolic disease (PE, DVT, stroke, MI - 10-30% incidence), acute kidney injury (20-40%, poor prognosis), myocardial injury (troponin elevation, myocarditis, stress cardiomyopathy), arrhythmias, shock (septic, cardiogenic), multiorgan failure

Subacute/Chronic (Post-Discharge): Post-COVID syndrome/"Long COVID" (30% of hospitalized patients - fatigue, dyspnea, cognitive impairment, lasting months), pulmonary fibrosis (after severe ARDS), chronic thromboembolic disease, persistent cardiac dysfunction (reduced EF, myocarditis sequelae), post-ICU syndrome (weakness, PTSD, depression), organizing pneumonia (can occur weeks post-infection)

Follow-up: All hospitalized patients should have follow-up within 1-2 weeks post-discharge. Assess for persistent symptoms, oxygen needs, functional status. Chest imaging (CXR or CT) at 4-12 weeks if severe disease or persistent symptoms. Pulmonary function testing if dyspnea persists. Consider post-COVID clinic referral for prolonged symptoms

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

Clostridioides difficile Colitis

[Content to be added in Phase 4]

58. UTI/PYELONEPHRITIS (COMPLICATED)

Upper Urinary Tract Infection ‚Ä¢ Sepsis Risk ‚Ä¢ Broad-Spectrum Antibiotics ‚Üí Narrow
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Pyelonephritis (Acute Kidney Infection): Bacterial infection of the renal parenchyma and collecting system. Can lead to sepsis, renal abscess, or chronic kidney disease if untreated

Complicated vs Uncomplicated UTI/Pyelonephritis:

  • UNCOMPLICATED: Otherwise healthy, non-pregnant women with no structural/functional urinary tract abnormalities. Typically outpatient management
  • COMPLICATED (Requires Hospitalization): Men, pregnant women, structural abnormalities (stones, obstruction, neurogenic bladder, indwelling catheter), functional abnormalities (vesicoureteral reflux), immunocompromised, diabetes, renal transplant, recent instrumentation, recent antibiotic use, or signs of sepsis/severe illness

Classic Pyelonephritis Presentation:

  • Fever: High-grade (>38.5¬∞C), rigors, chills
  • Flank pain: Unilateral or bilateral. Costovertebral angle (CVA) tenderness on exam (classic finding)
  • Lower urinary tract symptoms: Dysuria, frequency, urgency (may or may not be present)
  • GI symptoms: Nausea, vomiting (50%), anorexia
  • Systemic: Malaise, myalgias, headache

Indications for Hospitalization (Complicated Pyelonephritis):

  • Sepsis/systemic illness: Hypotension, tachycardia, altered mental status, lactate elevation, signs of organ dysfunction
  • Unable to tolerate PO: Severe nausea/vomiting, dehydration
  • Pregnancy: ALL pregnant women with pyelonephritis should be hospitalized
  • Urinary obstruction: Stones, mass, anatomic abnormality requiring intervention
  • Immunocompromised: Diabetes, HIV, transplant, chronic steroids, neutropenia
  • Recent hospitalization/antibiotics: Risk of resistant organisms (ESBL, Pseudomonas, Enterococcus)
  • Indwelling catheter/recent instrumentation: Higher risk of complications
  • Failed outpatient therapy: No improvement after 48-72hr of oral antibiotics
  • Age extremes: Elderly (>65), frail patients with limited support

Severe Complications (Requiring ICU):

  • Urosepsis: Septic shock requiring vasopressors. High mortality if obstructed
