  • Renal abscess: Fever persisting >72hr despite antibiotics. Requires drainage
  • Perinephric abscess: Extension beyond renal capsule. More severe than renal abscess
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis: Gas-forming infection (diabetes, obstruction). Life-threatening. May require nephrectomy
  • Papillary necrosis: Ischemic necrosis of renal papillae (diabetes, sickle cell, NSAIDs, obstruction)
  • Acute kidney injury: From infection, dehydration, sepsis

Common Pathogens:

  • E. coli (75-80%): Most common cause. Increasing rates of fluoroquinolone resistance, ESBL producers
  • Klebsiella: ESBL-producing strains increasingly common
  • Proteus mirabilis: Associated with struvite stones (triple phosphate)
  • Enterococcus: Healthcare-associated infections, instrumentation, catheters. VRE risk
  • Pseudomonas aeruginosa: Recent antibiotics, healthcare-associated, structural abnormalities
  • Staphylococcus aureus: Hematogenous spread (bacteremia, endocarditis). Consider if bacteremia/endocarditis
  • Candida species: Prolonged catheterization, recent antibiotics, diabetes, immunocompromised
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Urinalysis (UA) with Microscopy:

  • Pyuria: WBC >10/hpf (or >10 WBC/¬µL on dipstick). Highly sensitive but NOT specific (can be from any inflammation)
  • Bacteriuria: Bacteria present on microscopy or dipstick positive for leukocyte esterase/nitrites
  • WBC casts: SPECIFIC for pyelonephritis (upper tract infection). Indicates renal parenchymal involvement
  • Hematuria: Microscopic or gross (present in 30-50%)
  • Proteinuria: Mild (trace to 1+). NOT nephrotic range
  • Nitrites: Positive if Gram-negative bacteria (E. coli, Klebsiella, Proteus). False negatives with Enterococcus, Pseudomonas, Staph

Urine Culture & Sensitivity (ESSENTIAL):

  • Obtain BEFORE starting antibiotics: Critical for identifying organism and tailoring therapy
  • Collection: Clean-catch midstream (females: spread labia, males: retract foreskin). Catheterized specimen if unable to provide clean catch
  • Interpretation: ‚â•10‚Åµ CFU/mL = significant bacteriuria. ‚â•10‚Å¥ CFU/mL acceptable in symptomatic patients or catheterized specimens
  • Results: Preliminary at 24hr (Gram stain, growth), final at 48-72hr (organism, sensitivities)

Blood Cultures: Obtain in ALL hospitalized patients with pyelonephritis. Bacteremia present in 10-30%. Positive cultures indicate higher risk of complications, metastatic infection. Draw 2 sets from separate sites before antibiotics

Labs:

  • CBC: Leukocytosis (WBC >15,000 suggests severe infection). Bandemia (left shift) indicates bacterial infection
  • CMP: BUN/Cr (assess renal function, AKI), electrolytes (hypokalemia from vomiting)
  • Lactate: If sepsis suspected. >2mmol/L = tissue hypoperfusion. >4mmol/L = severe sepsis
  • Procalcitonin: Elevated (>0.5ng/mL) in bacterial infection. Helps differentiate from colonization/contamination

Imaging (When to Obtain):

  • CT abdomen/pelvis with IV contrast (CT urogram): GOLD STANDARD for complicated pyelonephritis. Obtain if: (1) No improvement after 48-72hr of antibiotics, (2) Sepsis/severe illness, (3) Suspected obstruction (hydronephrosis), (4) Suspected abscess, (5) Recurrent pyelonephritis, (6) History of stones, (7) Immunocompromised, (8) Men (higher risk of structural abnormality), (9) Pregnancy (but use MRI instead - no radiation)
  • Renal ultrasound: First-line in pregnancy. Can detect hydronephrosis, large stones, abscess. Less sensitive than CT
  • MRI: Alternative to CT if contrast contraindicated or pregnancy. No radiation

Imaging Findings:

  • Pyelonephritis: Wedge-shaped areas of decreased enhancement (striated nephrogram), renal enlargement, perinephric fat stranding
  • Obstruction: Hydronephrosis (dilated collecting system), hydroureter, obstructing stone
  • Abscess: Focal fluid collection with rim enhancement (renal or perinephric)
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis: Gas within renal parenchyma (surgical emergency)

When NOT to Image Initially: Young, otherwise healthy women with classic pyelonephritis symptoms, normal vital signs, no prior history of stones/urologic issues, who are responding to antibiotics within 48-72hr. Can manage without imaging

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

Empiric Antibiotic Therapy (Start IMMEDIATELY after cultures):

  • FIRST-LINE (No Recent Antibiotics, No Risk Factors for Resistance):
    • Ceftriaxone 1-2g IV daily: Excellent Gram-negative coverage. Safe, well-tolerated. Preferred first-line
    • Alternatives: Cefepime 1-2g IV q8-12hr (broader, covers Pseudomonas), Ertapenem 1g IV daily (ESBL coverage, carbapenem-sparing), Piperacillin-tazobactam 3.375g IV q6hr or 4.5g IV q8hr (Pseudomonas coverage)
  • RISK FACTORS FOR RESISTANCE (Recent antibiotics within 3mo, healthcare-associated, recent hospitalization, known ESBL colonization):
    • Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6hr: Covers ESBL (inoculum effect), Pseudomonas, Enterococcus
    • Cefepime 2g IV q8hr: Broader than ceftriaxone, covers Pseudomonas
    • Carbapenems (if high ESBL prevalence or documented ESBL): Meropenem 1g IV q8hr OR Imipenem 500mg IV q6hr. Reserve for documented ESBL or severe sepsis with high ESBL risk
  • ADD VANCOMYCIN 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hr (target trough 15-20) IF:
    • Risk for Enterococcus (recent antibiotics, instrumentation, catheter, chronic kidney disease)
    • Gram-positive cocci in blood cultures
    • Severe sepsis/septic shock with unknown organism
  • ADD ANTIFUNGAL (Fluconazole 400mg IV daily) IF:
    • Yeast/Candida on UA or culture
    • Prolonged catheterization, diabetes, recent antibiotics, immunocompromised
    • Consider micafungin 100mg IV daily if candidemia or critically ill

Antibiotic De-escalation & Duration:

  • Step 1 (24-48hr): Review culture results. Narrow to targeted therapy based on sensitivities
  • Step 2 (48-72hr): If clinically improved (afebrile, improved symptoms), transition to PO antibiotics to complete course
  • PO Step-Down Options (based on sensitivities):
    • Fluoroquinolones: Ciprofloxacin 500mg PO BID OR Levofloxacin 750mg PO daily (excellent bioavailability, tissue penetration)
    • Cephalosporins: Cefpodoxime 200mg PO BID OR Cefixime 400mg PO daily (3rd-generation oral cephalosporin)
    • TMP-SMX: 1 DS (160/800mg) PO BID (if susceptible, no longer first-line due to resistance)
    • Amoxicillin-clavulanate: 875/125mg PO BID (if susceptible, covers E. coli, Enterococcus)
  • Duration: 5-7 days for uncomplicated pyelonephritis responding to therapy. 10-14 days for complicated pyelonephritis (men, obstruction, abscess, bacteremia, immunocompromised, slow response)
  • IV-to-PO criteria: Afebrile >24hr, able to tolerate PO, hemodynamically stable, clinical improvement, organism identified with PO-sensitive antibiotic available

Management of Obstruction (URGENT):

  • Obstructed infected system = EMERGENCY: High mortality if not drained urgently. Antibiotics alone will FAIL
  • Indications for urgent decompression: Hydronephrosis + fever/sepsis, obstructing stone with infection, pyonephrosis (pus in collecting system)
  • Decompression options:
    • Percutaneous nephrostomy tube (PCN): Placed by interventional radiology. Immediate drainage. Preferred if unstable, coagulopathy, or failed cystoscopy
    • Ureteral stent (double-J stent): Placed by urology cystoscopically. Allows internal drainage. Preferred if stable and feasible
  • Timing: Decompression within 24hr of diagnosis. Don't delay for OR scheduling. Can be done at bedside (PCN) or in cysto suite

Management of Abscess:

  • Renal abscess <3cm: May respond to antibiotics alone. Monitor closely with repeat imaging
  • Renal abscess >3-5cm OR perinephric abscess: Requires drainage. Percutaneous drainage (IR-guided) vs surgical
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis: SURGICAL EMERGENCY. High mortality. Requires nephrectomy in severe cases. ICU, broad-spectrum antibiotics, urgent urology/surgery consult

Supportive Care:

  • IV fluids: NS or LR 1-2L bolus if dehydrated/septic, then 100-150mL/hr maintenance. Monitor urine output (goal >0.5mL/kg/hr)
  • Antipyretics: Acetaminophen 650-1000mg q6hr PRN or ibuprofen 400-600mg q6hr PRN for fever/pain
  • Antiemetics: Ondansetron 4-8mg IV q8hr PRN for nausea/vomiting
  • Analgesia: NSAIDs (ketorolac 15-30mg IV q6hr √ó 48hr max) or opioids (hydromorphone 0.5-1mg IV q4hr PRN) for flank pain
  • Urinary alkalization (if Proteus): Potassium citrate or sodium bicarbonate. Helps prevent stone formation (Proteus produces urease ‚Üí alkaline urine ‚Üí struvite stones)

Monitoring Response to Therapy:

  • Clinical improvement expected by 48-72hr: Defervescence, improved pain, stable vital signs
  • If NOT improving by 72hr: Obtain imaging (CT) to rule out obstruction, abscess, resistant organism. Review culture sensitivities. Broaden antibiotics if needed
  • Daily assessment: Vital signs, symptom improvement, tolerance of PO, urine output
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • WBC casts = pyelonephritis: Specific for upper tract infection (renal parenchymal involvement). Differentiates pyelonephritis from cystitis
  • Obstructed infected kidney = EMERGENCY: Septic shock, high mortality if not drained within 24hr. Get imaging early if high suspicion. Call urology/IR urgently
  • All hospitalized pyelo patients need blood AND urine cultures: 10-30% have bacteremia. Positive blood cultures = higher risk of complications, metastatic infection, longer treatment needed
  • Don't rely on UA alone: Pyuria (WBCs in urine) is sensitive but NOT specific. Can be from STI, contamination, catheter, sterile inflammation. Must have positive culture to diagnose UTI
  • CT if not improving in 72hr: Persistent fever despite antibiotics = obstruction, abscess, resistant organism, or wrong diagnosis. Don't wait - get imaging
  • Start broad, narrow quickly: Empiric therapy should cover E. coli, Klebsiella, Enterococcus (if catheter/instrumentation). Narrow to targeted therapy at 24-48hr when cultures result
  • Fluoroquinolones are NOT first-line anymore: High resistance rates (20-30% E. coli resistance). Use ceftriaxone first-line. Reserve fluoroquinolones for step-down therapy if susceptible
  • Men with pyelonephritis need imaging: Higher risk of structural abnormality (stones, obstruction, prostate disease). Always image men, especially first episode
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Septic shock (hypotension requiring vasopressors)
  • Lactate >4mmol/L or rising
  • Altered mental status, confusion
  • Acute kidney injury with oliguria (UOP <0.5mL/kg/hr)
  • Respiratory distress (ARDS from sepsis)
  • Emphysematous pyelonephritis (gas in kidney on imaging)
  • Obstructed infected system not responding to decompression
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Afebrile ‚â•24hr without antipyretics
  • Hemodynamically stable (no tachycardia, normal BP)
  • Tolerating PO intake and medications
  • Pain controlled with PO analgesics
  • Clinically improving (resolving flank pain, nausea)
  • Urine culture with identified organism and sensitivities
  • Appropriate PO antibiotic available to complete course (5-14 days)
  • No obstruction or abscess requiring intervention (or successfully treated)
  • Follow-up arranged (PCP or urology within 7-14 days)
  • Repeat UA/culture in 1-2 weeks if symptoms persist
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute: Urosepsis/septic shock (10-15%, high mortality if obstructed), acute kidney injury (15-25%, from ATN, obstruction, or direct infection), renal abscess (2-10%, requires drainage if >3-5cm), perinephric abscess (extension beyond Gerota's fascia, more severe), emphysematous pyelonephritis (gas-forming infection, diabetics, 20% mortality, may need nephrectomy), papillary necrosis (ischemic necrosis of papillae - diabetes, sickle cell, NSAIDs), bacteremia/metastatic infection (endocarditis, vertebral osteomyelitis, epidural abscess if S. aureus)

Chronic: Recurrent pyelonephritis (10-30% within 1 year - investigate for structural abnormality, stones, vesicoureteral reflux), chronic pyelonephritis (repeated infections ‚Üí scarring ‚Üí CKD), renal scarring (especially children with VUR), hypertension (from renal damage), xanthogranulomatous pyelonephritis (chronic destructive process, mimics renal cell carcinoma on imaging)

Special Populations: Pregnant women - risk of preterm labor, low birth weight (all pregnant women with pyelo should be hospitalized). Kidney transplant recipients - risk of graft dysfunction, rejection (treat aggressively). Diabetes - higher risk of emphysematous pyelonephritis, abscess, worse outcomes

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

59. SKIN/SOFT TISSUE INFECTIONS (CELLULITIS, NECROTIZING FASCIITIS)

Bacterial Skin Infection ‚Ä¢ Cellulitis vs Necrotizing ‚Ä¢ Early Surgery Saves Lives
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Cellulitis (Non-Necrotizing SSTI): Acute bacterial infection of dermis and subcutaneous tissue. Characterized by erythema, warmth, swelling, tenderness. Usually unilateral lower extremity

Cellulitis Presentation:

  • Erythema: Red, poorly demarcated area. Spreads centrifugally from entry site
  • Warmth: Affected area warm to touch compared to surrounding skin
  • Swelling: Edema of affected limb. May have peau d'orange appearance
  • Tenderness: Pain with palpation. Pain usually proportionate to appearance
  • Systemic symptoms (50%): Fever, chills, malaise. More common in severe cases
  • Regional lymphadenopathy: Inguinal nodes (lower extremity cellulitis), axillary nodes (upper extremity)
  • Lymphangitis (30%): Red linear streaking from site toward regional lymph nodes

Common Sites: Lower extremities (60-70%), upper extremities (20%), face (5-10%), trunk (rare). Look for portal of entry (trauma, insect bite, tinea pedis, chronic venous insufficiency, lymphedema)

Risk Factors for Cellulitis: Tinea pedis (athlete's foot - toe web spaces), venous insufficiency, lymphedema (prior surgery, radiation), obesity, diabetes, peripheral vascular disease, chronic kidney disease, immunosuppression, IV drug use, prior cellulitis (recurrence rate 20-30%)

NECROTIZING SOFT TISSUE INFECTIONS (SURGICAL EMERGENCY):

  • Necrotizing Fasciitis (NF): Rapidly progressive infection of fascia and subcutaneous tissue with necrosis. High mortality (20-30%). REQUIRES URGENT SURGICAL DEBRIDEMENT
  • Type I NF (Polymicrobial, 70-80%): Mixed aerobic + anaerobic bacteria (Streptococcus, E. coli, Bacteroides, Clostridium). Risk factors: diabetes, peripheral vascular disease, obesity, immunocompromised. Often post-surgical, perineal (Fournier's gangrene)
  • Type II NF (Monomicrobial, 20-30%): Group A Streptococcus (Strep pyogenes) OR Staphylococcus aureus (including CA-MRSA). Younger, healthier patients. Post-trauma, varicella, NSAIDs use

RED FLAGS for Necrotizing Fasciitis (Think NF!):

  • Severe pain OUT OF PROPORTION to physical findings: CLASSIC. Severe pain but skin may look relatively benign initially
  • Rapidly progressive erythema/swelling: Spreads within hours, not days
  • Systemic toxicity: High fever, tachycardia (HR >120), hypotension, altered mental status, septic shock
  • Skin changes (LATE findings): Dusky/purple discoloration, hemorrhagic bullae (blood-filled blisters), crepitus (gas in soft tissue), skin necrosis, anesthesia (nerve involvement)
  • Wooden hard induration: Firm, boardlike feel to subcutaneous tissue
  • Gas on imaging: Crepitus on exam or gas in soft tissue on X-ray/CT
  • Lab abnormalities: WBC >15,000, lactate >4, Cr elevated, CK elevated (myonecrosis), severe metabolic acidosis, thrombocytopenia

LRINEC Score (Laboratory Risk Indicator for Necrotizing Fasciitis): CRP ‚â•150mg/L (4 pts), WBC <15 or >25K (1-2 pts), Hgb <11g/dL (2 pts), Na <135mEq/L (2 pts), Cr >1.6mg/dL (2 pts), glucose >180mg/dL (1 pt). Score ‚â•6 = intermediate risk (50% PPV), ‚â•8 = high risk (75% PPV) for NF. HOWEVER: Clinical suspicion trumps score. Don't delay surgery for scoring

Other Necrotizing Infections:

  • Fournier's Gangrene: Necrotizing fasciitis of perineum/genitalia. Diabetics, elderly men. Polymicrobial. Extremely high mortality (20-40%)
  • Clostridial Myonecrosis (Gas Gangrene): Clostridium perfringens. Post-trauma (crush injury), post-surgical, spontaneous (malignancy, immunocompromised). Crepitus, serosanguinous discharge, rapid progression. Very high mortality
  • Streptococcal Toxic Shock Syndrome (STSS): Group A Strep with necrotizing fasciitis + shock + multiorgan failure. Mortality >30%

Common Pathogens by Infection Type:

  • Non-purulent cellulitis: Œ≤-hemolytic Streptococcus (Group A, C, G most common), S. aureus (less common)
  • Purulent cellulitis (abscess): S. aureus (including MRSA)
  • Water exposure: Vibrio vulnificus (seawater), Aeromonas (freshwater)
  • Animal bites: Pasteurella (cats/dogs), Capnocytophaga (dogs), Eikenella (human bites)
  • Immunocompromised: Pseudomonas, fungal (Mucor, Rhizopus)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Clinical Diagnosis (Cellulitis): Diagnosis is clinical based on characteristic findings (erythema, warmth, swelling, tenderness). Imaging and lab tests NOT routinely needed for uncomplicated cellulitis

Labs (for Severe or Complicated Infections):

  • CBC: Leukocytosis (WBC >15,000 suggests severe infection or necrotizing process). Bandemia (left shift). Thrombocytopenia (in sepsis, necrotizing infection)
  • CMP: BUN/Cr (assess renal function, AKI in sepsis), glucose (hyperglycemia in stress/infection, especially diabetes)
  • CRP: Elevated in bacterial infection. CRP >150mg/L increases suspicion for necrotizing infection (LRINEC score component)
  • CK: Markedly elevated (>1,000) suggests myonecrosis (muscle involvement). Concerning for clostridial myonecrosis or necrotizing infection
  • Lactate: >2mmol/L suggests hypoperfusion. >4mmol/L = severe sepsis. Associated with necrotizing infection
  • Blood cultures: Obtain in: fever + systemic toxicity, immunocompromised, diabetic with severe infection, suspected necrotizing infection, hemodynamic instability. Positive in only 5-10% of cellulitis cases

Microbiology (Limited Role in Cellulitis):

  • Blood cultures: Low yield (5-10%) in uncomplicated cellulitis. Obtain if septic, immunocompromised, or severe infection
  • Wound culture: Only if purulent drainage present (abscess). Swab of intact skin NOT useful (contaminated). Aspirate of abscess preferred
  • Tissue biopsy/culture: Reserved for: (1) Failed empiric therapy, (2) Immunocompromised (atypical organisms), (3) Surgical debridement (send tissue for culture)

Imaging:

  • Plain X-ray: Limited utility. Can detect gas in soft tissue (crepitus), foreign body, osteomyelitis (if concern for bone involvement). ALWAYS obtain if crepitus present
  • Ultrasound: Can differentiate cellulitis from abscess (hypoechoic fluid collection). Useful at bedside. "Cobblestoning" pattern suggests abscess. Can guide drainage
  • CT with IV contrast: GOLD STANDARD for necrotizing infections. Obtain urgently if high suspicion for NF. Findings: gas in fascial planes, fascial thickening, fluid tracking along fascial planes, lack of enhancement (necrosis), muscle involvement
  • MRI: Most sensitive for soft tissue infections. Can differentiate cellulitis, abscess, necrotizing fasciitis, myositis. Useful for deep infections, spinal/pelvic infections. NOT needed for routine cellulitis. Too slow if suspecting NF (do CT, then OR)

When to Image:

  • URGENT CT (within 1-2hr): Suspected necrotizing fasciitis (pain out of proportion, crepitus, skin necrosis, septic shock, LRINEC ‚â•6)
  • Ultrasound: Suspected abscess (fluctuance, purulent drainage). Guide I&D
  • X-ray: Crepitus on exam, foreign body, suspected osteomyelitis (bone tenderness, chronic wound)
  • NO imaging needed: Uncomplicated cellulitis with clear diagnosis, no red flags

Surgical Exploration (Diagnostic & Therapeutic): If high suspicion for NF but CT equivocal, proceed to OR for direct visualization. "Finger test" - blunt dissection along fascial planes. In NF, fascia separates easily ("dishwater" fluid, necrotic tissue, lack of bleeding). Don't delay surgery waiting for imaging

TREATMENT PROTOCOL

CELLULITIS (Non-Necrotizing) Management:

Non-Purulent Cellulitis (No Abscess):

  • Mild (outpatient): Cephalexin 500mg PO QID √ó 5-7 days OR Dicloxacillin 500mg PO QID OR Cefadroxil 1g PO BID
  • Moderate-Severe (hospitalized, no MRSA risk):
    • Cefazolin 1-2g IV q8hr: First-line. Excellent Strep/MSSA coverage
    • Alternatives: Nafcillin 1-2g IV q4-6hr, Oxacillin 1-2g IV q4-6hr
    • Penicillin allergy (non-anaphylactic): Ceftriaxone 1-2g IV daily (low cross-reactivity)
    • Penicillin anaphylaxis: Clindamycin 600-900mg IV q8hr OR Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hr

MRSA Risk Factors: Prior MRSA infection, injection drug use, incarceration, close contact with MRSA-infected person, purulent drainage, abscess

Purulent Cellulitis/Abscess OR MRSA Risk:

  • Abscess management: Incision & drainage (I&D) is PRIMARY treatment. Antibiotics are ADJUNCT if surrounding cellulitis or systemic symptoms
  • Empiric MRSA coverage (if systemic symptoms or extensive cellulitis):
    • Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hr (target trough 15-20): First-line IV MRSA coverage
    • Alternatives: Linezolid 600mg IV/PO q12hr, Daptomycin 4-6mg/kg IV daily (NOT for pneumonia), Ceftaroline 600mg IV q12hr
  • Outpatient MRSA coverage (mild purulent cellulitis post-I&D): TMP-SMX 1-2 DS PO BID + Cephalexin 500mg QID (for Strep coverage) OR Doxycycline 100mg PO BID OR Clindamycin 300-450mg PO TID

NECROTIZING FASCIITIS Management (SURGICAL EMERGENCY):

Step 1: URGENT SURGICAL CONSULTATION (STAT): Surgery is LIFE-SAVING. Time to OR directly correlates with mortality. Goal: debridement within 6 hours of diagnosis. "Time is tissue, tissue is life"

Step 2: Aggressive Resuscitation & Support:

  • IV fluids: 30mL/kg crystalloid bolus (NS or LR) within first hour. Continue aggressive fluid resuscitation. May need 4-10L in first 24hr
  • Vasopressors: Norepinephrine if hypotensive despite fluids. Target MAP ‚â•65mmHg
  • ICU admission: ALL suspected/confirmed NF patients
  • Blood cultures: 2 sets before antibiotics
  • Lactate: Follow serial lactates q2-4hr. Goal: lactate clearance (normalize within 6hr)

Step 3: BROAD-SPECTRUM Antibiotics (Start IMMEDIATELY, before OR):

  • Empiric Regimen for Necrotizing Fasciitis:
    • Vancomycin 15-20mg/kg IV q8-12hr (for MRSA, Strep)
    • PLUS Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6hr (broad Gram-neg, anaerobic coverage)
    • PLUS Clindamycin 600-900mg IV q8hr (CRITICAL - stops toxin production in Group A Strep, covers anaerobes, penetrates necrotic tissue)
  • Alternative regimens:
    • Vancomycin + Meropenem 1g IV q8hr + Clindamycin (if severe sepsis, immunocompromised)
    • Vancomycin + Ceftriaxone 2g IV daily + Metronidazole 500mg IV q8hr + Clindamycin
  • If Clostridial myonecrosis suspected (gas gangrene):
    • Penicillin G 4 million units IV q4hr + Clindamycin 900mg IV q8hr
    • Consider hyperbaric oxygen therapy (HBOT) if available (controversial, delays surgery)

Step 4: Surgical Debridement (URGENT):

  • Aggressive wide debridement: Remove ALL necrotic tissue, fascia, muscle. May require multiple trips to OR (second-look within 24-48hr). Debride until healthy bleeding tissue
  • Amputation: May be necessary for limb salvage if extensive involvement or refractory sepsis
  • Fournier's gangrene: Debride perineum, scrotum. Diverting colostomy if rectal involvement
  • Negative pressure wound therapy (VAC): After debridement. Promotes healing, facilitates subsequent closures

Step 5: Adjunctive Therapies (for Streptococcal Toxic Shock):

  • IVIG (Intravenous Immunoglobulin) 1-2g/kg IV once: Consider in Group A Strep NF with toxic shock syndrome. Neutralizes streptococcal superantigens. May reduce mortality (controversial, limited data)
  • Clindamycin is ESSENTIAL: Inhibits toxin production (unlike Œ≤-lactams which increase toxin release as bacteria lyse)

Duration of Antibiotics:

  • Uncomplicated cellulitis: 5-7 days (or until clinical improvement + afebrile 48hr)
  • Severe cellulitis: 7-10 days
  • Necrotizing fasciitis: 10-14 days (or longer until all necrotic tissue debrided and clinically improving)

Supportive Care for Cellulitis:

  • Elevation: Elevate affected limb above heart level. Reduces edema, improves lymphatic drainage
  • Mark borders: Outline erythema with marker to track progression/improvement
  • Analgesia: NSAIDs (ibuprofen 400-600mg PO q6-8hr) or acetaminophen 650-1000mg q6hr. Avoid NSAIDs in Group A Strep (theoretical increased risk of NF progression - controversial)
  • Treat predisposing conditions: Tinea pedis (antifungal cream), venous stasis (compression), edema (diuretics if CHF), optimize diabetes control
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • Pain out of proportion = necrotizing fasciitis until proven otherwise: Don't be fooled by benign-appearing skin. Severe pain with minimal skin findings is CLASSIC for NF. Call surgery immediately
  • Crepitus = gas in tissue = EMERGENCY: Crepitus (crackling feel) on palpation indicates gas-forming organisms (Clostridium, Streptococcus, polymicrobial NF). Get imaging (X-ray or CT) and call surgery STAT
  • Time to OR = survival in NF: Mortality increases 7-9% for every hour delay to surgery. Don't wait for CT if high clinical suspicion. "Stabilize in the OR, not the ICU"
  • Clindamycin is critical in Group A Strep NF: Stops toxin production (Œ≤-lactams don't). Add clindamycin to all suspected NF empiric regimens
  • Most cellulitis is streptococcal, not MRSA: Non-purulent cellulitis without risk factors ‚Üí don't need MRSA coverage. Cefazolin is appropriate. Reserve vancomycin for purulent infections or MRSA risk factors
  • Blood cultures are low-yield in cellulitis: Only 5-10% positive. Reserve for severe cases, sepsis, immunocompromised
  • Abscess = I&D, not just antibiotics: Antibiotics don't penetrate abscesses well. I&D is primary treatment. Antibiotics only needed if surrounding cellulitis or systemic symptoms
  • LRINEC score helps but don't rely on it: Score ‚â•6 raises suspicion for NF. But NF can occur with low scores. Clinical gestalt and imaging trump the score
  • Treat the source: Tinea pedis (athlete's foot) is the most common portal of entry for lower extremity cellulitis. Treat with topical antifungals to prevent recurrence
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • ANY suspicion for necrotizing fasciitis
  • Septic shock (hypotension requiring vasopressors)
  • Severe pain out of proportion to exam findings
  • Skin necrosis, hemorrhagic bullae, crepitus
  • Lactate >4mmol/L
  • Altered mental status, confusion
  • WBC >25,000 or <4,000
  • Rapidly progressive erythema (spreading over hours)
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Afebrile ‚â•24hr without antipyretics
  • Erythema improving (use marked borders to track)
  • Decreased swelling and tenderness
  • Able to ambulate (if lower extremity)
  • Tolerating PO antibiotics
  • Appropriate outpatient antibiotic regimen selected
  • Source control achieved (abscess drained, if applicable)
  • Follow-up arranged (PCP or ID within 3-5 days)
  • Patient educated on warning signs (worsening erythema, fever, systemic symptoms)
  • Predisposing factors addressed (tinea pedis treatment, wound care)
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Cellulitis Complications: Abscess formation (requires I&D), bacteremia/sepsis (5-10%), lymphangitis (ascending infection toward lymph nodes), recurrent cellulitis (20-30% within 3 years - higher if lymphedema, chronic venous insufficiency, or tinea pedis not treated), chronic lymphedema (from repeated episodes damaging lymphatics), post-cellulitis venous thrombosis (thrombophlebitis)

Necrotizing Fasciitis Complications: Septic shock (30-50%, high mortality), multiorgan failure (renal, respiratory, cardiac), limb loss (amputation needed in 20-30%), extensive scarring/disfigurement, chronic pain (neuropathic from nerve damage), functional impairment (prolonged rehabilitation), mortality (overall 20-30%, up to 50% if shock, 70-80% if clostridial myonecrosis)

Prevention of Recurrent Cellulitis: Treat tinea pedis aggressively (topical or oral antifungals - terbinafine 250mg daily √ó 2-4 weeks), compression stockings for venous insufficiency/lymphedema, elevate legs regularly, emollients for dry/cracked skin, prompt treatment of skin injuries/wounds, consider antibiotic prophylaxis if ‚â•3 episodes per year (penicillin VK 250-500mg daily OR erythromycin 250mg daily)

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

60. TUBERCULOSIS (ACTIVE DISEASE)

Mycobacterium tuberculosis ‚Ä¢ Pulmonary + Extrapulmonary ‚Ä¢ Multi-Drug Regimen ‚Ä¢ Airborne Isolation
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Tuberculosis (TB) Overview: Infectious disease caused by Mycobacterium tuberculosis. Transmitted via airborne droplets. Can affect lungs (pulmonary TB, 80-85%) or other organs (extrapulmonary TB, 15-20%). Highly contagious. Public health emergency requiring isolation and reporting

Latent TB vs Active TB:

  • Latent TB Infection (LTBI): Infected with M. tuberculosis but no symptoms, not contagious. Positive TST/IGRA, normal CXR. 5-10% lifetime risk of reactivation (higher if immunocompromised)
  • Active TB Disease: Symptomatic, contagious (if pulmonary/laryngeal), abnormal CXR, positive AFB smear/culture. Requires multi-drug treatment

PULMONARY TB (Most Common, 80-85%):

Classic Presentation (Subacute/Chronic):

  • Chronic cough: ‚â•3 weeks duration. Initially dry, becomes productive. Hallmark symptom
  • Hemoptysis: Blood-tinged sputum (25-50%). Can be massive in cavitary disease
  • Constitutional symptoms ("B symptoms"): Fever (low-grade, evening), night sweats (drenching, change clothes), weight loss (>10% body weight), anorexia, malaise, fatigue
  • Chest pain: Pleuritic (if pleural involvement), dull ache
  • Dyspnea: If extensive disease, pleural effusion, or miliary TB

Physical Exam (Often Unremarkable): May have rales/crackles over involved lung fields, dullness to percussion (consolidation, effusion), amphoric breath sounds (cavity), cachexia (advanced disease). Exam may be entirely normal despite extensive radiographic disease

EXTRAPULMONARY TB (15-20%, Higher in HIV/Immunocompromised):

  • Tuberculous Lymphadenitis (Most Common EPTB): Painless, firm, matted cervical or supraclavicular lymph nodes. May suppurate (cold abscess). Slow progression over weeks-months
  • Pleural TB: Exudative pleural effusion. Pleuritic chest pain, dyspnea. Effusion often lymphocyte-predominant. Pleural fluid ADA (adenosine deaminase) >40 U/L highly suggestive
  • Tuberculous Meningitis (TB Meningitis): Subacute headache, fever, altered mental status, cranial nerve palsies (especially CN VI), seizures. CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, low glucose, high protein. HIGH MORTALITY (20-50%) even with treatment
  • Miliary TB (Disseminated): Hematogenous spread to multiple organs. High fever, weight loss, hepatosplenomegaly. CXR: bilateral diffuse miliary (millet seed) nodules (1-3mm). Often in HIV, elderly, immunocompromised
  • Skeletal TB (Pott's Disease): Vertebral osteomyelitis (thoracic/lumbar spine most common). Back pain, paravertebral abscess, cord compression, gibbus deformity. MRI shows vertebral destruction, abscess
  • Genitourinary TB: Dysuria, hematuria, flank pain, sterile pyuria. Can cause ureteral strictures, hydronephrosis, chronic kidney disease
  • Pericardial TB: Pericardial effusion, tamponade, constrictive pericarditis (chronic). High mortality
  • Abdominal TB: Peritonitis (ascites, abdominal pain), intestinal involvement (chronic diarrhea, obstruction, perforation). Ascites: high protein, lymphocyte-predominant, ADA >39 U/L

High-Risk Populations:

  • HIV/AIDS (CD4 <200): 10-20x increased risk. More likely disseminated/extrapulmonary TB. TB accelerates HIV progression
  • Immunosuppression: TNF-Œ± inhibitors (infliximab, adalimumab - screen before starting), transplant recipients, chronic steroids (‚â•15mg prednisone daily), chemotherapy
  • Close contact: Household contact with active TB case (20-30% infection rate)
  • Endemic areas: India, China, Southeast Asia, Sub-Saharan Africa, Latin America, Eastern Europe
  • Congregate settings: Homeless shelters, prisons, nursing homes
  • Healthcare workers: Exposure to undiagnosed TB patients
  • Other: Diabetes, chronic kidney disease, silicosis, malignancy, malnutrition, alcoholism, IV drug use

Drug-Resistant TB:

  • Multidrug-Resistant TB (MDR-TB): Resistant to isoniazid (INH) + rifampin (RIF). 5% of new cases globally. Higher in HIV, prior TB treatment. Requires ‚â•18-24 months treatment. Lower cure rates (50-70%)
  • Extensively Drug-Resistant TB (XDR-TB): MDR-TB + resistance to fluoroquinolones + second-line injectable (amikacin, kanamycin, capreomycin). Very poor prognosis. Mortality 30-50%
  • Risk factors for resistance: Prior TB treatment, non-adherence, endemic areas (Russia, South Africa, India), HIV co-infection, close contact with known drug-resistant case
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Tuberculin Skin Test (TST) & Interferon-Gamma Release Assay (IGRA):

  • Use: Screen for LATENT TB infection. NOT for diagnosis of active TB disease
  • TST (Mantoux test): Intradermal injection. Read at 48-72hr. Induration ‚â•5mm (HIV, immunocompromised, close contact), ‚â•10mm (immigrants, healthcare workers, high-risk), ‚â•15mm (low-risk) considered positive
  • IGRA (QuantiFERON-TB Gold, T-SPOT): Blood test. Measures T-cell response to TB antigens. Not affected by BCG vaccination (unlike TST). Preferred in BCG-vaccinated individuals
  • Both can be negative in active TB: Anergy from severe illness, immunosuppression. Don't rule out active TB based on negative TST/IGRA alone

Chest X-Ray (CXR) - Essential:

  • Primary TB (children, recent infection): Hilar/mediastinal lymphadenopathy, middle/lower lobe infiltrates, Ghon complex (calcified granuloma + lymph node)
  • Reactivation TB (adults): Upper lobe infiltrates (apical/posterior segments), cavitary lesions (thin-walled cavities with air-fluid levels), fibronodular opacities, volume loss. Right upper lobe most common
  • Miliary TB: Bilateral diffuse miliary (millet seed) nodules, 1-3mm
  • Pleural effusion: Unilateral, moderate-large. May be only finding
  • Advanced disease: Fibrosis, traction bronchiectasis, destroyed lung

Note: CXR can be normal in early disease or extrapulmonary TB. HIV patients may have atypical or normal CXR despite active pulmonary TB

Sputum Testing (GOLD STANDARD for Pulmonary TB):

  • Collection: 3 separate sputum samples on 3 different days (ideally early morning, deep cough). If unable to produce sputum: induced sputum (nebulized hypertonic saline), bronchoscopy with BAL, gastric aspirate (children)
  • AFB Smear (Acid-Fast Bacilli): Ziehl-Neelsen or Auramine-rhodamine stain. Results in 24hr. Positive = patient is infectious. Sensitivity 50-60% (needs 5,000-10,000 organisms/mL). Negative smear does NOT rule out TB. Semi-quantitative: 1+ to 4+ (higher = more infectious)
  • Mycobacterial Culture (GOLD STANDARD): Solid media (Lowenstein-Jensen) takes 3-8 weeks. Liquid media (MGIT) faster (1-3 weeks). Definitive diagnosis. Allows drug susceptibility testing (DST). ESSENTIAL
  • Nucleic Acid Amplification Test (NAAT):
    • Xpert MTB/RIF (GeneXpert): Rapid PCR test (results in 2 hours). Detects M. tuberculosis + rifampin resistance. Sensitivity 80-90% (higher if smear-positive). WHO-recommended. Use on first sputum sample
    • Cepheid Xpert MTB/RIF Ultra: Improved sensitivity (90-95%). Detects lower bacterial loads
  • Histopathology (if tissue available): Caseating granulomas with Langhans giant cells. Supportive but not specific (fungi, sarcoid can mimic)

Extrapulmonary TB Diagnosis:

  • Pleural fluid: Lymphocyte-predominant exudate, ADA >40 U/L (90% sensitivity), low glucose, high protein. Pleural biopsy (caseating granulomas, culture) more sensitive than pleural fluid culture
  • CSF (TB meningitis): Lymphocytic pleocytosis (50-500 cells/¬µL), low glucose (<45mg/dL or <50% serum), high protein (>100mg/dL). AFB smear low sensitivity (10-20%). Culture takes weeks. Large volume (6-10mL) increases yield. Xpert MTB/RIF on CSF (sensitivity 50-60%)
  • Lymph node: Fine-needle aspiration (FNA) for AFB smear/culture, or excisional biopsy (caseating granulomas, culture)
  • Urine: 3 early morning urine samples for AFB culture. Xpert MTB/RIF on urine (moderate sensitivity)
  • Biopsy: Any suspected site (bone, peritoneum, pericardium) - send for AFB smear, culture, histopathology

Labs:

  • CBC: Anemia of chronic disease, lymphopenia (miliary TB), monocytosis
  • CMP: Hyponatremia (SIADH in TB meningitis, miliary TB), elevated alkaline phosphatase (hepatic TB, drug toxicity)
  • ESR/CRP: Elevated (nonspecific inflammatory markers)
  • HIV test: Obtain in ALL TB patients. HIV/TB co-infection common
  • Baseline LFTs: Before starting treatment (INH, RIF, PZA are hepatotoxic)

Drug Susceptibility Testing (DST): CRITICAL. Perform on all initial isolates. Guides therapy. Tests susceptibility to INH, RIF, PZA, ethambutol, fluoroquinolones, second-line drugs. Takes 2-4 weeks. Don't wait for results to start treatment. Adjust regimen when results available

Additional Testing:

  • CT chest: If CXR abnormal or high suspicion with normal CXR. Better delineates cavities, lymphadenopathy, complications
  • Bronchoscopy with BAL: If unable to produce sputum, high suspicion with negative sputum, suspected endobronchial TB
  • MRI spine: If Pott's disease suspected (back pain, neurologic deficits)
  • Ophthalmology exam: Check for choroidal tubercles (miliary TB), before starting ethambutol (monitor for optic neuritis)
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

ISOLATION (CRITICAL - Airborne Precautions):

  • Airborne isolation room: Negative pressure, ‚â•6 air changes/hour, HEPA filtration. Private room. Door closed
  • N95 respirators: For all healthcare workers entering room. Fit-tested. Surgical mask for patient when leaving room
  • Duration: Until 3 consecutive negative AFB smears (usually 2-4 weeks of treatment) OR clinically improving + receiving appropriate therapy √ó 2 weeks. TB meningitis/extrapulmonary TB (non-pulmonary) generally NOT infectious - standard precautions
  • Reporting: TB is REPORTABLE to public health department. Contact investigation for close contacts

STANDARD TREATMENT REGIMEN (Drug-Susceptible TB):

Intensive Phase (First 2 Months) - RIPE:

  • Rifampin (R): 600mg PO daily (or 10mg/kg/day, max 600mg)
  • Isoniazid (I/INH): 300mg PO daily (or 5mg/kg/day, max 300mg) + Pyridoxine (Vitamin B6) 25-50mg daily (prevent peripheral neuropathy)
  • Pyrazinamide (P/PZA): 1000-2000mg PO daily (weight-based: 40-55kg = 1000mg, 56-75kg = 1500mg, 76-90kg = 2000mg, >90kg = 2000mg)
  • Ethambutol (E): 800-1600mg PO daily (weight-based: 40-55kg = 800mg, 56-75kg = 1200mg, 76-90kg = 1600mg, >90kg = 1600mg)
  • Duration: 2 months (8 weeks) OR until AFB smear-negative and clinical improvement

Continuation Phase (Next 4-7 Months) - Rifampin + Isoniazid:

  • Rifampin 600mg PO daily + Isoniazid 300mg PO daily (+ pyridoxine 25-50mg daily)
  • Duration:
    • Uncomplicated pulmonary TB (cavitary disease, smear-negative at 2 months): 4 months continuation (total 6 months therapy)
    • Cavitary disease + positive smear at 2 months: 7 months continuation (total 9 months)
    • TB meningitis, miliary TB, bone/joint TB: 10-12 months continuation (total 12 months therapy)

Total Treatment Duration:

  • Drug-susceptible pulmonary TB: 6 months (2 months RIPE, then 4 months RI)
  • Extrapulmonary TB (lymph nodes, pleura): 6 months (same as pulmonary)
  • TB meningitis, miliary TB, bone/spine TB: 9-12 months
  • MDR-TB: 18-24 months (complex regimen, consult ID/TB specialist)

Directly Observed Therapy (DOT): STRONGLY RECOMMENDED for all TB patients. Healthcare worker observes patient taking medications (3-5 times/week or daily). Ensures adherence, prevents resistance, improves cure rates. Public health department coordinates DOT

Alternative Dosing (Intermittent Therapy): 3 times weekly dosing (after initial 2 weeks daily therapy) acceptable if DOT ensured. Used in resource-limited settings. Doses higher than daily dosing (consult ID/TB expert for dosing)

Drug-Resistant TB:

  • Rifampin-Resistant (RR-TB): Detected by Xpert MTB/RIF. Treat as MDR-TB until full DST results
  • MDR-TB (INH + RIF resistant): Requires 4-5 drugs including fluoroquinolone (levofloxacin, moxifloxacin) + second-line injectable (amikacin, capreomycin) + pyrazinamide + ethambutol + others (linezolid, bedaquiline, delamanid). CONSULT ID/TB SPECIALIST. Treatment 18-24 months
  • XDR-TB: MDR + resistant to fluoroquinolones + injectable. Very limited options. Newer drugs: bedaquiline, delamanid, pretomanid. MUST consult TB expert

HIV Co-Infection (TB-HIV):

  • Start TB treatment immediately: Don't delay for ART initiation
  • When to start ART:
    • CD4 <50: Start ART within 2 weeks of starting TB treatment (balance immune reconstitution vs TB treatment)
    • CD4 >50: Start ART within 8 weeks of TB treatment
    • TB meningitis: DELAY ART until 8 weeks (risk of IRIS with neurologic worsening)
  • Drug interactions: Rifampin induces CYP450 (decreases ART levels). Integrase inhibitors (dolutegravir, raltegravir) or efavirenz preferred. Avoid protease inhibitors with rifampin. Consider rifabutin (less CYP induction) instead of rifampin if needed

TB Meningitis (Special Considerations):

  • Adjunctive corticosteroids: Dexamethasone 0.3-0.4mg/kg/day IV √ó 4 weeks, then taper over 4 weeks. Reduces mortality and disability. START WITH FIRST DOSE OF TB DRUGS
  • Duration: 9-12 months total treatment
  • Monitor: Repeat lumbar punctures if worsening symptoms (hydrocephalus, increased ICP). Shunt if needed

Monitoring During Treatment:

  • Clinical: Weekly initially, then monthly. Assess adherence, symptoms, side effects
  • Sputum smears: Monthly until 2 consecutive negatives, then at end of treatment
  • LFTs: Baseline, then monthly (or sooner if symptoms of hepatitis). Hold drugs if ALT >3√ó ULN with symptoms or >5√ó ULN asymptomatic
  • CMP: Monitor creatinine (ethambutol cleared renally), uric acid (pyrazinamide causes hyperuricemia)
  • Visual acuity & color vision: Monthly (ethambutol optic neuritis). Baseline ophthalmology exam
  • CXR: At 2 months, end of treatment, then as needed. Improvement may lag clinical improvement

Treatment Failure: Persistent positive sputum cultures after 4 months of treatment. Suggests drug resistance, non-adherence, malabsorption. Repeat DST. Consult ID/TB specialist. Do NOT add single drug to failing regimen (creates resistance)

Supportive Care:

  • Nutrition: High-calorie, high-protein diet. Many patients malnourished
  • Diabetes control: Rifampin decreases efficacy of oral hypoglycemics. May need insulin
  • Symptom management: Cough suppressants, antipyretics, analgesics PRN
  • Contact investigation: Public health screens close contacts (TST/IGRA, CXR). Treat LTBI if indicated
üîë TEACHING PEARLS
  • TB is "the great mimicker": Can present as pneumonia, lymphoma, cancer, chronic meningitis, osteomyelitis. High index of suspicion in endemic areas, immunocompromised, chronic symptoms
  • Don't wait for culture to treat: Cultures take weeks. Start treatment based on clinical/radiographic suspicion and positive AFB smear or Xpert. Adjust when DST results available
  • Never add a single drug to a failing regimen: Creates resistance. If treatment failing, add ‚â•2 new drugs and consult TB specialist
  • Rifampin turns body fluids orange: Warn patients (urine, tears, sweat). Contact lenses permanently stained. Not harmful
  • INH peripheral neuropathy is preventable: Give pyridoxine (vitamin B6) 25-50mg daily with INH to ALL patients. Essential in diabetics, HIV, pregnant women, alcoholics, malnourished
  • Ethambutol optic neuritis is dose-dependent: Monitor visual acuity and color vision monthly. Mostly reversible if caught early. Higher risk if renal insufficiency (dose-adjust) or >2 months use
  • TB-HIV co-infection is deadly but treatable: Start TB treatment first, then ART (within 2-8 weeks depending on CD4). Watch for IRIS (immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome) - paradoxical worsening when starting ART
  • TB meningitis needs steroids: Dexamethasone reduces mortality and disability. Start with first dose of TB drugs. Delay ART until 8 weeks (risk of IRIS neurologic worsening)
  • Sputum cultures must be done: Only way to confirm diagnosis and perform drug susceptibility testing. Negative smears don't rule out TB (sensitivity only 50-60%). Culture is gold standard
  • Adherence is everything: Non-adherence ‚Üí treatment failure ‚Üí drug resistance ‚Üí MDR-TB/XDR-TB. DOT (directly observed therapy) is highly effective and recommended for ALL patients
‚ö†Ô∏è ESCALATE TO ICU IF:
  • Respiratory failure requiring mechanical ventilation (extensive TB pneumonia, ARDS)
  • TB meningitis with decreased consciousness (GCS <13), increased ICP, neurologic deterioration
  • Hemodynamically unstable (septic shock rare but possible)
  • Massive hemoptysis (>200mL in 24hr)
  • Drug-induced hepatotoxicity (ALT >10√ó ULN, coagulopathy, encephalopathy)
  • Severe IRIS (immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome) in HIV patients starting ART
‚úì DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Clinically stable and improving (no fever, improved symptoms)
  • Tolerating PO medications
  • 3 consecutive negative AFB smears (pulmonary TB) OR clinically improving on treatment √ó 2 weeks
  • Appropriate outpatient TB regimen prescribed (DOT arranged)
  • Public health notified (contact investigation underway)
  • Close follow-up arranged (ID clinic, public health DOT program)
  • Patient counseled on adherence, side effects, infectivity
  • Isolation precautions no longer needed (3 negative smears or appropriate duration on therapy)
  • Directly Observed Therapy (DOT) arranged through public health
COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR

Acute (During Active Disease): Respiratory failure (extensive pneumonia, ARDS), massive hemoptysis (eroded pulmonary artery - requires bronchial artery embolization or surgery), pneumothorax (cavitary disease rupture), empyema (pleural space infection), pericardial tamponade (pericardial TB), spinal cord compression (Pott's disease with epidural abscess), obstructive hydrocephalus (TB meningitis - may need shunt), ARDS (miliary TB, severe pneumonia)

Treatment-Related Complications:

  • Hepatotoxicity (INH, RIF, PZA): Transaminitis (10-20%), symptomatic hepatitis (1-3%). Monitor LFTs monthly. Hold drugs if ALT >5√ó ULN or >3√ó ULN with symptoms. Rechallenge drugs sequentially when LFTs normalize. INH most hepatotoxic
  • Peripheral neuropathy (INH): Prevented by pyridoxine (vitamin B6). Higher risk in diabetes, HIV, alcohol, malnutrition
  • Optic neuritis (Ethambutol): Decreased visual acuity, red-green color blindness. Monitor monthly. Usually reversible if caught early. Dose-adjust in renal insufficiency
  • Hyperuricemia/gout (Pyrazinamide): Elevated uric acid. Treat with NSAIDs, colchicine. Allopurinol if recurrent gout (but wait until after PZA phase)
  • Thrombocytopenia (Rifampin): Immune-mediated. Stop rifampin. Can use rifabutin instead
  • Orange discoloration (Rifampin): Body fluids (urine, tears, sweat) turn orange-red. Harmless. Warn patients (contact lenses stained)
  • Drug interactions (Rifampin): CYP450 inducer. Decreases levels of: oral contraceptives (use barrier method), warfarin, anticonvulsants, antiretrovirals, immunosuppressants
  • IRIS (Immune Reconstitution Inflammatory Syndrome): Paradoxical worsening when starting ART in HIV patients on TB treatment. Inflammatory reaction to dead/dying TB. Can be severe (lymph node enlargement, worsening infiltrates, fever). Manage with NSAIDs or steroids. Don't stop ART or TB drugs

Chronic (Post-Treatment): Chronic pulmonary sequelae (fibrosis, bronchiectasis, destroyed lung, aspergilloma in old cavities - hemoptysis risk), chronic pericarditis (constrictive - may need pericardiectomy), chronic meningitis sequelae (hydrocephalus, seizures, cranial nerve palsies, cognitive impairment), skeletal deformities (gibbus deformity from vertebral collapse), chronic kidney disease (GU TB), relapse (5% after adequate treatment - usually within 1 year, higher if HIV, drug-resistant TB, non-adherence)

Prevention: Treat LTBI (latent TB infection) in high-risk individuals: INH 300mg daily √ó 9 months OR rifampin 600mg daily √ó 4 months OR INH-rifapentine weekly √ó 3 months (3HP). BCG vaccine (limited efficacy in adults, not used in US). Infection control (airborne precautions, N95 respirators). Contact investigation and treatment of close contacts

‚Üë Back to Table of Contents

66. HIV/AIDS COMPLICATIONS

Immunodeficiency ‚Ä¢ Opportunistic Infections ‚Ä¢ Malignancies ‚Ä¢ ART Initiation
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

HIV Disease Stages (CDC Classification):

  • Stage 1 (Acute HIV): Acute retroviral syndrome 2-4 weeks post-exposure. Fever, pharyngitis, rash, lymphadenopathy, myalgia. High viral load, CD4 normal/transiently decreased. Many asymptomatic
  • Stage 2 (Chronic/Asymptomatic): CD4 ‚â•200. No AIDS-defining conditions. Can last years without treatment
  • Stage 3 (AIDS): CD4 <200 OR presence of AIDS-defining condition (regardless of CD4). High risk for opportunistic infections, malignancies

Common Hospitalization Scenarios:

  • Newly diagnosed HIV with acute illness: Patient presents with opportunistic infection, found to have HIV during workup
  • Known HIV, poor adherence to ART: Immunosuppression progresses ‚Üí opportunistic infections
  • Immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome (IRIS): Paradoxical worsening after starting ART (usually 2 weeks-3 months after ART initiation)
  • ART-related complications: Drug toxicity, interactions, side effects

OPPORTUNISTIC INFECTIONS BY CD4 COUNT:

CD4 <200 cells/¬µL:

  • Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (PCP): Subacute onset (days-weeks). Fever, nonproductive cough, dyspnea on exertion, hypoxemia. CXR: diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates or normal. LDH elevated. Dx: sputum/BAL for DFA/PCR. Rx: TMP-SMX high-dose 15-20mg/kg/day (TMP component) + prednisone if PaO2 <70 or A-a gradient >35
  • Candida esophagitis: Odynophagia, dysphagia, retrosternal pain. Thrush often present. Dx: endoscopy shows white plaques. Rx: fluconazole 200-400mg daily √ó 14-21 days
  • Toxoplasma encephalitis: Headache, confusion, focal neurologic deficits, seizures. MRI: multiple ring-enhancing lesions (basal ganglia, corticomedullary junction). Dx: clinical + imaging, serum toxoplasma IgG positive. Rx: pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + leucovorin
  • Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML): JC virus reactivation. Rapidly progressive neurologic decline, weakness, ataxia, visual changes. MRI: non-enhancing white matter lesions. NO treatment except ART. 30-50% mortality

CD4 <100 cells/¬µL:

  • Cryptococcal meningitis: Subacute headache, fever, altered mental status. Minimal meningismus. Dx: LP shows elevated opening pressure (>25cm H2O), low glucose, high protein, positive CSF cryptococcal antigen (>95% sensitive). India ink 50% sensitive. Rx: amphotericin B + flucytosine √ó 2 weeks, then fluconazole consolidation. Therapeutic LP if OP >25cm
  • CMV retinitis: Painless vision loss, floaters. Fundoscopy: "pizza pie" appearance (hemorrhages + exudates). Dx: dilated eye exam. Rx: ganciclovir or foscarnet
  • CMV colitis/esophagitis: Diarrhea (often bloody), abdominal pain. Dx: colonoscopy with biopsy (CMV inclusion bodies). Rx: ganciclovir

CD4 <50 cells/¬µL:

  • Mycobacterium avium complex (MAC/MAI): Disseminated infection. High fever, night sweats, weight loss, diarrhea, hepatosplenomegaly, anemia. Blood cultures grow MAC (takes 2-4 weeks). Rx: azithromycin + ethambutol
  • CMV disease: See above

ANY CD4 COUNT (but more common with low CD4):

  • Tuberculosis (TB): Can occur at any CD4. More likely extrapulmonary/disseminated with CD4 <200. Chronic cough, fever, night sweats, weight loss. AFB smear/culture. Rx: RIPE therapy
  • Bacterial pneumonia (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae): Increased risk even with preserved CD4. Acute onset fever, productive cough, infiltrate on CXR. Rx: standard CAP antibiotics
  • Kaposi sarcoma (KS): HHV-8 associated. Violaceous skin lesions, can involve GI tract, lungs. Dx: biopsy. Rx: ART (immune reconstitution treats KS), chemotherapy if severe
  • Non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL): Primary CNS lymphoma (ring-enhancing mass, single lesion - ddx from toxo). Rx: chemo + ART

IMMUNE RECONSTITUTION INFLAMMATORY SYNDROME (IRIS):

  • Definition: Paradoxical worsening of infection symptoms after starting ART due to recovering immune system. Typically occurs 2 weeks - 3 months after ART start
  • Presentation: New fever, worsening symptoms of prior infection (e.g., enlarging TB lymph nodes, worsening PCP infiltrates), new organ dysfunction
  • Common IRIS triggers: Mycobacterial infections (TB, MAC), cryptococcal meningitis, CMV, PCP, hepatitis B/C
  • Management: Continue ART (do NOT stop). Treat underlying infection. NSAIDs or corticosteroids for severe symptoms
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

HIV Testing (if newly diagnosed):

  • 4th generation Ag/Ab combo test: Detects HIV-1/2 antibodies + p24 antigen. Positive as early as 2-3 weeks post-exposure
  • Confirm with HIV-1/HIV-2 differentiation assay
  • HIV viral load (HIV RNA PCR): Quantifies virus. Used for monitoring, not diagnosis (can be falsely positive)

Baseline Labs (New Diagnosis or Hospitalization):

  • CD4 count: Determines stage, guides prophylaxis, predicts OI risk
  • HIV viral load: Baseline for monitoring ART response
  • CBC with diff: Anemia, thrombocytopenia, leukopenia common
  • CMP: Renal function (ART dosing), LFTs (hepatotoxicity risk, coinfection)
  • Hepatitis B and C serology: High coinfection rates. HBV/HCV accelerate HIV progression
  • Syphilis (RPR/VDRL): High coinfection rates, atypical presentations common
  • Toxoplasma IgG: If positive, at risk for reactivation when CD4 <100
  • CMV IgG: If positive, risk for reactivation when CD4 <50
  • Glucose, lipid panel: Baseline for metabolic complications of ART
  • Pregnancy test (if applicable): ART regimen selection differs in pregnancy

Resistance Testing:

  • HIV genotype: Check for drug resistance mutations BEFORE starting ART. Guides regimen selection

Workup for Suspected Opportunistic Infection:

  • If respiratory symptoms: CXR, pulse ox/ABG, sputum for bacterial culture, AFB, PCP (DFA/PCR), fungal culture. Consider CT chest if CXR nondiagnostic. Blood cultures
  • If neurologic symptoms: MRI brain with contrast (ring-enhancing lesions: toxo vs lymphoma vs abscess). LP if meningitis suspected (opening pressure, cell count, protein, glucose, Gram stain, bacterial/fungal cultures, cryptococcal Ag, VDRL). Serum cryptococcal Ag (very sensitive for disseminated crypto)
  • If GI symptoms: Stool studies (C. diff, bacterial pathogens, ova/parasites, Cryptosporidium, Microsporidium). Consider endoscopy/colonoscopy for persistent diarrhea or GI bleeding (CMV, KS, lymphoma)
  • If fever without source: Blood cultures (bacterial and mycobacterial), serum cryptococcal Ag, CMV PCR, CT chest/abdomen/pelvis. Consider bone marrow biopsy if pancytopenia
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

ANTIRETROVIRAL THERAPY (ART) - WHEN TO START:

  • START ART AS SOON AS POSSIBLE in all patients with HIV regardless of CD4 count (2023 DHHS guidelines)
  • Timing in hospitalized patients:
    • If NO active opportunistic infection: Start ART immediately (within days)
    • If active OI present: Start OI treatment first, then ART within 2 weeks (exceptions below)
    • DELAY ART if: (1) TB meningitis (wait 8 weeks), (2) cryptococcal meningitis (wait 4-6 weeks after starting antifungals AND CSF sterilized), (3) other CNS infections where IRIS could cause herniation

INITIAL ART REGIMENS (Treatment-Naive):

Preferred regimens (2023 DHHS):

  • Biktarvy (bictegravir/TAF/FTC): 1 tablet PO daily. Well-tolerated, high barrier to resistance, minimal drug interactions. Most common first-line choice
  • Dovato (dolutegravir/lamivudine): 1 tablet PO daily. Two-drug regimen, less nephrotoxicity. Cannot use if: HBV coinfection, HIV RNA >500,000, or before resistance testing
  • Triumeq (dolutegravir/abacavir/lamivudine): 1 tablet PO daily. REQUIRES HLA-B*5701 testing (abacavir hypersensitivity). Avoid if high cardiovascular risk
  • Symtuza (darunavir/cobicistat/TAF/FTC): 1 tablet PO daily with food. Protease inhibitor-based. Good for high viral loads or suspected resistance

ART in Special Populations:

  • Pregnancy: Start immediately. Preferred: dolutegravir-based or darunavir/ritonavir-based regimens. Avoid efavirenz (teratogenic)
  • Chronic kidney disease (CrCl <50): Avoid tenofovir disoproxil fumarate (TDF). Use tenofovir alafenamide (TAF) instead, or non-tenofovir regimens
  • Chronic hepatitis B coinfection: Must use ART regimen that treats both (tenofovir + emtricitabine or lamivudine). If stopping ART, monitor for HBV flare
  • TB coinfection: Drug interactions with rifamycins. Use dolutegravir 50mg BID (instead of daily) if on rifampin, or use efavirenz-based regimen

OPPORTUNISTIC INFECTION PROPHYLAXIS:

CD4 <200:

  • PCP prophylaxis: TMP-SMX DS 1 tablet daily (or 3x/week). Alternative: dapsone 100mg daily, atovaquone 1500mg daily, or inhaled pentamidine 300mg monthly
  • Toxoplasmosis prophylaxis (if Toxo IgG positive): TMP-SMX DS provides dual prophylaxis for PCP + Toxo

CD4 <100:

  • Toxoplasmosis prophylaxis (if not on TMP-SMX): Same as above

CD4 <50:

  • MAC prophylaxis: Azithromycin 1200mg weekly (or 600mg twice weekly). Alternative: clarithromycin 500mg BID

CAN DISCONTINUE prophylaxis when: CD4 >200 for ‚â•3 months on ART with undetectable viral load

SUPPORTIVE CARE:

  • Nutrition: High-calorie, high-protein diet. Consider appetite stimulants if severe wasting (megestrol, dronabinol)
  • Vaccinations (once CD4 >200 on ART): Pneumococcal (PCV20 or PCV15 + PPSV23), influenza annually, COVID-19, Tdap, hepatitis A/B (if non-immune), HPV (if age <26), meningococcal, varicella/zoster (if CD4 >200). NO live vaccines if CD4 <200
  • Smoking cessation, substance use treatment: Critical for reducing comorbidities

MONITORING ART RESPONSE:

  • Viral load: Check 2-4 weeks after starting ART, then q4-8 weeks until undetectable (<50 copies/mL), then q3-6 months
  • CD4 count: Check q3-6 months until consistently >200-300, then annually
  • Goal: Undetectable viral load (<50) within 6 months. CD4 should increase 50-150 cells/¬µL per year
üí° TEACHING PEARLS: HIV/AIDS COMPLICATIONS
  • CD4 <200 = Start PCP prophylaxis IMMEDIATELY (don't wait for first episode). TMP-SMX DS daily is gold standard
  • Toxo encephalitis vs CNS lymphoma: Toxo = multiple ring-enhancing lesions, Toxo IgG positive, responds to empiric pyrimethamine/sulfadiazine within 7-14 days. Lymphoma = single lesion, no Toxo IgG, needs biopsy. Empiric trial of toxo treatment reasonable before brain biopsy
  • Cryptococcal meningitis = Check opening pressure and reduce if >25cm H2O (therapeutic LP). Elevated ICP causes most deaths. Repeat LPs daily if needed to keep OP <20cm
  • PCP can present with normal CXR in early disease. If high clinical suspicion + hypoxemia + elevated LDH ‚Üí empiric treatment reasonable. Gallium scan or high-res CT more sensitive
  • CMV retinitis is a CLINICAL diagnosis by ophthalmology. Don't wait for PCR. Untreated ‚Üí blindness within weeks
  • IRIS typically occurs 2 weeks to 3 months after starting ART. Paradoxical worsening of TB, cryptococcus, or MAC. Continue ART + treat OI + add steroids for severe cases
  • MAC bacteremia occurs at CD4 <50. Fever, night sweats, diarrhea, hepatosplenomegaly. Blood cultures take 2-4 weeks to grow. Start prophylaxis (azithromycin) if CD4 <50
  • HIV patients with bacterial pneumonia: Higher rates of S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, and S. aureus. More severe disease. Treat like CAP but consider broader coverage
  • START ART in hospital if no contraindication (CNS OI, crypto meningitis, TB meningitis). Early ART improves outcomes. Consult ID if unsure
  • Abacavir hypersensitivity: Check HLA-B*5701 BEFORE starting. If positive, never give abacavir (can cause fatal reaction). If patient develops fever/rash/GI symptoms within 6 weeks of starting abacavir ‚Üí stop immediately, never rechallenge
  • Drug interactions with ART: Integrase inhibitors (dolutegravir, bictegravir) have fewest interactions. Protease inhibitors (ritonavir, cobicistat) are major CYP3A4 inhibitors ‚Üí many interactions. Check every new drug
  • Undetectable = Untransmittable (U=U). Patients with undetectable viral load on ART do not transmit HIV sexually. Important for patient counseling and reducing stigma
üö® ICU ESCALATION CRITERIA
  • Respiratory failure requiring intubation (PCP-ARDS, severe CAP)
  • Septic shock requiring vasopressors
  • Altered mental status with concern for herniation (toxo, crypto, PML, lymphoma)
  • Severe lactic acidosis from sepsis or antiretrovirals
  • Refractory seizures (toxo, CNS lymphoma, HIV encephalopathy)
  • Massive GI bleeding (CMV colitis, KS, lymphoma)
‚úÖ DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Opportunistic infection improving on treatment
  • ART initiated (or patient on stable ART regimen)
  • Tolerating PO intake, medications
  • Appropriate outpatient follow-up arranged:
    • HIV specialist within 1-2 weeks
    • Prophylaxis prescriptions filled (if CD4 <200)
    • Case management, social work support
  • Patient counseled on medication adherence, safe sex practices, U=U
  • If new diagnosis: Ryan White services, mental health support arranged
‚ö†Ô∏è COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR
ART Toxicities: Nephrotoxicity (tenofovir), hepatotoxicity, rash (especially NNRTIs), GI intolerance, metabolic (hyperlipidemia, hyperglycemia, lipodystrophy), bone loss (tenofovir), neuropsychiatric effects (efavirenz, dolutegravir)
Immune Reconstitution Inflammatory Syndrome (IRIS): Worsening of OI after ART initiation. Common with TB, MAC, cryptococcus. Occurs 2 weeks-3 months post-ART. Continue ART, treat OI, add steroids for severe IRIS
Drug Resistance: Develop if poor adherence. Check resistance testing if virologic failure (VL >200 after initial suppression). Switch to new regimen based on resistance mutations
Chronic Comorbidities: Increased cardiovascular disease (integrate statins, ASA), chronic kidney disease (avoid tenofovir), osteoporosis (bone density screening), malignancies (especially cervical cancer, anal cancer - screen regularly)
Mental Health: Depression, anxiety, substance use common. Screen regularly. Adherence counseling critical
Hepatitis B/C Coinfection: Accelerates liver disease. Treat both. If stopping ART with HBV coinfection ‚Üí risk of fulminant hepatitis B flare
‚Üë Back to Top

67. OPPORTUNISTIC INFECTIONS IN IMMUNOCOMPROMISED HOST

Non-HIV Immunosuppression ‚Ä¢ Transplant ‚Ä¢ Chemotherapy ‚Ä¢ Biologics ‚Ä¢ High-Index Suspicion
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Immunocompromised Populations at Risk:

  • Solid organ transplant recipients: On chronic immunosuppression (tacrolimus, mycophenolate, prednisone). Highest risk first 6 months post-transplant
  • Hematopoietic stem cell transplant (HSCT): Profound immunosuppression. Risk varies by time post-transplant and GVHD status
  • Hematologic malignancies: Leukemia, lymphoma, multiple myeloma. Disease + chemotherapy cause immunosuppression
  • Solid tumor malignancies on chemotherapy: Neutropenia (ANC <500) is major risk factor
  • Chronic high-dose corticosteroids: ‚â•20mg prednisone daily √ó ‚â•2 weeks. Risk proportional to dose + duration
  • Biologic immunosuppressants: TNF-Œ± inhibitors (infliximab, adalimumab, etanercept), rituximab (anti-CD20), JAK inhibitors. Risk of reactivation TB, fungal infections, viral reactivation
  • Chronic kidney disease (dialysis): Impaired cell-mediated immunity, uremia effects
  • Diabetes mellitus: Increased risk bacterial, fungal infections (especially if poor glucose control)
  • Cirrhosis/end-stage liver disease: Impaired opsonization, complement deficiency, bacterial translocation
  • Asplenia (surgical or functional): Encapsulated organism risk (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, N. meningitidis, Capnocytophaga)

Timing of Infections Post-Transplant:

Early (<1 month):

  • Nosocomial infections (surgical site, catheter-related, Clostridioides difficile, aspiration pneumonia)
  • Donor-derived infections

Intermediate (1-6 months):

  • Highest OI risk period. Peak immunosuppression
  • CMV disease, EBV (PTLD), BK virus (kidney transplant), HHV-6
  • Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (if no prophylaxis)
  • Invasive fungal infections: Aspergillus, Mucorales, endemic fungi (Histoplasma, Coccidioides, Blastomyces)
  • Toxoplasma (heart transplant from Toxo+ donor)
  • Nocardia, Listeria meningitis

Late (>6 months):

  • Community-acquired infections (similar to general population)
  • Reactivation of latent infections (TB, strongyloidiasis, Chagas, endemic fungi)
  • CMV if chronic rejection requiring increased immunosuppression

KEY OPPORTUNISTIC PATHOGENS BY TYPE:

FUNGAL INFECTIONS:

  • Invasive Aspergillosis: Prolonged neutropenia, HSCT, lung transplant, high-dose steroids. Fever unresponsive to antibiotics, cough, hemoptysis, pleuritic pain. CT chest: nodules with "halo sign" (ground-glass opacity around nodule), "air crescent sign" (later). Galactomannan in serum/BAL. Rx: voriconazole 6mg/kg IV q12h √ó2 doses ‚Üí 4mg/kg IV q12h. Alternative: isavuconazole, liposomal amphotericin B
  • Mucormycosis (Zygomycosis): Diabetes (DKA), neutropenia, iron overload, deferoxamine therapy. RAPIDLY PROGRESSIVE. Rhinocerebral (facial pain, nasal necrosis, black eschar, vision changes), pulmonary (hemoptysis), disseminated. CT: sinus opacification, bone destruction. Dx: tissue biopsy (broad nonseptate hyphae). Rx: urgent surgical debridement + liposomal amphotericin B 5-10mg/kg/day. High mortality (30-70%)
  • Cryptococcus (non-HIV): Solid organ transplant, TNF-Œ± inhibitors, chronic steroids. Meningitis (headache, fever, AMS). Pulmonary nodules/infiltrates. Dx: serum/CSF cryptococcal Ag, CSF culture. Rx: amphotericin B + flucytosine
  • Endemic Mycoses:
    • Histoplasmosis: Ohio/Mississippi River valleys. Disseminated disease in immunocompromised (fever, hepatosplenomegaly, pancytopenia). Urine/serum Histoplasma Ag. Rx: liposomal amphotericin B (severe) or itraconazole (mild-moderate)
    • Coccidioidomycosis: Southwest US. Pulmonary or disseminated (meningitis, bone/joint). CF test, coccidioidal Ab. Rx: fluconazole (meningitis), amphotericin B (severe)
    • Blastomycosis: Midwest/Southeast US. Pulmonary + skin lesions. Dx: culture, Blasto Ag urine. Rx: itraconazole or amphotericin B
  • Candida: Bloodstream infection (candidemia), hepatosplenic candidiasis, esophagitis. Remove central lines. Rx: echinocandin (caspofungin, micafungin) first-line. Fluconazole if susceptible species + stable patient

VIRAL INFECTIONS:

  • Cytomegalovirus (CMV): Transplant recipients (especially if donor+/recipient-), HSCT. CMV syndrome (fever, malaise, leukopenia, thrombocytopenia), end-organ disease (pneumonitis, colitis, hepatitis, retinitis). Dx: CMV PCR (quantitative viral load), tissue biopsy ("owl's eye" inclusion bodies). Rx: ganciclovir 5mg/kg IV q12h or valganciclovir 900mg PO BID √ó 2-3 weeks, then maintenance if ongoing immunosuppression
  • BK virus: Kidney transplant recipients. BK nephropathy ‚Üí graft dysfunction, hematuria. Urine/plasma BK PCR. Rx: reduce immunosuppression, cidofovir or leflunomide (off-label)
  • EBV (Post-Transplant Lymphoproliferative Disorder - PTLD): B-cell lymphoma driven by EBV. Lymphadenopathy, extranodal masses, organ dysfunction. Elevated EBV PCR. Dx: biopsy. Rx: reduce immunosuppression, rituximab, chemotherapy
  • Varicella-Zoster Virus (VZV): Reactivation ‚Üí disseminated zoster (multi-dermatomal, visceral involvement). Rx: acyclovir 10-15mg/kg IV q8h
  • Herpes Simplex Virus (HSV): Severe mucocutaneous disease, esophagitis, encephalitis. Rx: acyclovir IV
  • Adenovirus: HSCT, pediatric transplants. Pneumonia, hepatitis, hemorrhagic cystitis, disseminated disease. Dx: PCR. Rx: cidofovir (nephrotoxic), reduce immunosuppression
  • Respiratory viruses (RSV, influenza, parainfluenza, human metapneumovirus): Severe pneumonia in immunocompromised. Dx: respiratory viral panel (PCR). Rx: supportive, oseltamivir (influenza), ribavirin (RSV - off-label)

BACTERIAL INFECTIONS:

  • Listeria monocytogenes: Meningitis, bacteremia. Transplant recipients, chronic steroids, elderly. Rx: ampicillin 2g IV q4h + gentamicin
  • Nocardia: Chronic steroids, transplant recipients. Pulmonary nodules/cavities, brain abscesses, disseminated. Dx: culture (slow-growing, modified AFB stain). Rx: TMP-SMX high-dose, prolonged therapy (6-12 months)
  • Mycobacterium tuberculosis: Reactivation common in TNF-Œ± inhibitor users, transplant recipients. Screen with IGRA before starting biologics. Rx: standard RIPE therapy, but watch drug interactions (rifampin ‚Üì immunosuppressant levels)
  • Nontuberculous mycobacteria (NTM): M. avium complex, M. abscessus. Pulmonary disease, disseminated. Dx: culture. Rx: macrolide-based multi-drug regimens
  • Encapsulated organisms (asplenia): S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, N. meningitidis. Overwhelming post-splenectomy sepsis (OPSS). Rx: immediate broad-spectrum antibiotics (ceftriaxone + vancomycin), aggressive resuscitation
  • Legionella: Transplant recipients. Severe pneumonia. Dx: urinary Ag. Rx: fluoroquinolone or azithromycin

PARASITIC INFECTIONS:

  • Toxoplasma gondii: Heart transplant (donor+/recipient-), HSCT. Encephalitis (fever, AMS, focal deficits, seizures), myocarditis. MRI: ring-enhancing lesions. Dx: serology, PCR, biopsy. Rx: pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + leucovorin
  • Strongyloides stercoralis hyperinfection: HTLV-1 coinfection, corticosteroids, transplant. GI symptoms (diarrhea, abdominal pain), pulmonary infiltrates, Gram-negative bacteremia/meningitis (larvae carry bacteria). Dx: stool O&P (insensitive), serology, BAL. Rx: ivermectin 200mcg/kg/day √ó ‚â•2 days (may need prolonged)
  • Cryptosporidium, Microsporidia, Isospora: Chronic diarrhea. Dx: stool special stains. Rx: nitazoxanide (Crypto), albendazole (Microsporidia), TMP-SMX (Isospora)
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Initial Assessment:

  • History: Type of immunosuppression, duration, specific agents, recent changes in regimen. Prior infections, prophylaxis medications. Travel history, exposures, endemic area residence
  • Physical exam: Skin lesions (fungal, viral, Nocardia), oropharynx (thrush), lung exam, neurologic exam, fundoscopy (CMV retinitis)

Laboratory Studies:

  • CBC with diff: Neutropenia (ANC), lymphopenia, anemia, thrombocytopenia
  • CMP: Renal function (drug dosing, nephrotoxicity), LFTs (hepatitis, drug toxicity)
  • Inflammatory markers: CRP, ESR (may be blunted in immunosuppressed)
  • Blood cultures: Bacterial, fungal (hold ‚â•5 days for fungal)
  • Serum fungal biomarkers: Galactomannan (Aspergillus), (1,3)-Œ≤-D-glucan (most invasive fungi), Cryptococcal Ag, Histoplasma Ag, Blastomyces Ag, Coccidioides Ab
  • Viral PCR: CMV quantitative PCR, EBV PCR, BK virus PCR (urine/plasma)
  • Immunosuppressant drug levels: Tacrolimus, cyclosporine, sirolimus (under-immunosuppression ‚Üí rejection, over ‚Üí infections)

Imaging:

  • CXR: Initial screening. Nodules, infiltrates, cavities
  • CT chest (high-resolution, with contrast): More sensitive for pulmonary infections. Look for: halo sign (Aspergillus), nodules, tree-in-bud (atypical mycobacteria), cavitation (Nocardia, TB, fungi)
  • CT sinuses: If facial pain, headache (Mucor, Aspergillus)
  • MRI brain with contrast: If neurologic symptoms. Ring-enhancing lesions (Toxo, Nocardia, fungi, abscess), meningeal enhancement (Listeria, Crypto, TB)
  • CT abdomen/pelvis: Hepatosplenic candidiasis, CMV colitis, typhlitis

Invasive Diagnostics (Often Necessary):

  • Bronchoscopy with BAL: Send for: bacterial/fungal/AFB/viral cultures, Galactomannan, PCP DFA/PCR, cytology, CMV PCR. Consider transbronchial biopsy
  • Lumbar puncture: If meningitis suspected. Opening pressure, cell count, protein, glucose, Gram stain, bacterial/fungal/AFB cultures, Cryptococcal Ag, VDRL, HSV/VZV PCR, CMV PCR, Toxoplasma PCR
  • Tissue biopsy: Skin lesions, lymph nodes, lung nodules. Send for histopathology (special stains: GMS, PAS, AFB) + culture (bacterial, fungal, AFB, viral). CRITICAL for diagnosing invasive fungal infections
  • Bone marrow biopsy: If fever without source + pancytopenia. Culture for fungi, AFB, Histoplasma Ag
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

General Principles:

  • LOW THRESHOLD for empiric broad-spectrum therapy. Immunocompromised patients deteriorate rapidly
  • Consider reducing immunosuppression (coordinate with transplant team or oncologist). Balance infection control vs rejection/GVHD risk
  • Prolonged treatment courses often needed (weeks to months)
  • Combination therapy for invasive fungal infections
  • Monitor drug levels and interactions (especially azoles with calcineurin inhibitors)

Empiric Therapy for Fever in Neutropenic Patient (ANC <500):

  • Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q6h OR cefepime 2g IV q8h OR meropenem 1g IV q8h (if high MDRO risk, prior resistant organisms, or severe sepsis)
  • Add vancomycin if: skin/soft tissue infection, catheter-related infection, hemodynamic instability, or known MRSA colonization
  • Add antifungal if fever persists >4-7 days on antibiotics: Start empiric echinocandin (caspofungin 70mg load ‚Üí 50mg daily, or micafungin 100mg daily) OR liposomal amphotericin B 3-5mg/kg/day

Empiric Therapy for Pulmonary Infiltrates:

  • Bacterial coverage: Piperacillin-tazobactam or cefepime or meropenem (as above) + azithromycin 500mg IV daily (atypical coverage)
  • PCP coverage: TMP-SMX 15-20mg/kg/day (TMP component) divided q6-8h. Add prednisone 40mg BID if moderate-severe hypoxemia (PaO2 <70 or A-a gradient >35)
  • Aspergillus/mold coverage: Voriconazole 6mg/kg IV q12h √ó2 doses ‚Üí 4mg/kg q12h. Consider if: prolonged neutropenia, HSCT, lung transplant, nodules on CT, hemoptysis

Specific Treatments (see Clinical Presentation for details):

  • Invasive Aspergillosis: Voriconazole (first-line) OR isavuconazole OR liposomal amphotericin B. Consider combination therapy (vori + echinocandin) for severe disease
  • Mucormycosis: Urgent surgical debridement + liposomal amphotericin B 5-10mg/kg/day. Add posaconazole or isavuconazole as step-down. HIGH MORTALITY
  • CMV disease: Ganciclovir 5mg/kg IV q12h (induction) √ó 2-3 weeks ‚Üí valganciclovir 900mg daily (maintenance). Alternative: foscarnet (if ganciclovir-resistant or severe myelosuppression). Monitor CMV PCR to guide duration
  • Toxoplasma encephalitis: Pyrimethamine 200mg loading ‚Üí 50-75mg daily + sulfadiazine 1-1.5g q6h + leucovorin 10-25mg daily √ó ‚â•6 weeks
  • Nocardia: TMP-SMX 5mg/kg (TMP component) IV q6-8h √ó 6-12 months. Add imipenem or amikacin for CNS disease or severe infection
  • Listeria meningitis: Ampicillin 2g IV q4h + gentamicin 5-7mg/kg/day √ó ‚â•21 days
  • Strongyloides hyperinfection: Ivermectin 200mcg/kg/day √ó ‚â•2 days (may need weeks for disseminated). Add albendazole if severe. Treat concurrent Gram-negative sepsis

Prophylaxis (Prevention):

  • PCP prophylaxis: TMP-SMX DS 1 tab daily or 3x/week. Indicated for: HSCT, high-dose steroids (‚â•20mg prednisone √ó‚â•1 month), hematologic malignancies, selected solid organ transplants. Alternative: dapsone, atovaquone, inhaled pentamidine
  • Fungal prophylaxis: Fluconazole (Candida) or posaconazole/voriconazole (mold) for high-risk patients (HSCT, AML/MDS with prolonged neutropenia)
  • CMV prophylaxis (transplant): Valganciclovir 900mg daily (adjust for CrCl) if high-risk (D+/R-, lung transplant, treatment of rejection). Monitor CMV PCR (preemptive strategy)
  • HSV/VZV prophylaxis: Acyclovir 400mg BID or valacyclovir 500mg daily for HSCT or frequent reactivations

Immunomodulation:

  • G-CSF (filgrastim, pegfilgrastim): For prolonged neutropenia after chemotherapy. Accelerates neutrophil recovery
  • IVIG: For hypogammaglobulinemia (IgG <400mg/dL) with recurrent infections
üí° TEACHING PEARLS: OPPORTUNISTIC INFECTIONS IN IMMUNOCOMPROMISED
  • "Fever + neutropenia = Emergency." ANC <500 + fever (‚â•38.3¬∞C or ‚â•38¬∞C √ó 1hr) = neutropenic fever. Start broad-spectrum antibiotics within 1 hour. Don't wait for cultures
  • Invasive Aspergillus: Think "halo sign" on CT chest (ground-glass opacity surrounding nodule). Classic for angioinvasive Aspergillus in neutropenic patients. Air crescent sign appears later (healing phase)
  • Mucormycosis = SURGICAL EMERGENCY. Rapidly progressive, angioinvasive. Black nasal eschar, facial pain, vision changes. Amphotericin B + urgent surgical debridement. Don't delay for biopsy if high suspicion
  • TNF-Œ± inhibitors ‚Üí Reactivation TB and invasive fungal infections. Screen for latent TB (IGRA) and endemic fungi before starting biologics. Treat LTBI with isoniazid √ó 9 months or rifampin √ó 4 months before starting biologic
  • Strongyloides hyperinfection in steroid users: Can disseminate when steroids given. Presents with severe diarrhea, Gram-negative sepsis/meningitis (larvae carry gut bacteria). Screen for Strongyloides serology before starting immunosuppression in endemic areas. Treat with ivermectin if positive
  • CMV in transplant: "Donor+/Recipient-" = HIGHEST RISK. Seropositive donor ‚Üí seronegative recipient. Give prophylaxis (valganciclovir) or preemptive therapy (monitor CMV PCR weekly, treat if positive)
  • PCP prophylaxis = TMP-SMX DS daily or 3x/week. Indicated for chronic steroids ‚â•20mg √ó ‚â•1 month, HSCT, hematologic malignancies on chemotherapy, selected transplants. CD4 <200 also indication (HIV)
  • Nocardia presents with "brain abscess + lung nodules" in chronically immunosuppressed. Modified AFB stain positive (weakly acid-fast). Long treatment (TMP-SMX √ó 6-12 months)
  • Listeria meningitis in transplant/elderly: CSF shows lymphocytic pleocytosis (mimics viral). Gram stain: Gram-positive rods. MUST use ampicillin (cephalosporins don't work). Add gentamicin for synergy
  • Cryptococcus in non-HIV: More likely disseminated disease (meningitis + pulmonary). Check serum Cryptococcal Ag (highly sensitive). LP: opening pressure, CSF Crypto Ag. Rx: amphotericin B + flucytosine
  • Galactomannan: Aspergillus screening test. Serum galactomannan (sensitivity 70-80% for invasive aspergillosis). BAL galactomannan more sensitive (>90%). False positives: piperacillin-tazobactam, some food
  • Asplenia ‚Üí Encapsulated organism risk (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, N. meningitidis). Vaccinate 2 weeks before splenectomy if elective: PCV20/PCV15+PPSV23, Hib, MenACWY + MenB. If emergent splenectomy, vaccinate 2 weeks post-op. Lifetime penicillin prophylaxis (PCN VK 250mg BID or amoxicillin 250mg BID) controversial but reasonable
üö® ICU ESCALATION CRITERIA
  • Neutropenic septic shock requiring vasopressors
  • Respiratory failure requiring intubation (invasive Aspergillus ARDS, PCP, CMV pneumonitis)
  • Severe mucormycosis requiring urgent surgical debridement
  • CNS infection with concern for herniation (Toxo, Nocardia abscess, Listeria meningitis, fungal)
  • Multi-organ dysfunction from sepsis
  • Typhlitis (neutropenic colitis) with perforation risk
‚úÖ DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Neutropenia resolved (ANC >500) or improving trend
  • Afebrile ‚â•24-48 hours
  • Infection controlled on oral antimicrobials (or IV with home infusion arranged)
  • Hemodynamically stable
  • Tolerating PO intake
  • Appropriate outpatient follow-up:
    • Transplant ID or Oncology within 1 week
    • Home health for IV antibiotics if needed
    • Prophylaxis medications prescribed
‚ö†Ô∏è COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR
