Septic Shock: Rapid deterioration despite antibiotics. Early goal-directed therapy (fluids, vasopressors, source control). Consider G-CSF if neutropenic
Respiratory Failure: ARDS from PCP, invasive Aspergillus, CMV pneumonitis. High mortality if mechanical ventilation required. Early bronchoscopy for diagnosis
Drug Toxicities: Nephrotoxicity (amphotericin B, aminoglycosides, foscarnet, cidofovir), hepatotoxicity (azoles, TMP-SMX), myelosuppression (ganciclovir, TMP-SMX, flucytosine). Monitor levels, adjust doses, supportive care
Drug Interactions: Azoles (voriconazole, posaconazole) are CYP3A4 inhibitors ‚Üí increase tacrolimus/cyclosporine/sirolimus levels. Monitor drug levels closely, adjust doses. Rifampin decreases immunosuppressant levels ‚Üí rejection risk
Multi-Drug Resistant Organisms: ESBL, CRE, VRE, MRSA. Common in transplant recipients with frequent hospitalizations. Carbapenem or polymyxin-based regimens. Infection control precautions
Relapse/Recurrence: Common if immunosuppression continues. Secondary prophylaxis often needed (e.g., fluconazole after candidemia, voriconazole after Aspergillus). Prolonged treatment courses
‚Üë Back to Top

68. HYPERTHYROIDISM/THYROTOXICOSIS (NON-STORM)

Graves Disease ‚Ä¢ Toxic Adenoma ‚Ä¢ Thyroiditis ‚Ä¢ Beta-Blockers + Thiou reas
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Note: This section covers NON-STORM hyperthyroidism. For Thyroid Storm (life-threatening emergency), see Condition #31

Common Etiologies:

  • Graves disease (60-80%): Autoimmune (TSI antibodies stimulate TSH receptor). Diffuse toxic goiter. Associated findings: exophthalmos (proptosis), pretibial myxedema, thyroid acromegaly (hand swelling)
  • Toxic multinodular goiter (15-20%): Autonomously functioning nodules. More common in elderly. Gradual onset
  • Toxic adenoma (solitary hot nodule, 3-5%): Single hyperfunctioning nodule. Uptake in nodule, suppression of rest of gland on scan
  • Thyroiditis (subacute, silent, postpartum): Transient hyperthyroid phase (thyroid inflammation ‚Üí hormone release) followed by hypothyroid phase. Tender thyroid (subacute), painless (silent/postpartum). LOW radioiodine uptake (vs high in Graves/toxic nodules)
  • Iodine-induced (Jod-Basedow phenomenon): Excess iodine exposure (contrast, amiodarone, iodine supplements) ‚Üí thyrotoxicosis in patients with underlying thyroid disease
  • Amiodarone-induced thyrotoxicosis (AIT): Type 1 (iodine-induced, pre-existing thyroid disease) vs Type 2 (destructive thyroiditis from amiodarone)
  • Exogenous thyroid hormone: Over-replacement, factitious thyrotoxicosis (thyrotoxicosis factitia), weight-loss supplements

Classic Symptoms:

  • Metabolic: Weight loss despite increased appetite (most common presenting symptom), heat intolerance, increased sweating
  • Cardiovascular: Palpitations, tachycardia (often sinus), atrial fibrillation (10-15%, especially elderly), increased pulse pressure (wide), systolic hypertension
  • Neuropsychiatric: Anxiety, nervousness, emotional lability, insomnia, tremor (fine), hyperreflexia, proximal muscle weakness
  • GI: Increased frequency of bowel movements (not frank diarrhea usually), nausea
  • Reproductive: Oligomenorrhea or amenorrhea (women), decreased libido, gynecomastia (men)
  • Dermatologic: Warm, moist skin, fine hair, onycholysis (nail separation), pretibial myxedema (Graves), palmar erythema

Physical Exam Findings:

  • Goiter: Diffusely enlarged (Graves), nodular (toxic MNG/adenoma), or tender (thyroiditis)
  • Thyroid bruit: Audible over thyroid (Graves - increased vascularity)
  • Ocular: Lid lag, lid retraction, stare. Exophthalmos/proptosis (Graves only - infiltrative ophthalmopathy from TSI antibodies)
  • Cardiovascular: Tachycardia, widened pulse pressure, systolic flow murmur (high output)
  • Neuromuscular: Fine tremor, hyperreflexia, proximal muscle weakness

Apathetic Thyrotoxicosis (Elderly):

  • Presentation: Atypical, "apathetic" - Depression, lethargy, weight loss, atrial fibrillation, heart failure. LACK hyperadrenergic symptoms (anxiety, tremor, heat intolerance). High index of suspicion needed

Thyrotoxic Periodic Paralysis:

  • Presentation: Sudden-onset muscle weakness/paralysis (usually lower extremities). Hypokalemia (K+ shifts intracellularly). More common in Asian males. Triggered by high-carb meals, exercise. Treat hypokalemia + underlying thyrotoxicosis
DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Thyroid Function Tests:

  • TSH: Suppressed (<0.01 mIU/L) in primary hyperthyroidism. FIRST test to order
  • Free T4: Elevated (>1.8 ng/dL). Confirms hyperthyroidism if TSH low
  • Free T3: Elevated (>4.0 pg/mL). Can be elevated when T4 normal (T3 toxicosis). Check if TSH low but T4 normal
  • Interpretation:
    • Low TSH + High Free T4/T3 = Primary hyperthyroidism (thyroid gland problem)
    • Low TSH + Normal Free T4/T3 = Subclinical hyperthyroidism
    • High TSH + High Free T4 = Central hyperthyroidism (pituitary adenoma - rare) or assay interference

Etiology Workup (Determine Cause):

  • TSI (Thyroid-Stimulating Immunoglobulin) or TRAb (TSH Receptor Antibody): Positive in Graves disease (95%). Confirms autoimmune etiology. Order if Graves suspected
  • Radioactive Iodine Uptake (RAIU) scan: Determines etiology based on uptake pattern:
    • HIGH uptake (>30% at 24hr): Graves disease (diffuse uptake), toxic multinodular goiter (patchy uptake), toxic adenoma (focal uptake with suppression elsewhere)
    • LOW uptake (<5%): Thyroiditis (subacute, silent, postpartum), exogenous thyroid hormone, iodine-induced, amiodarone type 2, struma ovarii (ectopic thyroid tissue in ovary)
  • Thyroid ultrasound: Evaluate nodules, goiter size/character. Increased vascularity (Doppler) suggests Graves. Hypoechoic, heterogeneous suggests thyroiditis
  • ESR/CRP: Elevated in subacute thyroiditis (painful). Normal in Graves, toxic nodules
  • Thyroglobulin: Elevated in thyroiditis (thyroid destruction releases Tg). Suppressed in exogenous hormone use

Additional Labs:

  • CBC: Mild normocytic anemia possible
  • CMP: Hypercalcemia (10-15%, increased bone turnover), elevated alkaline phosphatase (bone isoform), transaminitis (mild)
  • Lipid panel: Low LDL, low total cholesterol
  • ECG: Sinus tachycardia, atrial fibrillation (10-15%), shortened PR interval
  • Potassium: If periodic paralysis suspected
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

SYMPTOMATIC CONTROL (All Patients):

  • Beta-blockers:
    • Propranolol 10-40mg PO q6-8h (also decreases T4‚ÜíT3 conversion). Titrate to HR <90, symptom control
    • Alternatives: Metoprolol 25-50mg PO BID-TID, atenolol 25-100mg daily
    • Use: Controls tachycardia, tremor, anxiety. Does NOT treat underlying hyperthyroidism. Continue until euthyroid
    • Caution: Avoid in severe asthma/COPD (use cardioselective like metoprolol), decompensated HF

DEFINITIVE TREATMENT (Etiology-Specific):

GRAVES DISEASE / TOXIC NODULES:

  • Thionamides (Antithyroid Drugs):
    • Methimazole 15-30mg PO daily: PREFERRED (once-daily dosing, less hepatotoxicity). Start 15mg if mild, 30-40mg if severe. Adjust dose based on Free T4 (goal: euthyroid in 4-8 weeks). Maintenance: 5-10mg daily
    • Propylthiouracil (PTU) 100mg PO TID: Use ONLY in: (1) First trimester pregnancy (methimazole teratogenic), (2) Thyroid storm, (3) Methimazole allergy/side effects. Higher hepatotoxicity risk
    • Mechanism: Block thyroid peroxidase ‚Üí inhibit thyroid hormone synthesis. PTU also blocks T4‚ÜíT3 conversion
    • Monitoring: Check Free T4/TSH q4-6 weeks initially, then q3 months once stable. Monitor CBC, LFTs baseline and if symptoms (see complications)
    • Duration: Graves: 12-18 months, then trial off (40-60% remission rate). Toxic nodules: lifelong or definitive therapy (RAI/surgery)
  • Radioactive Iodine (RAI) Ablation:
    • Indications: Definitive treatment for Graves, toxic MNG, toxic adenoma. Preferred for patients >40, large goiters, relapse after thionamides
    • Process: Oral I-131 uptake by thyroid ‚Üí destroys thyroid tissue. Outpatient. Takes 3-6 months for full effect
    • Outcome: 80-90% cure rate. Most become hypothyroid ‚Üí lifelong levothyroxine
    • Contraindications: Pregnancy (causes fetal hypothyroidism), breastfeeding, uncontrolled thyrotoxicosis (risk of thyroid storm post-RAI). Active/severe Graves ophthalmopathy (can worsen eye disease - give steroids if mild ophthalmopathy)
    • Pre-treatment: May give methimazole to achieve euthyroid state first (stop 3-5 days before RAI to allow uptake). Beta-blocker continuation OK
  • Thyroidectomy (Surgical):
    • Indications: Large goiter with compressive symptoms, contraindication to RAI (pregnancy, severe ophthalmopathy), patient preference, suspicion of malignancy
    • Pre-op preparation: MUST achieve euthyroid state with thionamides first (4-8 weeks). Add iodine solution 10-14 days pre-op (SSKI or Lugol's solution) to decrease vascularity
    • Complications: Hypocalcemia (hypoparathyroidism), recurrent laryngeal nerve injury (hoarseness), hypothyroidism (total thyroidectomy), hematoma/bleeding

THYROIDITIS (Subacute, Silent, Postpartum):

  • Treatment: SELF-LIMITED (hyperthyroid phase lasts 1-3 months ‚Üí hypothyroid phase ‚Üí recovery in 6-12 months). Symptomatic control only
  • Beta-blockers: Propranolol or metoprolol for symptom control
  • NSAIDs or steroids: For pain in subacute thyroiditis (prednisone 40mg daily √ó 1 week, then taper if severe)
  • NO thionamides (not making new hormone, just releasing stored hormone). Would be ineffective
  • Monitor: TSH/Free T4 q4-6 weeks. May need levothyroxine if prolonged hypothyroid phase

AMIODARONE-INDUCED THYROTOXICOSIS (AIT):

  • Type 1 AIT (Jod-Basedow): Pre-existing thyroid disease. RAIU increased (if can be done). Rx: Methimazole 40mg daily +/- perchlorate (blocks iodine uptake). May need RAI or surgery
  • Type 2 AIT (Destructive thyroiditis): No pre-existing disease. RAIU low. Rx: Prednisone 40mg daily √ó 2-3 months (anti-inflammatory). Self-limited
  • Mixed type: Treat with both methimazole + prednisone
  • Discontinue amiodarone if possible (but long half-life means thyrotoxicosis can persist months after stopping)

EXOGENOUS THYROID HORMONE:

  • Treatment: Stop thyroid hormone. If intentional over-replacement, reduce dose. If factitious, address underlying psychiatric condition

ATRIAL FIBRILLATION MANAGEMENT:

  • Anticoagulation: Calculate CHA2DS2-VASc score. If ‚â•2 (men) or ‚â•3 (women), anticoagulate with warfarin or DOAC
  • Rate control: Beta-blockers (metoprolol, atenolol) or diltiazem. Target HR <110
  • Cardioversion: 60% convert spontaneously to sinus rhythm once euthyroid. Consider cardioversion after 3-6 months of euthyroid state if still in AFib

SPECIAL POPULATIONS:

  • Pregnancy: PTU in first trimester (methimazole teratogenic). Switch to methimazole in 2nd/3rd trimester (PTU hepatotoxicity). Target Free T4 upper normal (avoid fetal hypothyroidism). RAI contraindicated
  • Elderly: Lower doses of thionamides. High AFib risk - anticoagulate. Consider RAI as first-line
üí° TEACHING PEARLS: HYPERTHYROIDISM
  • Distinguish thyrotoxicosis from thyroid storm: Storm = LIFE-THREATENING (fever >104¬∞F, tachycardia >140, AMS, HF, GI symptoms). Non-storm = stable, symptomatic but not critical. See Condition #31 for storm management
  • RAIU scan differentiates destructive vs synthesis causes: HIGH uptake (Graves, toxic nodules) = thyroid making excess hormone ‚Üí treat with thionamides/RAI/surgery. LOW uptake (thyroiditis, exogenous) = hormone release, not synthesis ‚Üí symptomatic management only, self-limited
  • Methimazole > PTU (except first trimester pregnancy): Once-daily dosing, better efficacy, less hepatotoxicity. PTU has black box warning for hepatotoxicity
  • Agranulocytosis = RARE but serious complication of thionamides (0.1-0.5%). Presents with fever, sore throat, infection. Check CBC if symptoms. STOP thionamide immediately if ANC <1,000. Never rechallenge. Switch to RAI or surgery
  • Graves ophthalmopathy is separate from hyperthyroidism (autoimmune, TSI-mediated orbital inflammation). Treat hyperthyroidism, but eye disease may persist/worsen. RAI can worsen ophthalmopathy (give steroids if mild eye disease, avoid RAI if severe)
  • Atrial fibrillation in hyperthyroidism: 60% spontaneously convert to sinus rhythm once euthyroid. Anticoagulate based on CHA2DS2-VASc. Rate control with beta-blockers
  • Thyrotoxic periodic paralysis: Sudden muscle weakness + hypokalemia in Asian males with thyrotoxicosis. K+ shifts intracellularly (NOT total body K+ deficiency). Give K+ cautiously (can rebound to hyperkalemia). Treat underlying hyperthyroidism with beta-blockers + thionamides
  • Apathetic thyrotoxicosis in elderly: Atypical presentation (depression, weight loss, AFib, HF) WITHOUT classic hyperadrenergic symptoms. Check TSH in elderly with new AFib or unexplained weight loss
  • Iodine-containing contrast can precipitate thyrotoxicosis (Jod-Basedow) in patients with nodular thyroid disease or iodine deficiency. Screen high-risk patients before contrast administration
  • Amiodarone-induced thyrotoxicosis: Differentiate Type 1 vs Type 2. Type 1 = treat like Graves (methimazole +/- perchlorate). Type 2 = destructive thyroiditis (prednisone). Mixed type common (treat both). Discontinue amiodarone if cardiac status allows
  • RAI is contraindicated in pregnancy/breastfeeding and uncontrolled thyrotoxicosis. Achieve euthyroid state with methimazole first before RAI (reduces risk of thyroid storm post-RAI). Stop thionamide 3-5 days before RAI to allow iodine uptake
  • Monitoring thionamides: Free T4 normalizes BEFORE TSH. TSH can remain suppressed for months after T4 normalizes. Use Free T4 to guide dose adjustments (goal: normal T4). Check TSH q3 months once stable
üö® ICU ESCALATION CRITERIA
  • Progression to thyroid storm (fever >104¬∞F, HR >140, AMS, severe agitation, HF)
  • Atrial fibrillation with RVR + hemodynamic instability
  • Acute heart failure from high-output state
  • Thyrotoxic periodic paralysis with respiratory muscle weakness
  • Severe agranulocytosis from thionamides with sepsis
  • Post-thyroidectomy complications (airway hematoma, severe hypocalcemia with tetany/seizures)
‚úÖ DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Hemodynamically stable, symptoms controlled with beta-blockers
  • Thionamide therapy initiated (if indicated) + patient counseled on side effects
  • Atrial fibrillation rate-controlled + anticoagulation started if indicated
  • Endocrinology follow-up arranged within 1-2 weeks
  • Patient understands:
    • Medication adherence critical
    • Warning signs of agranulocytosis (fever, sore throat) ‚Üí stop med, seek care immediately
    • Return precautions: worsening symptoms, palpitations, dyspnea, confusion
‚ö†Ô∏è COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR
Thyroid Storm: Life-threatening decompensation. Triggered by infection, surgery, trauma, RAI, discontinuation of antithyroid drugs. See Condition #31 for management
Atrial Fibrillation/Flutter: 10-15% of hyperthyroid patients. Thromboembolic risk. Anticoagulate based on CHA2DS2-VASc. Rate control. 60% convert to sinus rhythm once euthyroid
Heart Failure (High-Output): Tachycardia-induced cardiomyopathy. Treat hyperthyroidism + beta-blockers + diuretics. Usually reversible once euthyroid
Thionamide Side Effects: (1) Agranulocytosis (0.1-0.5%): fever, sore throat ‚Üí check CBC, stop drug immediately. (2) Hepatotoxicity (PTU > methimazole): transaminitis, rare fulminant hepatic failure. (3) Rash, arthralgia (5-10%). (4) Vasculitis (rare, PTU). Monitor CBC, LFTs
Graves Ophthalmopathy: Proptosis, diplopia, vision loss (optic nerve compression). Smoking worsens. Manage with selenium, steroids, orbital decompression surgery, radiation. RAI can worsen (give steroids prophylactically)
Osteoporosis: Increased bone turnover ‚Üí bone loss. Screen with DEXA if prolonged hyperthyroidism. Treat with bisphosphonates if osteoporotic. Reversible with treatment
‚Üë Back to Top

69. HYPOTHYROIDISM (NON-MYXEDEMA)

Hashimoto ‚Ä¢ Iatrogenic ‚Ä¢ Levothyroxine Replacement
CLINICAL PRESENTATION

Note: This section covers NON-COMA hypothyroidism. For Myxedema Coma (life-threatening emergency), see Condition #32

Common Etiologies:

  • Hashimoto thyroiditis (autoimmune, 90% in US): Anti-TPO antibodies, anti-thyroglobulin antibodies. Progressive thyroid destruction. Goiter early, atrophy late
  • Iatrogenic: Post-radioiodine ablation (most common cause post-treatment), post-thyroidectomy, external beam radiation (neck, mediastinum)
  • Medications: Lithium (inhibits T4 release), amiodarone (type 2 - destructive thyroiditis), tyrosine kinase inhibitors, interferon, immune checkpoint inhibitors
  • Iodine deficiency: Most common worldwide (rare in US due to iodized salt)
  • Central hypothyroidism (secondary/tertiary): Pituitary (TSH deficiency) or hypothalamic (TRH deficiency) disease. Tumor, surgery, radiation, infiltrative (sarcoidosis, hemochromatosis), Sheehan syndrome
  • Transient: Thyroiditis (post-hyperthyroid phase), postpartum thyroiditis

Symptoms (Insidious Onset, Nonspecific):

  • Metabolic: Fatigue, cold intolerance, weight gain (modest, 5-10 lbs), decreased appetite
  • Cardiovascular: Bradycardia, diastolic hypertension, pericardial effusion
  • Neuropsychiatric: Depression, cognitive impairment ("brain fog"), psychomotor slowing, memory problems, carpal tunnel syndrome, delayed deep tendon reflex relaxation
  • GI: Constipation, decreased bowel sounds
  • Reproductive: Menorrhagia (women), decreased libido, infertility, galactorrhea (elevated prolactin from TRH)
  • Dermatologic: Dry, coarse skin, hair loss (especially lateral third of eyebrows), brittle nails, nonpitting edema (myxedema - mucopolysaccharide deposition)
  • Musculoskeletal: Myalgia, arthralgia, muscle cramps, weakness

Physical Exam:

  • Goiter (Hashimoto's - firm, rubbery) or absent thyroid (post-ablation/surgery)
  • Bradycardia, diastolic HTN
  • Dry, cool, pale skin, periorbital puffiness, macroglossia
  • Delayed relaxation of deep tendon reflexes ("hung-up" reflexes)

Subclinical Hypothyroidism: Elevated TSH (5-10 mIU/L) + NORMAL Free T4. Mild symptoms or asymptomatic. Controversial whether to treat. Consider treatment if: TSH >10, anti-TPO positive, symptomatic, goiter, pregnancy/planning pregnancy, dyslipidemia

DIAGNOSTIC WORKUP

Thyroid Function Tests:

  • TSH: Elevated (>4.5-5 mIU/L) in primary hypothyroidism. FIRST test
  • Free T4: Low (<0.8 ng/dL) confirms overt hypothyroidism
  • Free T3: Usually not needed (last to drop, nonspecific)
  • Interpretation:
    • High TSH + Low Free T4 = Primary hypothyroidism (thyroid gland failure)
    • High TSH + Normal Free T4 = Subclinical hypothyroidism
    • Low/Normal TSH + Low Free T4 = Central hypothyroidism (pituitary/hypothalamic). Order MRI pituitary, assess other pituitary hormones

Etiology Workup:

  • Anti-TPO antibodies: Positive in Hashimoto's (90-95%). Confirms autoimmune etiology
  • Thyroid ultrasound: Not routinely needed. Order if palpable nodule or goiter. Hashimoto's: heterogeneous, hypoechoic
  • If central hypothyroidism suspected: MRI pituitary, cortisol, LH/FSH, prolactin, GH (assess for hypopituitarism)

Additional Labs:

  • CBC: Normocytic or macrocytic anemia (decreased erythropoietin)
  • CMP: Hyponatremia (impaired free water excretion), hypoglycemia, elevated creatinine (decreased GFR), transaminitis (mild)
  • Lipid panel: Elevated LDL, total cholesterol, triglycerides
  • CK: Elevated (myopathy)
  • Prolactin: Mildly elevated (TRH stimulates prolactin)
  • ECG: Sinus bradycardia, low voltage, prolonged QT, flattened/inverted T waves
TREATMENT PROTOCOL

LEVOTHYROXINE (T4) REPLACEMENT:

  • Dosing:
    • Young, healthy patients: Start 1.6 mcg/kg/day PO (typical: 75-125 mcg daily). Can start full dose
    • Elderly or cardiac disease: Start LOW (25-50 mcg daily), increase by 12.5-25 mcg q4-6 weeks. Risk of precipitating MI/arrhythmia
    • Severe hypothyroidism + cardiac disease: Start 12.5-25 mcg daily, very slow titration
  • Administration: Take on empty stomach, 30-60 min before breakfast (or at bedtime, 4 hrs after last meal). Consistent timing. Avoid simultaneous: calcium, iron, PPIs, antacids, bile acid sequestrants (decrease absorption)
  • Monitoring:
    • Check TSH in 6-8 weeks after starting or dose change
    • Goal TSH: 0.5-2.5 mIU/L (normal range: 0.4-4.5, but aim for lower half)
    • Once stable: Check TSH annually
  • Dose adjustments: If TSH >4: increase levothyroxine by 12.5-25 mcg. If TSH <0.5: decrease by 12.5-25 mcg

SPECIAL POPULATIONS:

  • Pregnancy: Increase dose by 30-50% as soon as pregnancy confirmed (increased T4 requirements). Goal TSH <2.5 in 1st trimester, <3.0 in 2nd/3rd. Check TSH q4 weeks
  • Central hypothyroidism: Dose by Free T4 level (NOT TSH, which is inappropriately low/normal). Goal: Free T4 in upper half of normal range. MUST treat adrenal insufficiency FIRST (before levothyroxine) to avoid precipitating adrenal crisis
  • Cardiac disease: Start low (25 mcg), go slow (increase q4-6 weeks). Monitor for angina, arrhythmia

TREATMENT OF SUBCLINICAL HYPOTHYROIDISM:

  • Indications for treatment: TSH >10 mIU/L, anti-TPO positive (will progress to overt), symptomatic, goiter, pregnancy/planning pregnancy, dyslipidemia
  • If TSH 5-10 + asymptomatic + anti-TPO negative: Monitor TSH q6-12 months. May not need treatment
  • Dose: Start low (25-50 mcg daily), titrate to TSH 0.5-2.5

COMPLICATIONS OF OVER-TREATMENT (TSH <0.1):

  • Atrial fibrillation (especially elderly)
  • Osteoporosis (increased bone turnover)
  • Hyperthyroid symptoms (palpitations, tremor, weight loss)
  • Reduce levothyroxine dose if TSH suppressed
üí° TEACHING PEARLS: HYPOTHYROIDISM
  • Hypothyroidism symptoms are NONSPECIFIC. Fatigue, weight gain, depression common in general population. Have LOW threshold to check TSH in at-risk patients (autoimmune disease, prior thyroid treatment, neck radiation, elderly)
  • TSH is MOST sensitive test for primary hypothyroidism. Elevated TSH (>4.5-5) = thyroid dysfunction. Confirms diagnosis even before Free T4 drops (subclinical hypothyroidism)
  • Start levothyroxine LOW and GO SLOW in elderly/cardiac disease. Rapid T4 replacement can precipitate MI, arrhythmia. Start 25 mcg, increase by 12.5-25 mcg q4-6 weeks
  • Levothyroxine absorption affected by many meds/supplements. Take 30-60 min before breakfast, separate from calcium, iron, PPIs, antacids by 4 hrs. Consistent timing critical
  • Pregnancy increases levothyroxine requirements by 30-50%. Increase dose as soon as pregnancy confirmed. Check TSH q4 weeks. Goal TSH <2.5 (1st trimester), <3.0 (2nd/3rd). Uncontrolled hypothyroidism ‚Üí miscarriage, preterm birth, impaired fetal neurodevelopment
  • Central hypothyroidism: Dose by Free T4, NOT TSH. TSH inappropriately low/normal despite low T4. Goal: Free T4 in upper half of normal. TREAT ADRENAL INSUFFICIENCY FIRST (before levothyroxine) to avoid adrenal crisis
  • Hashimoto's thyroiditis: Anti-TPO antibodies positive in 90-95%. Progressive autoimmune destruction. May have transient hyperthyroid phase initially (hashitoxicosis) from thyroid inflammation/hormone release
  • Subclinical hypothyroidism: TSH 5-10 + Normal Free T4. Treat if: TSH >10, anti-TPO positive (will progress), symptomatic, pregnant. If asymptomatic + TSH 5-10 + anti-TPO negative ‚Üí monitor q6-12 months
  • Check TSH 6-8 weeks after dose change (levothyroxine half-life 7 days, takes 4-6 weeks to reach steady state). Once stable, check TSH annually
  • Over-replacement (TSH <0.1) ‚Üí Atrial fibrillation + osteoporosis. Especially in elderly. Aim for TSH 0.5-2.5, not suppressed
  • Medications that increase levothyroxine requirements: Estrogen (increases TBG), rifampin (increases metabolism), phenytoin, carbamazepine, sertraline. Check TSH 6-8 weeks after starting these meds, may need dose increase
  • Myxedema coma vs non-coma hypothyroidism: Coma = AMS, hypothermia (<95¬∞F), bradycardia, hypotension, hypoventilation, hyponatremia. MEDICAL EMERGENCY. See Condition #32. Non-coma = stable, symptomatic, can treat outpatient with levothyroxine
üö® ICU ESCALATION CRITERIA
  • Progression to myxedema coma (AMS, hypothermia <95¬∞F, hypoventilation, hypotension)
  • Pericardial effusion with tamponade physiology
  • Severe hyponatremia (<120 mEq/L) with seizures
  • Acute MI precipitated by levothyroxine initiation
  • Respiratory failure (CO2 retention from hypoventilation)
‚úÖ DISCHARGE CRITERIA
  • Levothyroxine initiated at appropriate dose
  • Patient counseled on:
    • Take on empty stomach, 30-60 min before breakfast
    • Separate from calcium, iron, antacids by ‚â•4 hrs
    • Lifelong therapy (most cases)
    • Symptoms may take 4-6 weeks to improve
  • Endocrinology or PCP follow-up in 6-8 weeks for TSH recheck
  • If cardiac disease: Close cardiology follow-up, ECG monitoring as needed
‚ö†Ô∏è COMPLICATIONS TO MONITOR
Myxedema Coma: Severe, life-threatening decompensation. Triggered by infection, cold exposure, trauma, sedatives. AMS, hypothermia, hypoventilation, hypotension. HIGH MORTALITY (20-50%). See Condition #32
Cardiovascular Complications: Pericardial effusion (resolves with treatment), diastolic dysfunction, atherosclerosis (elevated LDL), brady arrhythmias. Risk of MI/AFib if rapid levothyroxine replacement in elderly/cardiac patients
Psychiatric: Depression (treat hypothyroidism first before antidepressants), cognitive impairment ("myxedema madness"), psychosis (rare). Symptoms improve with levothyroxine
Hyponatremia: Impaired free water excretion. Dilutional hyponatremia. Treat underlying hypothyroidism + fluid restriction. Avoid rapid correction
Hyperlipidemia: Elevated LDL, total cholesterol, TG. Treat hypothyroidism first ‚Üí lipids improve in 3-6 months. May need statin if persistent
Pregnancy Complications (Untreated): Miscarriage, preterm birth, preeclampsia, placental abruption, impaired fetal neurodevelopment. Screen TSH in all pregnant women in 1st trimester (or preconception)
